A. Project Development Objective
1. Project development objective: (see Amnex 1)
Background
Unemployment in Rural Areas. Unemployment in Poland remains a key issue. Even though the
unemployment rate has fallen in the last five years from 16.4% to 12. 1%, it is unequally distributed across
regions. In addition, because of the high degree of over-employment in agriculture, unemployment in rural
areas is expected to increase in the medium to long run. In the post-reform period, agriculture has served as
a buffer accommodating excess labor in the economy, therefore creating a high share of hidden
unemployment in rural areas. In the process of Poland's accession to the EU, the farming system will need
to be reformed, and the number of people employed in agriculture will have to be reduced to make the
sector competitive with other EU countries. Unemployment in rural areas will then rise because of the
additional outflow of labor from agriculture. In addition, there is a higher proportion of youth and children
in Poland's rural areas than in cities, and young adults are surprising immobile, so issues of job creation
and relevant education are particularly critical in rural areas. The following main issues have been
identified as obstacles to the creation of off-farm jobs in rural areas.
Little Support for Private Sector Activities. Since the early 1990s, the fastest growth in durable
employment in Poland has been in Small and Medium Enterprises (SMEs) concentrated in urban areas.
SNMEs provide almost 4.8 million jobs, 83% of total registered employment in the private sector, and almost
60% of all employment in the country. These enterprises have reasonable access to bank credit, although
lack of collateral and failure to present bankable business plans often stand in the way of obtaining
commercial credit. The sector is supported by a large number of institutions and agencies for SME
promotion and development. However, the SME sector and its support structure are concentrated in urban
areas. Rural areas, so far, have not seen this small enterprise sector develop to the same extent because of
constraints to entrepreneurship, including the limited availability of banking services, the reluctance of
many rural dwellers to deal with banks and the limited collateral value of most rural properties.
Low Level of Human Capital Development. Some segments of the rural population possess
entrepreneurial spirit, but lack skills. A large number of both youth and adults are structurally unemployed
as a result of having insufficient, unmarketable, and/or outdated skills that are no longer needed in the local
or regional labor market. The current reform of education has been designed to address some of these
issues. The proposed changes in school curriculum, teaching, management and financing are designed to
make education more flexible and responsive to the demands of the workplace and the local community.
Gminas (villages and townships) have now the responsibility for managing pre-school and education for the
first nine grades, therefore they will play an important role in implementing this reform. Since most rural
gminas have limited administrative and managerial capacity in this field, they will need assistance in
implementing the reform.
Lack of Expertise in Local and Regional Administrations. Since January 1999, Poland has implemented
a comprehensive reform of the public administration by decentralizing public services and finances to two
new levels of self-government: powiats (counties) and voivodships (regions or provinces). In addition, the
government administration in the voivodship has been deeply reshaped. Most of the tasks previously
undertaken by the Voivoda (the representative of the Council of Ministers in the voivodship) have been
shifted to the new levels of self-governments. The decentralization of public services and finances to local
self-governments is placing great demands on public administrators, especially in rural areas. Councilors
- 3 -



and board members in rural areas often do not have the necessary organizational and managerial skills.
There are new functions to administer and new processes to apply. Moreover, local and regional
administrators have insufficient capacity to implement EU pre-accession adjustments, therefore affecting
the possibility for rural areas to absorb EU pre-accession and structural funds.
Need for Upgrading Infrastructure. Inadequate infrastructure is one of the most significant barriers to the
development of rural areas. The rural road network system is underdeveloped, and needs rehabilitation and
expansion. Water supply networks and facilities need to be developed, or in many cases replaced and
modernized. Proper wastewater collection (either on site, or off site with a collection network) and
treatment are lacking. Poor wastewater disposal practices are not only a threat to the environment and
health, but also to the regions' ability to attract investment, which requires at least minimally acceptable
conditions. Storage in landfills is the common method of disposing of solid waste in rural areas, but many
communities lack landfills or have facilities that do not fulfill legal, safety and sanitary requirements. The
organizational and institutional structures and the regulatory environment do not support the efficient
delivery of services, which results in unaffordable higher unit costs. As a result services either are
subsidized or remain delivered below acceptable standards.
Government's Strategy for Rural Development. The government recognizes the current and potentially
worsening problem of rural unemployment. The new objectives of its policy regarding rural regions
incorporate four main and complementary directions: i) the improvement of working and living conditions;
ii) the support for the creation of new jobs by the private sector; iii) the acceleration of the restructuring of
the farming sector; and iv) the protection of the environment. The Government puts emphasis on
coordinated efforts by policy makers (agriculture, finance, labor, education, administration and
environment protection), and consolidating various programs directed to rural areas to achieve synergy
effects in economic development. The Government realizes that the challenge to restructure and adjust
Polish agriculture to operate competitively in the European Union is closely related to multi-functional
development of rural areas.
Poland's current rural development policy prioritizes actions and measures which will effectively remove
the most severe obstacles for private investment initiatives in rural areas. These include (i) helping the
private sector to identify opportunities for investment, (ii) enhancing present labor force quality and
adjusting working population skills to both market economy and to the potential needs of private investors
in rural areas, and investing in the future labor force by improving education quality for children, (iii)
raising quality of public administration in rural areas, (iv) building of new and upgrading existing public
infrastructure, and (v) providing technical assistance for localities necessary to identify and coordinate
public investment efforts.
Agriculture Policy Framework. The government has requested the Bank's assistance in financing a Rural
Development Project (RDP), that will address the issues mentioned above. The RDP has not been designed
to be an agriculture project. The project does not aim at improving agricultural production, but will seek to
provide an answer to the problem of the outflow of labor force from agriculture. The government has
developed a strategy for agriculture and one of its main instruments to achieve efficiency is to reduce the
number of people employed in the sector. This project will therefore help the government with this objective
by creating off-farm employment.
Bank Support for Rural Development to Date. Since 1990, the Bank has had a number of operations in
various rural sub-sectors. This includes an Agriculture Sector Adjustment Loan of US$300 million
(ASAL), two agricultural credit projects of US$100 million each, a forestry project of US$146 million, and
two investment projects of DEM28 million and DEM20 million respectively, to finance the construction of
- 4 -



wholesale markets in Gdansk and Lublin. The Bank has over the past nine years developed its policy
dialogue on the basis of i) the recommendations by a joint task force comprising representatives of the
Polish Government, the European Union and the World Bank; ii) the negotiations of the ASAL; and iii) a
series of in-depth studies on agriculture (in particular as part of the preparation of the Country Economic
Memorandum of 1997), as well as on rural development which included two main efforts: one proposing a
general framework for rural development in Poland (the final report entitled "Competitiveness and
Employment" was discussed with representatives of the central and regional governments in 1998). The
second provided support to several voivodships (regions) of Northern Poland in preparing their regional
development policies (1997 and 1998), summarized in a series of reports entitled "Northern Poland
Regional Development." As a result of this long involvement with agriculture and rural institutions, the
Bank has developed a substantial knowledge of the sector, and of local government conditions in Poland.
Among the emerging issues and risks on which special attention is currently paid, the Bank is focusing on:
i) growing income disparities and inequalities which raise questions about the adequacy of the social safety
net and which could lead to the emergence of hard core poverty, especially in depressed rural areas; ii) the
ability of Poland to manage the restructuring of the agriculture sector in preparation for the transition to the
EU; and iii) improving the effectiveness of the state administration to act as a policy regulator of the
agriculture and rural areas and its capacity to comply with EU rules and procedures. Finally, a review of
the functioning of the factor and product markets in agriculture is being prepared in order to help the
Government identify institutional and policy issues in agriculture (including land market issues), and
facilitate the development of more effective instruments to promote major adjustments in the sector.
The RDP fits into the past and present strategy of the Bank, and follows the Medium-Term Strategy for
Agriculture and Rural Development, which is the continuation of the Agricultural Strategy for Poland,
elaborated in 1990 by a task force consisting of representatives of the World Bank, the European Union
and Polish experts.
Purpose of the RDP. The RDP has been designed to provide medium-term support to the development of
the rural sector in Poland. The RDP will contribute to (i) increase the level of off-farm employment in rural
areas, (ii) contribute to the on-going decentralization of self-government and regional development, and (iii)
assist Poland in building institutional capacity to absorb EU pre-accession and structural funds.
As the project is implemented, the results will be evaluated, and the lessons learned might guide the design
of subsequent projects.
2. Key performance indicators: (see Annex l)
The project will be evaluated against the following outcome indicators:
* Number ofjobs outside agriculture created in project gminas, as compared to non-project gminas
* Cost per job created
* Increase in non-agricultural income in project gminas
* Number of enterprises established
* Guidelines in place and regional assistance provided to rural gminas in analyzing, preparing and
implementing projects
Output indicators for each component are specified in Annex 1.

B. Strategic Context
1. Sector-related Country Assistance Strategy (CAS) goal supported by the project: (see Annex 1)
Document number: R97-66[IFC R97-47]                     Date of latest CAS discussion: 09/16/99
One of the CAS' four main objectives is to sustain private sector growth, by increasing private sector
participation in infrastructure development, especially in those sectors that might not find adequate
financing such as rural development and environmental investments, and strengthening human capital
growth. Bank lending activities should focus on improving the education system, including preschool and
vocational training. Another key objective of the Bank's strategy in Poland is to improve the
competitiveness of Polish agriculture and agro-industry. The Bank will support Poland in its reform agenda
for rural development, that includes the promotion of non-farm rural activities through build-up of local
governments and institutions, training and selective infrastructure investment. This project will address
each one of these objectives.
The project will follow the approach suggested in the CAS by focusing on education and the design of
sustainable programs of rural development, areas where the Bank has a comparative advantage and which
are not likely to be covered by other IFIs and donors, and by developing such programs in partnership with
central and local governments, local communities, NGOs and the EU. In particular, new possibilities for
co-financing environmental and local infrastructure projects with EU will be explored.
2. Main sector issues and Government strategy:
In 1997, a Bank discussion paper identified three main themes that should underlie the policy dialogue on
agriculture and rural development between the Bank and the Polish government: (i) competitiveness of
Polish agriculture and EU accession; (ii) rural development and rural poverty; and (iii) rural finance. A
subsequent workshop and a project identification mission followed up on this, and responded to a request
from the government to consider two main issues: alleviation of rural unemployment, and consideration of
ways and means to continue support for the program of rural infrastructure development formerly financed
with ASAL derived resources. As a result of this mission, it was agreed with the government to prepare a
rural development program that would seek to mitigate unemployment, in part through direct measures
such as training, technical assistance and financial support to entrepreneurs, and in part through continued
development of rural infrastructure to help create the necessary preconditions for sustainable economic
growth in rural areas.
The RDP will contribute to Poland's national program of rural development, described in the Poland's
Medium-Term Strategy for Agriculture and Rural Areas Development (April, 1998), and to the Bank's
strategy for rural development in Poland.
3. Sector issues to be addressed by the project and strategic choices:
The key sector issue addressed by the project is rural development, aiming at the creation of off-farm
employment. It was decided to design the project on the basis of the different approaches described below.
Multisectoral Approach. As presented above, a whole range of services from private sector support to
education are in need of improvement in rural areas. Therefore, despite the recognized risks of complexity,
a multisectoral approach is required to tackle the problem of unemployment in rural areas. For this reason,
the project has been designed to include three components dealing respectively with micro-credit
(component A), human capital development (component B) and infrastructure (component C). Only a
- 6 -



comprehensive program that tackles simultaneously the different causes of rural unemployment can provide
sustainable solutions. A sectoral approach risks to cure only one of the symptoms without improving
overall conditions. Investments in education, training, health and safety of rural families will improve the
productivity of rural people and increase their opportunities to be employed in off-farm jobs. The focus is
on off-farm employment, therefore the project will concentrate on facilitating the future transfer of
employment outside the agricultural sector.
Geographic Approach. Investments in rural infrastructure and training programs under sub-component
B-3 will be implemented in the whole country, while the other components will be implemented in selected
voivodships: Kujawsko-Pomorskie, Lubelskie, Malopolskie, Podkarpackie, Swietokrzyskie,
Warminsko-Mazurskie and Zachodniopomorskie. They were selected on the basis of four criteria:
unemployment rate, GDP per capita, education level and over-employment in agriculture. This set of
criteria was selected in cooperation with the government and Polish experts, and with the involvement of
the local communities. A short justification for choosing each criterion is given below.
1) Rate of unemployment. This criterion has been used as a proxy for the low diversification of the rural
economy, which correlates directly with lack of development. Although changes in unemployment are
lagged indicators of economic growth, it is generally recognized that areas with good economic
development exhibit low unemployment rates. Studies on Poland show that a low unemployment rate is
positively correlated with the degree of urbanization, with relatively well-developed service sectors and with
the share of the private non-agricultural sector in the local economy.
2) GDP per capita. This is another proxy for the low diversification of the economy. It provides an
objective measure of lack of development and is particularly appropriate in Poland, since evidence suggests
that GDP growth has been largest in areas with a diversified economy. It is therefore justifiable, to address
the project to poor and badly performing rural areas located in those voivodships where the GDP per capita
is low. The GDP criterion is also consistent with EU criteria.
3) Share of population with more than elementary education. An undereducated population is often a
constraining factor for private sector investment, leaving areas with poorly educated people
underdeveloped. This creates a vicious circle, since limited employment opportunities and low incomes
constrain education prospects, even for bright children. Improving educational opportunities is therefore
justified and, conversely, the education level is an appropriate criterion to judge the relative backwardness
of an area and, more important, its likely potential.
4) Employment in agriculture. In the context of Polish agriculture, this criterion can be seen as a proxy for
hidden unemployment and lack of productivity in agriculture. The much vaunted "buffer for the
unemployed" that agriculture provides is a cause of lack of diversification, and lack of economic
development. Regions with a high share of agriculture in production and employment usually have high
unemployment rates and, in addition, accommodate more hidden unemployment than regions whose
economy is not dominated by agriculture. It is therefore justifiable to use the criterion of agricultural
employment in geographic pre-selection of areas where the program will be implemented. Agricultural
(hidden) unemployment is a regional phenomenon. The share of agricultural employment in total
employment varies from 6 to 72 percent among Poland's powiats according to estimates by the main
statistical office (GUS).
Participatory Approach. A participatory approach is essential to the whole idea of regional development.
Self-governments have to be involved in the decision-making for the allocation of funds, otherwise the
support provided without involving the beneficiaries risks to be ineffective in the long run and create
-7-



dependency. Moreover, a participatory approach is now required by the on-going decentralization of
administrative functions and finances in Poland, that requires voivodship self-governments to submit
proposals for regional development to the central government.
Having this in mind, the RDP has been designed with a bottom-up approach. Requests for support from the
project have been submitted by interested gminas, powiats and voivodships. These proposals reflect
priorities incorporated in local development programs, and as such have been approved by local
self-governments. Regional Steering Committees, including representatives from all sectors of society, have
been formed with the purpose of ranking the submitted proposals. In parallel to that, social assessments
have been conducted to better reflect local needs and priorities in the project design.
The risk that the most developed rural areas will prepare proposals for co-financing while undeveloped
ones will be cut off from the support of the project has been considered. For this reason, criteria for the
selection of projects have been developed that will target resources on the unemployed, while limiting the
share of funding to certain activities, such as public works, that otherwise might absorb a disproportionate
share of resources. Moreover, matching funds requirements have been structured in a way that takes into
account the ability of the beneficiaries to contribute, in a further attempt at social equity. The Regional
Steering Committees have already ranked a preliminary set of sub-projects using a set of criteria that also
addressed social equity issues. Attention has also been given to selecting sub-projects where the use of
public funds will not be a substitute to the use of otherwise available private funds, but will be a
complement to it.
EU Partnership. The preparation of EU funded rural development initiatives requires the Ministry of
Agriculture and Rural Development (MARD) to present a plan for the absorption of SAPARD funds. The
plan has to be prepared in consultation with stakeholders, and must present financial tables for the
allocation of funds for the next seven years. As it is designed, the RDP will provide technical assistance to
people responsible for the preparation of regional development plans in the voivodship self-governments.
Parallel financing by the EU of investments in rural infrastructure and institution building in local
governments is foreseen utilizing the mechanisms of both SAPARD and EU-Phare programs.
As the RDP will be partly financed by the EU, EU criteria for parallel co-financing of sub-projects have
been taken into consideration while designing the project. The RDP will put together an institutional
mechanism which will contribute to the absorption of both EU pre-accession and post-accession structural
funds.
C. Project Description Summary
1. Project components (see Annex 2 for a detailed description and Annex 3 for a detailed cost
breakdown):
The project is designed so that individual elements from any component can be implemented at the local
level, singly or in combination with others. For example, a gmina may identify the need for a waste-water
treatment investment, training for several unemployed in business management, and a credit to help an
entrepreneur start a small business. A package of services and investments will be developed to meet these
needs drawing on resources across all project components. Such packages will form part of the broader
investment program submitted by gminas to the regional authorities for co-financing.
The following are the components and sub-components financed under the project:
Component A: Microcredit (US$19.05 million). This component is centered on the development of
-8-



micro-enterprises. Its objective is to reduce hidden and registered unemployment in rural areas, while also
allowing an empirical comparison of the micro-credit approach with the cost of the current regime of social
assistance (welfare). In particular, the component will promote off-farm self-employment in rural gminas of
five voivodships. To achieve its objective, the component will finance (a) small, one time only grants to
cover the minimum social insurance premium that beneficiaries will have to pay if they decide to leave their
status of unemployed. This will allow for a wider participation to the micro-credit program; (b) micro-loans
up to US$5,000 to new and existing rural micro-enterprises; (c) advisory services for newly established
micro-enterprises created under the component; and (d) operational cost and technical assistance on
micro-lending to non-governmental organizations or financial institutions which will be selected to assist in
the implementation of the component as service providers. The service providers will enter into a legal
agreement with the National Economy Bank (BGK). This agreement will cover the terms and conditions for
the operations of the micro-credit programs including risk sharing, and the conditions for technical
assistance and training. See Annex 2, para. 3 to 56 for component details.
Component B: Human Capital Development
Sub-component B- 1: Labor Redeployment (US$44.84 million). The objective of the sub-component is to
assist economically disadvantaged in rural communities to expand and use their human capital in response
to economic and labor market opportunities. The sub-component will finance two main tasks: (i) economic
and labor market surveys; and (ii) labor redeployment services, such as specialized employment services,
on-job and institutional training, support to temporary community employment, small business assistance
services, small business incubators, promotion activities and local economic development planning grants.
The menu of labor redeployment will be made available to economically disadvantaged and unemployed
citizens in rural communities, through local service providers which will be selected on a competitive basis.
See Annex 2, para. 57 to 82 for sub-component details.
Sub-component B-2: Education (US$24.67 million). A key component in the effort to attract new
businesses in rural areas is the implementation of a comprehensive program for the development of a strong
education and training system, capable of providing graduates with the level of knowledge, skills and
capacity to learn required by a modern market economy. The overall objective of the education
sub-component is to improve the efficiency of the education system and the quality of graduates in target
gminas by enhancing the quality of teaching in rural primary schools and gymnasia, and improving the
utilization of educational resources. The education sub-component will finance five major activities: (i) a
program for improving the quality of teaching in primary schools and gymnasia in target areas through
training; (ii) a program for improving the quality of school equipment through the provision of instructional
material; (iii) a program for supporting the introduction of computers for instructional purposes in rural
gminas; (iv) a program for improving school facilities, and education efficiency, through activities leading
to the consolidation of the school network; and (v) support to project dissemination, implementation and
monitoring. The project will be implemented in a sub-set of gminas covered by the project based on an
assessment of the current situation of their education system. Gminas will select from the above menu a
combination of investments which addresses the main problems and challenges identified during the
assessment. See Annex 2, para. 83 to 107 for sub-component details.
Sub-component B-3: Institution Building in Local and Regional Administrations (US$12.62 million). The
overall objective of this sub-component is to increase the level of efficiency and effectiveness in eligible
units of local and regional administration (gminas, powiats and voivodships) within the voivodships
covered by RDP, through institutional development, training programs, and capacity building measures,
thus improving the conditions for the creation of non-farm employment in rural areas. An efficient public
administration will improve the provision of goods and services to the public and the private sectors, thus
-9-



facilitating economic activities. The project will finance: (i) institutional development programs (IDP) in
selected pilot units to develop a framework for improving the general efficiency of regional and local
administrations, including management of human and financial resources; (ii) training programs to improve
the quality of services delivered by local officials and public servants, and build capacity for implementing
EU rules and regulations; and (iii) and a capacity building program aimed at supporting the Ministry of
Interior and Public Administration in linking IDP and training activities, and at developing surveys and
studies. Training will be provided to selected public officials, i.e. elected councilors and board members,
and employees of different levels of self-government administration. The training will place special
emphasis on public management and administrative services important for the successful implementation of
RDP and its project components. See Annex 2, para. 108 to 140 for sub-component details.
Component C: Rural Infrastructure (US$194.89 million). The development and improvement of
infrastructure is regarded as a prerequisite for stimulating private sector development. Areas with
developed infrastructure attract investors, whereas areas with inadequate infrastructure place a burden of
additional costs on potential investors, who may need to build roads, water supply, waste water collection,
access to district heating, telecommunication, etc., in order to start and operate a new business. The
objective of this component is to improve technical infrastructure in rural areas, in order to stimulate
private sector investments, facilitate the creation of non-farm jobs, improve environmental and health
conditions, introduce alternative organizational and institutional structures for the sustainable delivery of
affordable services, introduce market oriented approaches in the development and operation of
infrastructure networks, and finally narrow the gap between rural and urban areas in economic
development. The project will finance water supply systems, sewage collection and treatment facilities,
solid waste management systems and rural roads. The limitation to investments in the water, waste water,
roads and solid waste sectors is based on the low demand expressed by local governments for
telecommunication and energy projects. High priority sub-projects have been already identified by the local
governments in seven voivodships. Proposals for investments in rural telecommunication were only 0.8
percent of the total investment portfolio, and eligible energy projects were only 1.3 percent. The average
size of a rural infrastructure investment financed under the RDP is expected to be US$460,000. The
sub-projects will be further screened and evaluated during implementation according to technical, financial
and institutional requirements. A comprehensive training and technical assistance program has been
developed to provide technical staff, project staff in the voivodship and local government officials, with the
skills necessary to implement the project. See Annex 2, para. 141 to 170 for component details.
Component D: Project Management (US$3.77 million). The project will also finance the costs of a Project
Coordination Unit (equipment, staff and O&M), and a provision is made for the costs incurred by the
project teams in the line ministries and the Voivodship Project Implementation Units.

2. Key policy and institutional reforms supported by the project:
Decentralization. The project will work within and support the recent changes in policy and institutional
responsibilities that are resulting from the fundamental restructuring and decentralization of governmental
responsibilities to regional and local authorities. The project is specifically intended to support the new
policy framework of local responsibilities, and to assist new and existing institutions in the formative stages
of their expanded obligations. It is particularly important to strengthen those institutions that will be
charged with disbursing EU structural funds for rural development, upon accession. The project will assist
local and regional government institutions in developing the capacity to implement programs financed
through the EU structural funds.
Comprehensive Rural Development. The project will support the issues identified in the Medium-Term
Strategy for Agriculture and Rural Areas Development, designed to create greater opportunities for
off-farm employment for those currently un- or under-employed, and in anticipation of the expected shift of
part of the current farm work force to non-farm work. The project will, inter alia, assist in improving living
and working conditions in rural areas. Specifically, the project will contribute to the creation of the
following physical structures, institutions and services identified in the Medium Term Strategy:
- 11 -



Poland's Medium-Term Strategy for Aericulture and   Rural Development Program
Rural Areas Development
a) Support for the creation of new non-agricultural  Rural Development Program (in

b) Development of economic advisory services,     Component B- I - Labor
particularly those related to taxes, organization and  Redeployment (Business Support

c) Comprehensive reform of the schooling system.  Component B - Human Capital
Development
(Education sub-component to
address school modernization and
reform, education quality and
efficiency and teacher training)
d) Support to rural infrastructure as the basic condition    Component C - Infrastructure
for stimulating economic activity (top priority)
Through the education component, the RDP supports the education reform which is the fourth biggest
reform initiated by the government, after the reforms of the administration, health, and pension system. The
first phase of the reform extends compulsory education from eight to nine years, and splits it into a cycle of
six years of primary school and three years of gymnasium. The reform enhances changes in the school
network to improve efficiency of the system, and aims at improving quality of education through changes in
curricula. The education component activities support the first phase of the education reform in the rural
gminas in the targeted areas, which will probably have more difficulties with the introduction of the reform
requirements as compared to other more affluent communities.
3. Benefits and target population:
Activities to be financed under the proposed project will (i) create new places of employment in rural areas,
(ii) improve the operational environment for economic activities in rural areas, (iii) offer broad based
support for the creation of new activities, (iv) generate employment and improve rural living conditions.
The target population is expected to be the 40% of Poland's rural population, whose conditions may
aggravate as labor will have to shift away from agriculture in the coming years.
4. Institutional and implementation arrangements:
The implementation arrangements for the RDP are designed to ensure the efficient and transparent
achievement of the project objectives. With some exceptions pointed out in the description of the tasks, the
project will have two main levels of decision making: (i) gminas and regional self-governments will have
the programming and implementation function; (ii) the central government will have a coordination and
support function.
Bottom-up Approach. Gminas have already begun to pick from a menu of options the investment
- 12-



activities that are essential for addressing the objectives as they are reflected in their local development
strategies. The RDP operational manual provides the guidelines and the procedures to help gminas take
rational and financially sound decisions. In addition, the sub-component on Institution Building in Local
and Regional Administrations will provide assistance to gmina, powiat and voivodship self-governments in
rationalizing their decision-making process. The RDP will therefore lead to a better resource utilization at
the local and regional level, increasing the overall economic efficiency.
Economies of Scale. Once a portfolio of sub-projects is identified and approved by the gmina, it is
reviewed by the powiat to ensure the harmonization of sub-regional investments. The RDP will enhance
economies of scale by giving preference to gminas that jointly decide to develop one landfill, instead of
having a landfill built in each one of them; sub-regional schools, instead of a costly system where there is a
primary school in nearly every village; or common water supply and waste water treatment facilities,
instead of scattered expensive investments that will have a limited positive environmental impact.
ARMA. In the case of the infrastructure component, all project proposals applying for co-financing under
the project will be submitted to the relevant local office of ARMA for review. The local office of ARMA
will have three weeks to: (i) check proposals against eligibility criteria for co-financing under the project,
and (ii) perform the financial and economic analysis of proposals. ARMA has no approval power, and after
evaluating the proposals, the local offices of ARMA will send the documentation to the Voivodship Project
Implementation Unit/Regional Steering Committee with copy to the applicant. To ensure the efficient
evaluation of proposals, by the end of March 2000, the local offices of ARMA will need to have the
capacity to assess the economic, financial, environmental and technical quality of projects, and a financial
management system satisfactory to the Bank.
Voivodship Project Implementation Unit Once projects' proposals are sent for review to the Voivodship
Project Implementation Unit (VPIU), they are screened and checked for compliance with regional
development policies and against criteria, procedures and standards as defined in the operational manual
and required by local law. For this task, the project will use the expertise already in place in the Regional
Development Agencies (RDA) or other institutions. The VPIU will consist of at least a project director, an
assistant to the director who will work as secretary of the Regional Steering Committee, a chief accountant,
a financial management specialist, an MIS specialist, three procurement specialists and rural infrastructure
specialists, and a part-time legal advisor. The professional staff will be supported by support staff.
Regional Steering Committee. A Regional Steering Committee (RSC), consisting of representatives of the
voivodship, powiat and gmina self-governments, the voivoda office, the chamber of commerce and/or
industry and trade, the chamber of agriculture, the curator and NGOs, will rank eligible sub-projects which
are submitted by local governments and reviewed by the VPIU concerning technical, financial, economic,
environmental and institutional requirements. The RSCs have begun to prioritize sub-projects following a
set of agreed criteria. For the infrastructure component - as an example - the criteria include: i) importance
for the broader regional economic development plan; (ii) potential for direct and indirect job creation; (iii)
proposal jointly presented by a group of gminas which reflects the principle of economy of scale in project
design; (iv) contribution of local government to investment cost. Similar criteria were developed for other
sub-components. The package of investment projects as ranked by the Regional Steering Committee will
form the basis for the contract that the Marszaleks will negotiate with the central government for the
allocation of funds among voivodships. Based on the funds available for each voivodship, the RSC will
approve the individual sub-projects in the order of the ranking list. Detailed rules and procedures can be
found in the Operational Manual.
- 13-



Central Government. In Poland, MARD has the overall responsibility for rural development, however the
following ministries and agencies, responsible for specific aspects of rural development, will be involved in
providing assistance to self-governments in implementing the project. MARD will coordinate all the issues
related to the implementation of the RDP and component C. The other ministries involved in the project are
the Ministry of Finance (MOF), the Ministry of Economy (MOE), the Ministry of Labor and Social Policy
(MOLSP), responsible for the implementation of components A and B-1, the Ministry of National
Education (MONE), responsible for the implementation of component B-2, and the Ministry of Interior and
Public Administration (MIAPA), responsible for the implementation of component B-3.
National Steering Committee. A National Steering Committee, composed of the representatives of the
ministries mentioned above and two representatives of the Board of Marszaleks appointed by the Joint
Commission of Government and Self-governments, will allocate project funds among voivodships, oversee
project implementation, review annual audits, resolve any problems arising in the coordination of the
different activities, and approve changes to the operational manual. The Minister of Agriculture and Rural
Development, as Chairman of the National Steering Committee, will invite the representatives of the
Ministry of Environment and of the Committee for European Integration, the Chairmen of the Rural
Association of Gminas and Association of Powiats to participate in the work of the committee. Other
Marszaleks will participate in the working sessions of the NSC. The National Steering Committee will meet
at least every three months. By making the Marszaleks members of the National Steering Committee, the
RDP strengthens the cooperation between local and central governments, and creates a forum for
discussing the process of allocating limited funds among the different regions of the country. The RDP
establishes procedures and criteria that will enable consensus building between local, regional and central
layers of the government, therefore supporting the decentralization process. In addition, the procedures for
identifying priorities and allocating funds will allow the better usage of local and EU pre and
post-accession funds.
Funding Agreements. The contract between each Marszalek and the Minister of Agriculture and Rural
Development, as chairman of the National Steering Committee will constitute the basis for the allocation of
funds by voivodship. The allocation of funds will be made in consideration of economic and social equity
issues, and will be made for investments under the active labor programs, the education and infrastructure
components. In the case of the microcredit activities, pilot voivodships have been pre-identified, and this
will be reflected in the allocation of funds. The institution building activities will be managed at central
level by MIAPA, and the allocation of funds will take this into account.
Financing of Sub-projects. Sub-projects to be supported under this operation will be financed from
different sources: gmina and powiat budgets, EU funds, Bank funds, environmental protection funds,
EcoFund, commercial banks and private sector. With respect to commitment of EU-Phare funds, a lead
ministry is designated by the government as responsible for each program, its management and resource
allocation. It is expected that the respective ministry will annually arrange for a decision on the amount
allocated for rural development to be executed at the regional level, and delegate the detailed allocation by
individual sub-projects to the National Steering Committee. With respect to national funds, e.g. the
National Fund for Environmental Protection and Water Management and the Labor Fund, it will be the
responsibility of the gmina to obtain such funding approvals for the sub-projects proposed to the Regional
Steering Committee.
Flow of Funds. The World Bank will make an initial authorized allocation into the Special Account
registered with the Ministry of Finance in the National Bank of Poland, and thereafter transfers will be
made upon requests received from the MOF. Funds will flow from the Special Account to the Financial
Departments of the Voivoda Offices (with expenditures justified to the Bank within a thirty days period) in
- 14 -



the case of infrastructure component. Should the Public Finance Law be amended, then the funds will flow
according to that amendment. Based on the approval of sub-project packages by the National Steering
Committee and the funding agreements signed by each Marszalek (see para. 48), the Ministry of Finance
will transfer funds according to government budget procedures and terms agreed for each component as
presented in Annex 2. The advance for each sub-project package will cover no more than 20% of the
overall approved amount. The VPIU will then prepare co-financing agreements to be signed between the
Marszaleks and the gminas. Once these agreements are signed, the gminas will start the implementation
process with the assistance of the VPIU. In the case of the institution building activities and the
establishment of a resource center under the Education component, the responsible ministries will directly
pay the service providers. In the case of component A, Microcredit, payments will be channeled via the
BGK through selected service providers.
The Ministry of Finance will transfer a portion of the overall approved amount as an advance (no more
than 20%), and subsequently on the basis of replenishment requests from the VPIUs. Once 50% of the
advance has been used, the VPIUs will submit a replenishment request to the PCU that will forward the
necessary documentation to the MOF, that will then submit replenishment requests of the Special Account
to the Bank. Provisions will be made for funds to be returned in the event of cost-savings or ineligibility of
expenditures.
By keeping the programming and implementing function separate from the paying function, the process for
decision-making under the RDP is subject to public control and allows for a transparent allocation of
funds. It therefore allows for better governance and introduces a clear division of responsibilities between
local, regional and national governments.
Project Coordination Unit. The National Steering Committee will be supported by a Project Coordination
Unit located within the Foundation for Assistance Programs for Agriculture (FAPA), an independent
agency reporting to the Minister of Agriculture and Rural Development that has demonstrated its capacity
in implementing similar projects. The Project Coordination Unit will have the responsibility for the overall
coordination of the project, and fulfill the role of secretariat of the National Steering Committee. The
Project Coordination Unit will receive periodic progress reports from VPIUs and project teams in the
responsible ministries, and will prepare consolidated reports for the Government, the Bank, and other
co-financing organizations as appropriate. The Project Coordination Unit will be responsible for the project
launch workshop, and for providing assistance to all levels of project management. It will consist of
eighteen staff members headed by a project director, and will include a procurement specialist, a financial
management specialist and a MIS specialist (see annex I of the operational manual).
Implementation Agreement. The Minister of Agriculture and Rural Development, as Chairman of the
National Steering Committee, will sign the Implementation Agreement with the Minister responsible for
each sub-component (see para. 46). The Implementation Agreement will define the responsibilities of each
ministry and the reporting procedures.
Monitoring Function. Each ministry participating in the project will have a monitoring function for its line
of responsibility. To carry out that function, each ministry will designate a department, or an external
service provider, to carry out these functions. These project teams will cooperate at the central level with
the Project Coordination Unit, and provide information on the implementation in line with the project
indicators.
Project progress reports will be submitted by all lower level implementing units to the Voivodship Project
Implementing Units or, in the case of institution building activities, service providers reports will be
- 15-



submitted through project teams in appropriate ministries to the PCU. The Project Coordination Unit will
prepare periodic consolidated financial management reports for the National Steering Committee, the
Ministry of Finance, and the Bank. These reports will be part of a project Management Information
System, providing reports on the achievement of objectives compared to funds spent. A periodic RDP
evaluation process (initially every six months), at both the regional and central levels, is built into the
project implementation schedule for continuous process improvements.

D. Project Rationale
1. Project alternatives considered and reasons for rejection:
The project could have been designed as a simple continuation of the past program of support for the
creation of rural infrastructure (Agriculture Sector Adjustment Loan, ASAL300, 1994-1997). This option
was rejected because of the need to develop an integrated approach that would extend the scope of the
project to support rural entrepreneurship and changes in the education of the rural labor force.
Full reliance upon commercial financing has been rejected because of the so far limited availability of
appropriate instruments such as municipal bonds and the concurrent limitations for financing public works,
particularly in rural areas. Moreover, a hands-off approach by central government would not have allowed
for targeting the most distressed rural areas, for instance east of the Vistula river, and would have made it
difficult, if not impossible, to leverage the structural support from the EU effectively.
2. Major related projects financed by the Bank and/or other development agencies (completed,
ongoing and planned).

3. Lessons learned and reflected in the project design:
Agriculture Sector Adjustment Loan. While designing the RDP, an analysis of the effects of the ASAL
(Agricultural Structure Adjustment Loan) was undertaken. One of the principal objectives of the
Government of Poland in utilizing the proceeds of the ASAL was to stimulate rural areas through the
development of infrastructure (water supply, sewage collection and telecommunication projects) for which
USS250 million were allocated. The planning, implementation and fund disbursement functions were
entrusted to ARMA (Agency for the Reconstruction and Modernization of Agriculture), which distributed
grants for approximately US$222 million. These funds were distributed to gminas in the form of subsidies,
complementing other, for example NGOs grants, preferential credits, commercial financing, which was
usually for 60% or more of total investment in the sub-project concerned. The lessons learned from the
ASAL are the following: (i) construction of infrastructure stimulates the economy of rural areas. ASAL
investments contributed to the generation of jobs directly through the construction and operation of the
infrastructure projects, and indirectly through the development of the local economy; (ii) stimulation of
rural areas through infrastructure projects should be based on strategic plans of regional economic
development. It is necessary to supplement the extension of infrastructure with other elements like
education, stimulation of small businesses (supplying them with information, legal assistance, joint
promotion, business incubators), adaptation of local regulations (land use planning), and involvement of
private business in the development of the area (privatization, joint public and private projects); (iii)
infrastructure projects should be located in areas with relatively high concentration of population.
Bank-financed Rural Development Projects. In addition, the Bank's experience with rural development
projects has generated important lessons that were considered while preparing the project. These include: (i)
the support program should be well known to the beneficiaries; (ii) the implementing agency should be
viewed as a tool of the program and not the 'donor'; (iii) clear rules for qualifying investment projects for
financial support should be in place; (iv) information availability, clarity of criteria should be supplemented
by necessary technical assistance; (v) thorough evaluation of the effects of program implementation is
essential; (vi) the project design should emphasize that a decentralized multi-component program is more
effective than separate projects for infrastructure investment, training, education, and private sector
development.
Similar Activities in Poland. Most of, if not all, the activities proposed for the RDP are not new to
Poland. On the contrary, they have been tried and tested in the Polish developing market economy since the
early nineties. Some of them have been part of previous Bank supported projects, others have been
successfully undertaken by other institutions and by the Polish government. For instance, the support
activities proposed for SMEs are essentially the extension to rural areas of what has been pioneered very
successfully by Polish institutions, initially with support from the EU and from several bilateral
organizations, notably the UK Know How Fund. In the same vein, the entire Labor Redeployment
component is based on the recently concluded, urban based, Bank supported Active Labor Program that
was implemented by the Ministry of Labor and Social Policy, the same entity that will be looked at for
overall coordination, under the RDP, of what is essentially the extension of that program to rural areas.
This same ministry has extensive experience in operating unemployment related programs from the Polish
government, including in rural areas and including microcredit schemes. What is arguably the largest
precursor of what is proposed here was the 250 million dollar equivalent program of rural infrastructure
development financed in conjunction with the Bank's ASAL in the early '90s. Under that program, about
4,200 gmina level infrastructure projects were built. Finally, the demand driven approach of the RDP is
responsive to the spontaneous initiatives observed in several rural locations in Poland, where, for instance,
the local population has formed cooperatives to create their own telephone network, or where several
- 19-



communities have combined to build sewage treatment plants and, lately, solid waste disposal sites. These
experiences have largely guided the design of the RDP with respect to the targeting of beneficiaries,
potentially effective implementation modalities, and identification and selection of investments.
Bilateral Donor Coordination. The British Know How Fund (KHF), with its Public Investment Program
(PIP), and the United States Agency for International Development (USAID), with its Local Government
Partnership Program (LGPP), have demonstrated a strong involvement in Poland for strengthening the
capacity of local governments. The Bank has established a close cooperation with both organizations. As a
result, the KHF has supported the RDP with the development of models for the economic and financial
analysis of rural infrastructure investments. In addition, USAID, KHF and LGPP participated in designing
the B-3 component. Furthermore, the USAID, KHF and the Bank team have developed the training and TA
elements for the rural infrastructure component in close coordination and cooperation with the ongoing PIP
and LGPP programs.
4. Indications of borrower commitment and ownership:
The Government has emphasized the need to prepare and implement the program on an expedited schedule
in order to maximize its coordination with EU programs, and has worked actively to prepare the project.
The National Steering Committee, project teams at the ministerial level and Regional Steering Committees
have been created. Sub-projects under the infrastructure and education components have already been
identified by the beneficiaries, and Marszaleks have financed local consultants to help identify them.
5. Value added of Bank support in this project:
Bank support will allow for the coordination of otherwise disparate efforts, incorporating other
international assistance for the creation of rural infrastructure, the promotion of enterprises in rural areas
and the generation of employment. Bank participation in the project will assist the government in designing
a framework for efficient use of EU pre-accession and post-accession funds for rural development. Bank
funding will supply the missing long term financing, without which many projects for the creation of public
goods cannot be realized. Bank initiative might allow for the development of a new system that could serve
as the financing vehicle for all future Polish regional and rural development programs, both internationally
and domestically funded. In doing this, it will provide a mechanism by which Polish democracy will make
its influence felt on development at all government levels, in many relevant sectors.
E. Summary Project Analysis (Detailed assessments are in the project file, see Annex 8)
1. Economic (see Annex 4):
O Cost benefit    NPV=[]S$ million; ERR =- % (see Annex 4)
O Cost effectiveness
* Other (specify)
Given the multisectoral nature of the project, a range of benefits is expected, many of which are not
quantifiable or additive. The project thus does not lend itself as a whole to a detailed ex-ante cost-benefit
analysis and a rate of return calculation. The project encourages decentralized decision-making based
largely on the priorities of local communities. Such demand-driven investments are expected to provide
greater ownership and utility to the community. Active involvement and participation of the community is
expected to improve the cost-effectiveness of the investment options. To help the communities, the project
- 20 -



documents provide a framework of selection parameters for the menu of components based on the lessons
of previous operations (ASAL) or extensive studies. Based on the ASAL experience, the investment of the
RDP could lead to incremental generation of about 44,000 permanent jobs at a cost of around US$5,000
(PLN 20,000) per job, remarkably low by world standards.
Micro-credit: The essence of the economic and financial analysis of this component is a comparison
between (a) the average cost of providing social assistance to one unemployed person under the current
policy regime, taking into account the expected duration of an unemployment spell, and (b) the estimated
average cost of the alternative intervention implicit in the design of the sub-component; namely a "package"
of grants, technical assistance, and micro-loans to create new and promote existing micro-enterprises in
rural areas. Annex 4 summarizes data that suggest that, under rather conservative assumptions, the
package of investments and activities financed under the sub-component may be a lower cost alternative to
the instruments currently used by the government to deal with long term rural unemployment. The main
findings presented in annex 4 are (a) a survival rate of micro-enterprises created under the component of
above 59 percent after two years will make the proposed package more cost effective than the current
regime, and (b) the main difficulty faced by a micro-credit outlet in achieving sustainability is the low
incidence of entrepreneurship in the country's rural areas - which implies that achieving sustainability in
rural Poland may require more time than in other environments (see Annex 4 for assumptions and risk
analysis).
Labor Redeployment: The basic economic rationale is that moving workers, via labor redeployment
programs, from jobs where they are not productive and receive public subsidies to jobs where they are
productive and do not receive public subsidies reduces public budget outlays and increases GDP. At the
same time, GDP will increase to the extent that the jobs filled by redeployed workers are new jobs or higher
productivity jobs than participants held previously. The sub-component finances specific services to: (a)
improve labor mobility and address unemployment; (b) enhance and change human capital, via retraining,
to address structural unemployment and improve productivity; and (c) address the lack of demandfor
labor including support to individuals to help them start and maintain micro and small businesses, and to
communities to implement temporary community employment schemes and design local economic
development plans. Since ex-ante cost benefit analysis is not possible the sub-component will be monitored
for cost-effectiveness. The project provides for monitoring of the status of program participants relative to
their post-program employment status, earnings, and receipt of continued public income support payments.
It is anticipated that the gross impact of the services will equal or exceed those found in the study "
Evaluating the Impact of Active Labor Programs: Results of Cross Country Studies in Europe and Central
Asia" (World Bank Discussion Paper, June, 1999), and that post program employment will be a minimum
of 15% after receipt of employment services, 50% for participants in retraining, 10% of employment in
temporary community employment programs, 5% of those who make initial contact for assistance in
starting small businesses will actually start businesses, and 90% of those entering business incubators will
continue to operate a small business at the end of one year.
Education: Rehabilitation of school infrastructure and development of a school transportation system in
rural gminas will promote the consolidation of the current school network leading, on the one hand, to
increased efficiency in the utilization of resources by increasing student-teacher and student-classroom
ratios. On the other hand, it will bring savings into the system by evolving a school network that is more
energy efficient and is better utilized. Improvement in the quality of education will reduce repetition and
dropout ratios. As a consequence of this more students will graduate and it will take less time and resources
to produce a graduate, thus reducing the average cost of producing a graduate. On the other hand, quality
improvement will produce graduates with better and more relevant knowledge and skills, which in turn will
increase their potential to be employed, productive and trainable. Selection of education sub-projects will
- 21 -



be based on measures of cost-effectiveness. Table I below shows the cost-effectiveness of the main
sub-components of the RDP investments under education. It is based on pilot programs undertaken by the
Ministry of Education in 1998 and 1999.

Implementation
The project provides for monitoring of the status of teachers relative to their post-program employment
status, performance standards and receipt of continued public income support payments, if they are
currently unemployed. It is anticipated that the gross impact of the services will equal or exceed those
found in the pilot programs of the Ministry of Education, and will result in greater cost savings than
without project practice. Independent evaluators are evaluating the pilot project. Based on the latest
information, the teachers trained by the program have expressed their satisfaction with the contents and
method of training. The pilot training program has raised the level of teachers' qualifications thus training
them to teach new school courses and remain in the profession by qualifying them for the future
accreditation system to be implemented.
Institution Building in Local and Regional Administrations: The proposed sub-component will provide a
number of benefits. The project will assist in output recovery and sustained economic growth through
policy actions that promote among other things private investments. It will increase the levels of efficiency
- 22 -



and effectiveness of public services by decentralizing decision making, implementation, and monitoring of
programs and investments. Data were lacking for empirical cost-benefit analysis. Instead, a cost-recovery
calculation analysis was prepared, in which the efficiency gains have been expressed as percentages of the
1999 annual budgets of voivodship (annual budget USD 40 million in the Kujawsko-Pomorskie
voivodship), powiat (annual budget USD 7 million in the Kujawsko-Pomorskie voivodship) and gmina
(annual budget USD 1.5 million, countrywide average). It was assumed efficiency gains would amount to
2% of the budget including the participants in the general training program. Full development was expected
in year seven. Over a period of analysis of 15 years, the Internal Rate of Return (IRR) amounts to about
39%, and the Net Present Value (NPV) at 12% discount rate is about USD 19 million. A sensitivity
analysis was performed for the number of participating units, which showed that with one participating
voivodship, two participating powiats and seven participating gminas, and reaching out with general
training to 100 units only, the lRR would be about 13% and the NPV USD 0.5 million.
Infrastructure: Quality Review for investment proposals will be carried out by the local offices of ARMA
against technical, economic, financial and institutional requirements. Clear rules for qualifying investment
projects include a financial and economic justification based on models prepared by Agrisystems Inc. and
financed by the British Know How Fund. Investments should only be approved if they generate a nominal
EIRR higher than 10 percent (see Table 2 below). These models for the financial and economic analysis of
small infrastructure investments are incorporated in the operational manual. In addition, consultants will
provide hands-on training for the VPIUs, local offices of ARMA and gminas during implementation. As an
example, the model for rural roads is attached in Annex 4, Appendix 2-1. Models for other infrastructure
investments are included in the operational manual.
Table 2. Selected thresholds for the Selection of Rural Infrastructure Sub-projects
Thresholds
Local government contribution                                  30% and 50%
Nominal EIRR                                                   10%
Cost Recovery of O&M costs for revenue generating services     Full
The RSC has ranked the projects following a set of criteria: (i) importance for the broader regional
economic development plan; (ii) potential for direct and indirect job creation; (iii) joint proposals among
gminas which reflects the principle of economy of scale in project design; (iv) contributions of local
government to investment cost; and (v) economic rate of return.
2. Financial (see Annex 5):
NPV=US$ million; FRR= % (see Annex 4)
In several components, financial criteria and analyses will serve as the basis, and in a few even as a proxy,
for economic analysis (see above).
Fiscal Impact:
Fiscal Impact: Fiscal costs of unemployment were analyzed to determine the range of benefits arising from
employment generation. The analysis was performed per person moving from unemployed to employed,
- 23 -



using the 1997 data of the Central Statistical Office.
Budget cost of unemployment, as in Table 3 consists of three major elements: (i) unemployment benefit, (ii)
contribution to health insurance schemes, and (iii) administrative costs of managing the unemployment
scheme. Annual cost of unemployment benefit per one unemployed reached about PLN 2,705.
Table 3. Derivation of Annual fiscal cost per unemployed person (based on 1997 data)

Unemployment benefits (total Poland)            3,300,838
Insurance of unemployed (total Poland, govt.    1,463,176
contribution)
Administrative costs (total Poland)             176,808
Total budget expenditure (total Poland)         4,940,822
Number of unemployed                            1,826,000
Annual fiscal cost per unemployed in PLN        2,705.22
This analysis indicates that annual fiscal savings from converting one unemployed to employed will be
about PLN 2,700 (US$825). Employment of one previously unemployed person will also generate
additional fiscal benefits to the budget in the form of income tax and higher revenue from the Value Added
Tax. Estimation of these benefits was performed under two assumptions (i) minimum salary; and (ii)
average salary (see Table 4).

salary in PLN)

5,121.00            12,789.12
