Summing up the income taxes and higher VAT revenues gives a range between PLN 1,050 and 3,300 per
year from creation of one job for an unemployed person. Finally, total fiscal benefits were calculated by
adding budget costs foregone and new revenues. The range is between PLN 3,800 and 6,000 per year.
The above estimates are in 1997 PLN, as were the estimates of the impact of ASAL (see
E. Summary Project Analysis 1. Economic, page 22). On that basis and assuming all
project costs are borne by the government, such costs should be recouped fiscally in about
four years.
3. Technical:
Poland has well developed technical standards in all of the infrastructure sub-components, that are now
being updated to comply with the latest EU requirements. Although technical standards exist and are
enforced, their application in specific situations may not take into account engineering economy
considerations and may result in over designed facilities and waste of resources.
Parallel to the use of these standards, better procedures which would yield least cost solutions would
improve the efficiency of service delivery. They would also help achieve compliance with broad
environmental standards, since low cost provision of sewer and solid waste services encourages increased
subscription. There is already evidence in Poland of consumers revolting against high cost services (usually
based on over designed facilities) by withdrawing their subscription. The project appropriately addresses
this issue by providing detailed guidelines and recommendations through the operational manual, and
assisting the VPIU and local governments with extensive training and technical assistance. The emphasis in
sub-project selection on economies of scale in infrastructure (see Economies of Scale, page 13) and
consolidation in rural schooling (see Institutional Buildings in Local and Regional Administration, page 24)
should also raise economic efficiency in those fields.
4. Institutional:
4.1 Executing agencies:
Gminas. The gmina is the basic level of self-government. Since 1990, there are almost 2,500 gminas in
Poland, headed by a Wojt, Chairman of the Board, in the case of rural gminas, and by a Mayor (Chairman
of the Board), in the case of urban gminas. As a result of the decentralization process, gminas have been
strengthened, and they have been provided with the tools and the means to independently structure their
social and economic development, while putting in place safeguards to prevent over spending and
increasing debt exposure. A large city is headed by a president. The Gmina performs all public tasks of
- 25 -



local significance that have not been reserved by law for other entities.
The decision to recommend and design a bottom-up approach with very strong involvement of local
self-governments is based on an institutional assessment carried out as part of the project's preparation
process, that also covers the environment management system of the country. This assessment is also
supplemented by experience gained under an on-going project, the Poland Flood Emergency project. Based
on the findings of the institutional assessment report, the Bank concluded that in the majority of cases the
institutional capacity of the gminas is sufficient to identify and implement the infrastructure and the
education components. The activities financed under these components are being fulfilled on a daily basis
by gminas.
A review of the public procurement procedures also reveals that the proper legal and institutional elements
are in place, that allow gminas to invite tenders and carry out sub-projects' implementation.
Powiat. Powiat is a local self-governing community. This new administrative unit was restored with the
reform of public administration in January 1999. There are 308 powiats and 65 cities with the status of
powiat. The powiat is headed by the Starosta (Chairman of the Board). A city with the status of poviat is
headed by a president. A powiat covers a whole area of adjacent gminas, but the powiat does not supervise
the activities of gminas. A powiat performs public tasks whose scope goes beyond the gmina boundaries.
The involvement of powiats in the programming phase is limited to facilitating a consultation process that
allows for economies of scale.
Voivodship Self-government. The voivodship self-government is a regional self-governing community and
the largest unit of the territorial division of the state. Since January 1999, the number of voivodships has
decreased from forty-nine to sixteen. The voivodship self-government consists of the voivodship Sejmik, the
decision-making and controlling body, and the voivodship Board, the executive body. The voivodship
Marszalek is the chairman of the voivodship board. The Marszalek's office manages the daily affairs of the
voivodship, represents it and provides assistance to the voivodship Board. The voivodship self-government
is responsible for programming and stimulating regional development.
Voivodship Project Implementation Unit. The assessment of the institutional strengths and weaknesses of
these new established local self-government administration pointed out the need for identifying existing
institutions that have experience in managing development projects in cooperation with the central
government and the EU, and the proper staffing and professional skills required to provide that kind of
service to the Marszaleks. Such institutions have been identified in the Regional Development Agencies. An
institutional assessment was carried out, and an agreement was reached with the Marszaleks that these
agencies will provide services under contractual arrangements to act as VPIUs. RDAs are
semi-independent government foundations, which exist at the regional level in Poland, and whose purpose is
to help the jurisdiction to which they are attached to stimulate and improve local economic development. As
such, they are a natural choice as the managing body of national and international programs at the
voivodship level, and in the case of this project as the Voivodship Project Implementation Units (VPIUs).
Moreover, upon accession, RDAs will likely play an important role in building capacity for absorption of
the EU Structural Funds. Their main responsibility will be to support regional public administrations in
implementing the programs financed by the Structural Funds. In particular, the RDAs' activities will
concentrate on preparing regional strategic plans at the request of the voivodship government, identifying
specific investments, organizing a financial montage for the sub-projects, promoting EU programs,
conducting training programs on the EU structural policy for local and regional self-governments, as well
as for other social partners. Therefore, the objective of the RDP to co-operate and co-finance sub-projects
with the EU also argues for RDAs as the best candidates to work as voivodship implementation units for
- 26 -



the project.
Voivodship and Powiat Labor Offices. The Labor Redeployment component relies on the institutional
capacity that has been developed by the Ministry of Labor and Social Policy, principally through the
activities financed by its Labor Fund. The key institutions under this component is the Voivodship and
Powiat Labor Offices which signal labor services contracts.
As far as the microcredit component is concerned, there is a positive institutional experience of Polish
NGOs managing micro-credit funds. The Ministry of Labor and Social Policy also has positive experience.
Therefore, there was enough confidence to recommend designing this component in such a way that it will
utilize the NGOs' capacity, on a competitive basis.
Regional Steering Committee. The Regional Steering Committee is a new institutional set-up, therefore
there is some uncertainty as to how it will perform. However, the confidence needed to recommend such a
design is based on the analysis and the assessment of a multi-year regional program covering several
voivodships supported by the EU-Phare program, the Malopolska regional development program. The
positive results of that program (in one of the newly enlarged voivodships included in the RDP) and its
institutional and implementation arrangements have been used as a model to design the programming and
implementation arrangements for the RDP at the voivodship and gmina levels. Through the latter
preparation process for this project, the RSCs have already begun to operate with positive effectiveness.
4.2 Project management:
At the central level, the ministries involved in over-seeing the components have enough in-house capacity,
acquired either from former Bank projects, EU or bilateral programs, to efficiently fulfill their project
management role and function. This is true for the Ministry of Finance, the Ministry of Labor and Social
Policy and the Ministry of National Education. The Ministry of Agriculture and Rural Development has
previous experience in managing the ASAL project.
Project Coordination Unit. An important role in project management and implementation is foreseen for
the Project Coordination Unit. Previous Bank's experience with Project Coordination Units embodied
within the government structure has pointed out the need to locate the Project Coordination Unit outside
such structure, but reporting to the Minister of Agriculture and Rural Development. The professional skills
required from the employees of the Project Coordination Unit are different from those of government civil
servants.
An important element to maintain a multi-sector focus during project implementation is the co-operation
among ministries that will play a role in influencing the rural development program in Poland. Experience
with such an inter-ministerial body is mixed. One of the key factors to maintain interest and willingness to
co-operate is foreseen in the benefits that a given ministry will get from co-operating.
National Steering Committee. It is also imperative for the National Steering Committee to function
efficiently, and that the Minister of Agriculture and Rural Development will fulfill his role as Chairman,
personally or through an appointed deputy, with full determination. The formal prerequisite for this already
exists, since the government has formally established an inter-ministerial committee for the RDP, and
MARD has been designated as the lead agency for rural development.
As far as funds management and disbursement are concerned, there is positive evidence obtained under the
- 27 -



Poland Flood Emergency project that has led the team to recommend the same model for the RDP.
4.3 Procurement issues:
Procurement of goods and works will be done in accordance with World Bank Guidelines: Procurement
under the IBRD Loans and IDS Credits (issued in January 1995, revised January and August 1996,
September 1997, and January 1999). Consulting Services, technical assistance and training will be
procured in accordance with the Guidelines - Selection and Employment of consultants by World Bank
Borrowers, January 1997, revised September 1997 and January 1999. The Bank's Standard Bidding
Dbcuments, Request for Proposals and Forms of Consultants' Contract will be used. Goods and technical
assistance to be financed by the co-financiers will be procured in accordance with the public procurement
regulations or the regulations and practices of each co-financier. A general procurement notice will be
published in the Development Business of the UN in April 2000. For more details see Annex 6
4.4 Financial management issues:
General. The PCU, to be established within FAPA, will oversee the implementation of the project,
including its financial management arrangements. The key financial management position in the project will
be that of the prospective PCU's Financial Manager. The PCU will work closely together with the project
teams created in the responsible line ministries to oversee the project's individual components and
sub-components. By working closely with all these agencies, the PCU will ensure the appropriateness of the
project's financial management arrangements as a whole as well as those of the implementing agencies. It is
a condition of project effectiveness that the financial management arrangements of the project are
satisfactory to the Bank. It should be noted, however, that by design, some of the implementing agencies
will only be selected after project effectiveness; in these circumstances, the PCU will ensure that both the
Terms of Reference for these implementing agencies as well as their actual financial management
arrangements are consistent with the existing financial management arrangements of the project and are
satisfactory to the Bank.
Financial Management Assessment. As a condition of loan effectiveness, a financial management system
acceptable to the Bank will be put in place. The following pre-requisites will have to be satisfied:
-  one accountant, whose qualifications and experience are acceptable to the Bank, will need to be
employed at each participating VPIU;
*  three accountants, whose qualifications and experience are acceptable to the Bank, will need to be
employed at the PCU;
*  a letter will need to be sent to the Marszaleks of the participating voivodships explaining the
requirements of the financial management system that will need to be established at their VPIUs,
including the formats of the PMRs that will need to be produced by their VPIUs' financial management
systems;
*  at least five participating VPIUs, the responsible ministry project teams and PCU will need to have an
installed and operational internet email connection;
*  the production of a paper describing the operation of the financial management system as installed at
each of the participating VPIUs, responsible ministry project teams and PCU;
*  the evaluation and certification of the acceptability of the financial management arrangements of the
project by a Bank Financial Management Specialist in accordance with the Bank's Loan Administration
Change Initiative (LACI);
*  the financial management system of the project must be capable of producing PMRs showing the
project's Sources and Uses of Funds, Uses of Funds by Activity, and Special Account Statement.
- 28 -



Under the infrastructure component, the requirements concerning the VP1Us will also appropriately apply
to the regional offices of ARMA. A detailed time-bound action plan to strengthen the financial management
system, and enable the preparation of Project Management Reports acceptable to the Bank not later than
December 31, 2000, will be presented prior to effectiveness.
Financial Management Systems. The project's management systems will be computer-based, networked
and distributed across the various project teams and implementing agencies to allow for the timely and
efficient collection, processing and transfer of information, including the request for and approval of
transfers of project funds. Loan conditions assure that the financial management system is acceptable to the
Bank. The design and operation of the project's financial management systems is documented in Annex 2
of the operational manual.
Project Management Reporting. The formats of the Project Management Reports (PMRs), consistent
with the formats advocated in the Bank's Loan Administration Change Initiative (LACI) Implementation
Handbook and related guidance is presented in the operational manual, and will be a key consideration in
the design of the project's financial management systems that will be developed prior to project
effectiveness. The PCU will have overall responsibility for the co-ordination of the production of the PMRs
by the various implementing agencies. The PMRs will be prepared as at the end of every calendar quarter
and submitted within 45 days thereof with the first set of PMRs being due for the period ending December
31, 2000. Draft formats for the PMRs are included as an attachment to Annex 2.4 of the operational
manual.
Disbursement. The project will initially disburse under the Bank's traditional method of disbursement,
with the option of moving to the Project Management Reports (PMR)-based disbursement method at the
mutual agreement of the Borrower and the Bank.
Audit Arrangements. Audits, conducted in accordance with appropriate Polish regulations and principles
that respect International Standards on Auditing (ISA) as issued by IFAC, by auditors acceptable to the
Bank, will be performed: in the case of the project, annually for each fiscal year of the life of the project as
well as at the completion of the project; and in the case of the implementing agencies, annually for each
fiscal year of the life of the project. The scope of the project audit will agree with that outlined in the Bank'
s Financial Accounting Reporting and Auditing Handbook (FARAH) and in Annex 10 to the Project
Financial Management Manual and will include an audit of the PMRs, a Summary Statement of SOEs
submitted in the period and a Special Account Statement. The audit of the project's financial statements
will probably require the appropriate inclusion of the financial systems and records of the project teams and
implementing agencies, as deemed necessary by the project's auditors. The scope of the audits of the
implementing agencies will include their financial statements prepared in accordance with appropriate
Polish regulations and principles that respect International Accounting Standards (IAS) as issued by the
International Accounting Standards Committee. The audited financial statements will be prepared and
submitted within six months of the end of every fiscal year. The short-listing of the project's potential
auditors is a condition of project effectiveness.
5. Environmental:                 Environmental Category: F
5.1 Summarize the steps undertaken for environmental assessment and EMP preparation (including
consultation and
disclosure) and the significant issues and their treatment emerging from this analysis.
Major environmental issues: environmental issues are primarily associated with component C
- 29 -



(Infrastructure). Under this component, the issues are likely to be experienced primarily in the construction
phase ( land clearing, movement of men, equipment and materials to and from project site, etc.). However,
if the projects are improperly designed, environmental issues would be manifested during operations (e.g.
wastewater sludge disposal, solid waste leachates contaminating groundwater supplies, etc.).
Other environmental issues: the variety of sub-projects likely to be supported under the project will require
careful attention and evaluation by responsible local/regional (voivodship, gmina etc.) and possibly national
environmental authorities. Environmental legislation and the environmental regulatory framework in Poland
are in general both adequate and effective. However, specific local institutional capability and mechanisms
must be carefully delineated and assessed for effectiveness.
Justification/Rationale for category rating: projects will be identified and prepared at local levels. Since
funds will flow through intermediate institutions, the Fl Category (financial instruments) is considered most
appropriate.
A rural environmental protection project has been negotiated in January 1999. For RDP, as part of project
preparation a paper has been prepared that: i) describes the main features of the Polish environmental law
and procedures as they apply to the types of sub-projects likely to be funded under the project; ii) assesses
the quality of the implementation of those procedures in the proposed project area, with special attention to
less-developed regions; and iii) pinpoints any areas where procedures may need tightening, staff may need
training or the Bank's prior review of sub-projects might be indicated. Results of the study have been used
to guide pertinent parts of the operational manual.
Actions taken during project preparation: specific mechanisms and specific institutions likely to be
responsible for specific sub-project environmental evaluation, permitting, licensing, approval etc. have been
identified. Furthermore, an assessment of the technical capabilities of environmental institutions has been
done, and as required a program of institutional strengthening has been included in the project for them to
effectively discharge their responsibilities for environmental approval. Additionally, a comparison between
World Bank environmental review (EA) requirements and those of Poland has been made. To the extent
that differences (e.g. category assignment, public participation, documentation and implementation
requirements for environmental management plans etc.) were found, local Polish environmental institutions
have been provided with adequate and sufficient strengthening to review and approve projects in a manner
that is consistent with both requirements of Polish law and World Bank environmental policies and
procedures.
Local groups and NGOs consulted: numerous self-government organizations, foundations and NGOs have
been consulted during identification. Many of these are also participating together with ministries and
agencies in the Inter-Agency Task Force convened to assist in criteria selection and other aspects of project
preparation. A partial list of domestic non-governmental and self-government organizations contacted to
date includes:
*   Water Supply Foundation
*   Ekoland Association
*   Polish Academy of Science
*   EcoFund
*   Polish Foundation for SME Promotion and Development
*   Voivodship Offices in all project provinces repeatedly
*   Malopolska Regional Council
*   European Fund for Polish Village Development
- 30 -



*   Fund for Development of Polish Agriculture (FDPA)
*   Agriculture Development Fund
*  Association of Gminas
*   Upper Raba Water-shed Federation
*   Many project powiats, and an average of forty project gminas.
5.2 What are the main features of the EMP and are they adequate?
This project covers the whole territory of the country and is characterized as a bottom-up approach in
identifying small infrastructure projects. This approach requires an adequate environmental management
capacity to enforce compliance of environmental standards and procedures. Following an institutional
assessment of the environmental management capacity, it was decided that local environment department
would be responsible for environmental screening of potential investment projects issuing environment
permits and enforcement of the permits. During project implementation, the implementing agencies will be
required to measure environmental progress against environmental indicators incorporated in the
Operational Manual as well as in the environment permits.
5.3 For Category A and B projects, timeline and status of EA:
Date of receipt of final draft: April 29, 1999
5.4 How have stakeholders been consulted at the stage of (a) environmental screening and (b) draft EA
report on the environmental impacts and proposed environment management plan? Describe mechanisms
of consultation that were used and which groups were consulted?
During project preparation, environment screening was incorporated in project identification at the
community level through direct consultations with beneficiary communities. Responsibility for
enforcement of environmental standards and procedures is with the local environment departments
throughout the country. As discussed above, the institutional environmental management capacity in
Poland is in general sufficient to assure compliance with environmental standards and laws.
5.5 What mechanisms have been established to monitor and evaluate the impact of the project on the
environment? Do the indicators reflect the objectives and results of the EMP?
Each small infrastructure investment project would be subject to environmental screening and permitting by
appropriate environment department. Only project projects that comply with EU and Polish environmental
standards will be eligible for project financing. Environmental indicators have been incorporated in the
Operational Manual and each voivodship implementation unit will provide an annual summary of the
environmental impacts of the implemented investments.
6. Social:
6.1 Summarize key social issues relevant to the project objectives, and specify the project's social
development outcomes.
The Social Assessment consisted of two main activities (see Annex I 1): (a) a collection of case studies,
expert consultations, and position papers focusing on the three topical areas of local governance, education,
and employment; and (b) the start of a system of monitoring and evaluation of primary schools in two
voivodships. Fieldwork in the form of in-depth interviews and participatory observation was carried out in
three rural towns in the Southeastern voivodship of Podkarpacki by a network of researchers highly
respected in the fields of anthropology and sociology; students; and community field workers and NGO
representatives with expertise in rural development and village life. The second activity involved a
collaboration with the Curator's Office in the Podkarpacki voivodship to build upon, and standardize,
existing data collection efforts taking place at individual schools with the aim to assess how they are faring
- 31 -



before and after consolidation.
The poor rural infrastructure contributes to a number of problems in rural Poland. Poor
telecommunications prevent rural residents from finding out about job opportunities, and enterprises from
reaching their markets. The costs and infrequency of transportation likewise interfere with schooling and
employment. Transportation costs discourage individuals from seeking employment away from agriculture,
and limits families' ability to pay for their children's education. Poor transportation also interferes with
agricultural business by raising costs and lowering the quality of perishable foods which must be delivered
quickly, and may add costs to setting up non-agricultural businesses.
Citizens' participation in government appears limited both by a culture of passivity and by the scarcity of
formalized opportunities to participate in decisions which affect their lives. Their reactions to educational
reform are a case in point. Education is seen as a priority by the citizens; however, they feel ambivalence
towards the decentralization of education. While they support pedagogical and curriculum reforms, they
express uncertainty about the ability of sub-national governments to pay for education without financial
support from the central government. They feel distressed by the hasty introduction of reforms and the lack
of consultation with local officials and residents. Moreover, the closure of schools, which have often been
important foci of community activism and cooperation, is seen as a blow to local prestige. Therefore,
instead of being completely shut down, local schools should be converted into community centers which
would both be able to take over some non-pedagogical functions of education like extracurricular activities,
and would provide a location in which gmina officials could meet on a regular basis with local residents.
This would also help overcome local resistance to educational reform by seeking out community input and
retaining the benefits of past cooperation.
Self-government officials often do not devote enough attention to actively seeking input from citizens, nor
do they actively seek out opportunities to co-operate with other gminas or develop specific plans for their
gminas. Instances of "best practice" by government officials appear to be the results of the specific
personalities involved, rather than the operation of regularized government procedures. Therefore,
programs should be developed to raise the professionalization of gmina officials, who should be required to
formally engage their communities on a more frequent, regularized basis. In addition, they should receive
assistance in developing realistic plans for their communities. Examples of "best practice" in community
relations, development planning, and inter-gmina cooperation can be found in various actions of
self-governments, especially those in the Upper Raba water-shed (Malopolskie voivodship).
Although many residents are willing to find non-farm employment, existing job training programs do not
provide skills appropriate to the new economy. Individuals seeking to start their own business are hindered
by poor rural infrastructure, and by a lack of credit to support such ventures. Therefore, job training and
support for entrepreneurship in the countryside need to be developed. In particular, job training must
become more responsive to the needs of job seekers as they themselves express them, and a credit program
should be instituted to provide capital for enterprises which would take advantage of the rural regions'
existing advantages, namely, natural beauty and traditional farming methods. Agro-tourism and small-scale
market food production should be encouraged.
Recommendations from the Social Assessment have been incorporated in the project design and
implementation in the following ways. First, a variety of mechanisms have been put into place to ensure
on-going and regular public participation. Second, a follow-on Social Assessment addressing issues related
to the school network, has been conducted in early August. The findings of the assessment have been used
to assist the Bank in making recommendations to the gmina departments of education on the ways they can
mitigate any negative consequences arising from the school consolidation process. Third, examples of best
- 32 -



practice in the two areas of private sector development and education will be supported during project
implementation. These examples are important in showing residents how they can support investments
through locally-inspired community initiatives. Fourth, gender issues have become more fully integrated
into monitoring and evaluation through the development of gender-based indicators and through the
adoption of equal opportunity criteria in the allocation of SME credit.
In addition to these activities, a set of social indicators (refer to Annex 12) for the project as a whole and
for each component will be regularly monitored during supervision. Education indicators at the school level
will also monitor the progress of schools and students before and after school consolidation.
6.2 Participatory Approach: How are key stakeholders participating in the project?
Primary project beneficiaries will be rural residents, and those served by public utilities (recipients of new
and improved services), civil servants (recipients of training and better systems management), unemployed
persons and former state farm employees (recipients of training and greater job opportunities), new and
existing entrepreneurs (recipients of credit, technical assistance, training), parents and children (recipients
of better quality schools), teachers and local administrators (recipients of training and an improved teaching
environment), and women. Hundreds of these individuals have been interviewed by Bank staff and
consultants (including professionally qualified local consultants) involved in project design, and a larger
number were formally canvassed and surveyed. The design of the project is the result of this effort, as well
as the formal choices of the elected local leaders concerned.
Other stakeholders would include: government institutions (MOF, MARD, MOLPS, MONE, MEP,
MIAPA), self-governments and financial organizations, NGOs, private sector organizations, and EU
institutions and other donors addressing rural development issues in Poland. The Government of Poland is a
key stakeholder because it is interested to see that a viable financing system for rural development
develops, and because it seeks admission into the EU, which will require improved environmental and
economic development conditions in lagging rural regions.
These organizations have participated in the design process in a number of ways. First, individual gminas
and associations of local communities have been consulted on past efforts to finance infrastructure and on
priority investment needs. Literally dozens of gminas, in every corner of Poland, have been visited and
canvassed in detail by Bank mission staff over 1998-1999, and over a thousand self-government officials
participated in seminars for identification of sub-projects. These consultations were critical in pushing
gmina officials to develop their land use plans and prepare proposals for the pipeline, and to mobilize local
support for the project. A needs assessment has also been done for the education component. Second, an
umbrella network of mostly urban based business incubators, established under a Bank-supported urban
employment project, has been consulted on ways to apply the concept of business incubators to rural areas.
Positive impressions gained from visits to several urban incubators led to the conclusion that rural
incubators should be supported in this project. Third, the Social Assessment utilized a highly participatory
approach with contributions from local residents and employees of school and employment institutions,
officials at the MONE and MOLSP, community based workers, NGO personnel, university professors, and
school curators in one voivodship (See Annex 11). Fourth, surveys, beneficiary assessments, and public
awareness campaigns and consultations are planned as regular activities under the various components and
sub-components. These consultations will provide much of the informational bases of the sub-components,
and their results will serve as mechanisms for stakeholders and beneficiaries to voice their opinions, and in
some instances, actively participate in project activities. Finally, there has been tremendous grassroots
involvement from over 32 NGOs during project preparation. NGOs, together with Ministries and other
agencies, developed the eligibility criteria for the selection of gminas into the project, as well as assisted
- 33 -



other areas of project preparation. NGOs will play important roles during project implementation, chiefly
as service providers of educational and employment services and training, and as partners in the creation
and operation of business centers.

F. Sustainability and Risks
1. Sustainability:
The project has developed procedures for identifying and screening investment projects, that will ensure the
efficient and transparent use of funds. This system will allow for parallel financing of projects from
different EU sources (SAPARD and PHARE), therefore creating the basis for the absorption of EU
structural funds after accession of Poland to the EU. This system will also create the basis for cooperation
between the local and national governments in achieving regional and national objectives in a harmonized
way. In addition, sustainability will also be ensured by the demand-driven identification of projects, and the
substantial required local co-financing. A project management structure and a maintenance plan for
infrastructure, including sustained annual budgeting and appropriate user fees, will be an obligatory part of
the design of each sub-project. Appropriate TA is provided to enhance sustainability of sub-projects. The
main difficulty faced by the off-farm employment sub-component in achieving sustainability is the low
incidence of entrepreneurship in the country's rural areas, which implies that achieving sustainability in
rural Poland may require more time than in other environments.

1. Project Components
1.    The project is designed so that individual elements from any component can be implemented at the
local level, singly or in combination with others. For example, a gmina may identify the need for a
waste-water treatment investment, training for several unemployed in business management, and a credit to
help an entrepreneur start a small business. A package of services and investments will be developed to
meet these needs drawing on resources across all project components. Such packages will form a part of
the broader investment program submitted by gminas to the regional authorities for co-financing. The new
administrative structures that began operating as of January 1, 1999 were obliged to formulate investment
plans by the end of June 1999.
2.     The following are the components and sub-components financed under the project:
Component A: Micro-credit
Component B: Human Capital Development
Sub-Component B-I: Labor Redeployment
Sub-Component B-2: Education
Sub-Component B-3: Institution Building in Local and Regional Administrations
Component C: Rural Infrastructure.
By Component:
Project Component I - US$19.05 million
Component A: Microcredit (USS19.05 million).
3.     Component Objectives. The objective of this component is to assist the Government in promoting
micro-enterprises to create and maintain self-employment in rural areas. A micro-enterprise in this context
is defined as enterprise with five or less employees. In so doing, it will allow empirical comparisons of this
proactive approach versus the current predominant regime of passive social assistance. In particular, the
component will promote off-farm self-employment in rural gminas of five voivodships. These five
voivodships will be selected by the Ministry of Labor and Social Policy (MOLSP) on the basis of the
following criteria: (i) high unemployment rates in rural areas, (ii) high share of households with state
agriculture as main source of income in 1988, (iii) high number of employed in state agriculture as a
percentage of the total population working in agriculture in 1988, and (iv) state-used land as a percentage
of total agricultural land in 1988.
4.    In addition to the criteria listed above to select the voivoships in which the component will be
implemented, the Borrower may explore the possibility of including as an additional criterion the
willingness of potentially interested voivodship governments to contribute additional resources to the
component. These additional resources may (a) finance a portion of incremental costs incurred in
implementing the component, (b) increase the pool of funds to be on-lent to eligible micro-borrowers under
the component in the corresponding voivodship, or (c) both. The introduction of such a criterion, and its
detailed implementation arrangements will require Bank agreement.
5.    Investments in acquiring the empirical evidence alluded to above, of the relative
cost-effectiveness of micro-enterprise promotion versus social welfare payments, are justified because
government interventions in labor and rural financial markets are very costly. Any mistakes may be
- 48 -



extremely expensive. Hence, the component will underpin actions to improve policy development for
dealing with rural unemployment, which is one of the most urgent public policy issues in Poland.
6.     The ex-ante economic and financial analysis in Annex 4 provides reasonable indications that
a package of grants, technical assistance, and micro-loans to create new and promote existing
micro-enterprises may become a cost effective alternative to the instruments used by the government
to deal with long term rural unemployment. The analysis compares the expected costs of these
alternative instruments for reasonable values of variables such as (a) size of start-up grants, (b)
survival rates of newly created micro-enterprises, (c) loan losses on micro-credits, (d) administrative
costs and service fees, (e) expected duration of unemployment spells, and (f) social assistance
payments to unemployed.
7.     Tasks. To achieve its objectives, the component will finance (a) one-time small grants for the
chronically unemployed to compensate for labor market regulations which make the transition from
dependence on social security assistance to reliance on self-employment simply too risky; (b) micro-loans
to new and existing rural micro-enterprises; (c) advisory services for newly established micro-enterprises
created under the component; and (d) component implementation and management costs incurred by the
National Economy Bank (BGK) which has been identified as the implementing agency, the MOLSP and the
non-governmental organizations or financial institutions which will be selected to assist in implementing the
component as Service Providers (SPs); as well as (e) technical assistance to these entities. All project costs
include a small (2.5%) contingency. Details on these activities follow.
8.     One-time Grants to the Rural Unemployed (US$2.9 million). The Government will provide
enough resources as counterpart funding for the component to allocate a one-time-only grant of PLN3,600
(US$842.5 equivalent) to those individuals who meet the eligibility criteria detailed below.
9.    It is expected that during the implementation of the component approximately 3,400 beneficiaries
will receive these grants. The total amount of grants is thus expected to be about PLN 12.2 million (US$2.9
million equivalent).
10.    The purpose of the grants is to provide chronically unemployed rural residents with the resources
to pay the minimum social insurance premium, the so called ZUS tax (Zaklad Ubezpieczen Spolecznycm)
required by law, PLN3,600 a year. The grants will be disbursed in four quarterly installments of PLN900
each (US$210.6 equivalent). The second, third, and fourth installments will be contingent on verification of
continued self-employment and eligibility criteria of beneficiaries. The grants are justified in the context of
this component because it is unlikely that unemployed individuals would be willing to relinquish social
assistance subsidies and pay ZUS taxes upon gaining (registered) self-employment. Thus, they may serve
as a critical prerequisite for wide participation in the micro-credit program.
II.    Eligibility Criteria for One-time Grants. Beneficiaries of grants must (a) have resided in the
rural gminas of the selected voivodships for at least one year; (b) intend to become self-employed by
establishing a micro-business in such gminas; (c) insure that their micro-businesses comply with all
applicable laws and regulations in force in Poland; and (d) meet one or more of the following additional
conditions (i) be a registered unemployed, (ii) be beneficiary of social aid willing to give up such assistance
upon gaining self-employment, or (iii) be full time farmers controlling less than 3 hectares of land.
12.    Eligible individuals may receive only one grant during the implementation of the component.
SPs and BGK (see implementation arrangements beldw) will maintain and share among themselves a
list of all grant recipients. These lists will include the name and national identification card number
- 49 -



(Dowod Osobisty) of each grant beneficiary and will be used to prevent individuals from obtaining
more than one grant.
13.    Micro-loans to the Rural Unemployed and Existing Micro-Entrepreneurs (US$13.89 million).
The component will provide financing for a US$13.89 million line of micro-credit to finance eligible
sub-projects. Funding for the credit line will be obtained from the World Bank loan (US$10.42 million) and
from the Service Providers (US$3.47 million) which will be selected to assist in implementing the
component (see implementation arrangements below). The following are the main terms and conditions for
the allocation and recovery of the component's credit line.
14.    Eligible Borrowers and Sub-projects. Borrowers eligible to obtain micro-loans under the
component will comply with the following conditions: (a) they will be either unemployed willing to gain
self-employment outside agriculture or existing micro rural entrepreneurs (e.g. cottage industry producers,
agro-processors, artisans, service providers, and small traders and merchants); (b) they will be residents of
the rural gminas of the selected voivodships, including towns within those voivodships of less than 15,000
inhabitants; (c) they will be individuals (physical persons), although their enterprises may be incorporated
as legal entities; and (d) their newly established or existing micro-businesses must comply with all
applicable laws and regulations in force in Poland, including registration requirements.
15.    According to the Law on Economic Activity dated December 23, 1988 (article 9) economic
activities performed by persons from which those persons earn an additional income (casual
profit-making occupation), shall not be required to be registered. They will be, therefore, also eligible
to receive micro-credits under the component despite not being registered. The exempted activities are
(a) production for self-consumption and production of handicrafts and art; (b) maintenance of family
or farm equipment, including houses, and other services performed with own materials and tools,
except profit-making transportation of passengers; (c) commercial transactions of agricultural
products including vegetables, fruit, animals, meat, forest products and meals; and (d) leasing of
rooms or lots for tents given by farmers to tourists within the areas of their farms as well as the sale
of meals and performance of other services connected therewith.
16.   Micro-loans financed under the component could finance all short and medium term
investments required to start new or expand existing rural micro-enterprises. Short and medium-term
investments are those whose cash flows will allow repayment of micro-loans within 36 months. Land,
existing buildings and other assets previously used in the rural sector (except breeding livestock) will
be ineligible as well as investments in tobacco production and processing.
17.    Terms and conditions on micro-loans. As detailed in the section on implementation
arrangements (see below), non-governmental organizations or financial institutions with sufficient
financial means and the willingness-to accept the risk for at least 25 percent of the micro-loans will
be selected as service providers (SPs) to assist in implementing the component. Therefore, the
conditions on micro-loans set forth below reflect those of micro-loans jointly funded by the
Government using World Bank Loan proceeds and by the SPs using their own resources.
18.    The maximum amount outstanding of all the micro-loans granted to a single borrower will be
US$5,000 equivalent. The maximum repayment term for micro-loans will be 36 months. Repayment
terms and schedules for each micro-loan will be determined by the SPs on the basis of the cash flow
profile of the sub-project financed. Collateral requirements would be determined by SPs to adequately
cover the risks associated with the nature of the sub-borrower's activities. Acceptable security will
include (a) joint and several liability among groups of borrowers (i.e. group lending); (b) co-signers
- 50 -



with fiduciary liability for the micro-loans; and (c) real assets pledged as collateral.
19.    The interest rate on micro-loans will be the weighted average of (a) the interest rate charged
by the Government on its 75 percent share of each micro-loan, and (b) the interest rate charged by the
eligible Services Providers on the remaining 25 percent of each micro-loan. The initial rate charged
by the Government on its share of the funds will be 13 percent (this rate is based on the 12% interest
rate on 1-year Treasury Bills plus a 1% risk margin). The rate charged by SPs on their share will be
determined by SPs on the basis of prevailing local market conditions but it will not be more than 500
basis points above the rate charged by the Government on its share. The interest rates on the
Government's and the relevant voivodship self-government's share of micro-loans will be determined
semi-annually and in no case would be below the six-month average official inflation plus 150 basis
points.
20.    Micro-loans will be denominated in PLN and the Government and the SPs will bear the
foreign exchange risk on their corresponding shares. The exchange risk to the Government ought to
be compensated by the interest rate differential between IBRD's standard rate and the rates paid on
project funds by borrowers. This differential is considered appropriate because the exchange rate risk
is low as the country enjoys exchange rate stability, international capital mobility, and low inflation.
The Government will accrue interest and principal payments on its 75 percent share of each
micro-loan exactly in the same terms and conditions (back-to-back) of the micro-loans allocated to
eligible borrowers.
21.    At the end of component implementation, the Government will decide on the future allocation
of (a) its 75 percent share of the outstanding portfolio of micro-loans, and (b) cash from interest and
principal payments generated under the component. Options available to the Government for these
assets include (a) continuing with a similar program, (b) divesting its share in the portfolio of
micro-loans, (c) reverting to the Government's budget the interest and principal collected as
micro-loans mature.
22.    Advisory Services to Micro-Entrepreneurs (US$0.28 million). The SPs will provide technical
assistance and business advice to borrowers and beneficiaries of grants. These non-financial services will
focus on (a) business registration; (b) business planning; (c) record keeping; and (d) taxation and relevant
legislation. Advisory services will be conducted in small groups, enabling an individual and concrete
approach, yet encouraging interaction and mutual support among group members. SPs will be able to
choose between providing these services in house and sub-contracting with suitable institutions satisfactory
to BGK and the Bank. SPs will remain, however, responsible for the quality and timely delivery of these
services.
23.    Training and Technical Assistance (US$0.28 million). The component will finance (a) training
and technical assistance to BGK and government officials (US$130,000), and (b) assistance to selected SPs
on best practices and implementation of the micro-credit program (US$150,000).
24.    Component Management (US$0.47 million). The component will also finance (a) the incremental
costs incurred by BGK in implementing the component and in managing the assets of the flow of funds
under the component (US$330,000), (b) the incremental costs incurred by the Department for Structural
and Regional Policy (DSRP) in the MOLSP in monitoring and evaluating the component (US$140,000).
25.    Implementation. The project will be implemented by BGK in close collaboration with up to five
non-governmental organizations or financial institutions which will act as SPs; the MOLSP who will have
- 51 -



the overall monitoring responsibility; and consultants widely recognized as experts in micro-finance to
provide specialized advice to BGK, the SPs and MOLSP.
26.    Treasury Bank. The Bank Gospodarstwa Krajowego (BGK) has been identified to act as (a)
implementing agency, and (b) treasurer and cashier for the funds available to the component. BGK
will enter into a legal agreement with the Government. Based on the legal agreement BGK will (i)
select the SPs in close cooperation with the relevant VPIUs; (ii) sign the Agency Agreements with the
selected SPs; (iii) monitor the SP's continuing compliance with eligibility criteria; (iv) disburse loan
funds and payments to the SPs; (v) collect interest accrued and principal repayments received on
micro-loans; (vi) organize financial control and audit of the SPs; and (vii) maintain adequate records
of the portion of the portfolio of micro-loans outstanding due to the Government under the
component.
27.    BGK will be the treasurer and cashier for (a) funds disbursed and to be disbursed to SPs for
financing eligible (i) grants, (ii) micro-loans, and (iii) fees for services; and (b) interest accrued and
principal repayments received on micro-loans.
28.    Ministry of Labor and Social Policy. Responsibilities within MOLSP will be allocated to
the Department for Structural and Regional Policy (DSRP). To support the DSRP, a consultant may
be recruited. The DSRP will: (a) monitor the economic sustainability, outreach, and general operation
of the SPs, (b) promote coordination and sharing of lessons learned among participating SPs, and (c)
observe and evaluate the socioeconomic profile of the component's beneficiaries. Details on these
activities are provided in the section below on Monitoring and Evaluation.
29.   Incremental costs and computer equipment for DSRP. As mentioned above, the World
Bank loan proceeds will provide financing (US$140,000) for (a) one long term consultants, and (b)
one computer (including software) and printer to assist in the monitoring and supervising of the
component. The consultant will report to the DSRP Director, and his/her services will be engaged
under individual fixed term professional service contracts (up to two years).
30.    Voivodship Project Implementation Units. The role of the relevant VPIUs will be to (a)
select service providers in close collaboration with the BGK, (b) collect monthly and semi-annually
reports from the selected service providers, and (c) inform the relevant Regional Steering Committees
(RSCs) about the performance of the service providers.
31.    Service Providers. Up to five non-governmental organizations or financial institutions with
demonstrated experience in micro-finance and/or retail lending, sufficient financial means, and the
willingness to accept the risk for at least 25 percent of the micro-loans financed under the component
will be selected on a competitive basis as service providers (SPs).
32.    Services Rendered by Service Providers. In implementing the component, the Government
will grant the authority and SPs will assume the responsibility for (a) verifying eligibility of
beneficiaries of the grants, micro-loans, and services financed under the component; (b) disbursing
grants to eligible beneficiaries; (c) allocating micro-loans to new and existing rural micro-enterprises
financed with Government resources (75 percent) and with its own resources (25 percent); (d)
providing advisory services for newly established or expanded micro-enterprises under the
component; (e) collecting repayments of interests and principals; and (f) if required, enforcing
micro-loan contracts in Poland's courts on behalf of the Government and in its own behalf.
- 52 -



33.    Eligibility criteria for service providers. Eligible SPs must be legally incorporated private
entities, satisfactory to the World Bank, which are able to demonstrate enough capabilities to deliver
microcredit and provide business services in rural areas, including (a) sound organization with
competent management, strong internal control, financial management and operational capacity, (b)
preferably an established record of delivering successfully services in rural areas and proven ability
to work with gminas and local partners, and (c) willingness and ability to share in the credit risk and
to co-finance up to 25 percent of the micro-loans allocated under the component. The risk sharing
mechanism has been designed to provide incentives to the selected SPs for the careful screening of
sub-borrowers and strong incentives to recover the micro-loans financed under the component. A list
of specific indicators for verifying the eligibility of SPs is presented in the operational manual.
34.   In appraising this component, the project team verified that there are sufficient micro-finance
organizations in Poland which comply with these eligibility criteria and which are also interested in
participating in the program. The operational manual (Section III - Annex II) also contains financial
information and letters of intention from several micro-finance NGOs in Poland. Nonetheless and in
order to guarantee a transparent and competitive selection process, there will be widespread
advertisement among all potentially qualified institutions in the country and maybe even
internationally (see below section on selection of SPs).
35.    Payments to Service Providers. Proceeds from the World Bank loan for an estimated
amount of US$1.3 million will be used to cover the fees that will be charged by SPs for their
services. This estimate corresponds to the total fees over a period of three years. The following is a
summarized itemization of the estimated fees: (a) US$920,000 to cover 75% of the initial investments
and 85% of operational costs of managing the Government's share in the portfolio of the micro-loans
component for ten micro-finance outlets in rural areas plus reasonable contingencies; (b)
US$140,000 for allocating approximately 3,400 grants to beneficiaries, the amount corresponds to 5
percent of the estimated US$2.9 million equivalent to be disbursed in grants plus reasonable
contingencies; and (c) US$280,000 to cover advisory services to an estimated number of 3,900
beneficiaries.
36.    The above service fees are just illustrative of reasonable estimated costs. The actual amount
of the fees will likely deviate from these estimates simply because SPs will be selected through
competitive bidding (see below). Organizations interested in being selected as SPs will submit bids
for the amount of fees they would require to deliver the services and take the necessary credit risks.
These bids will not necessarily coincide with the estimates above.
37.    Selection of Service Providers. The BGK together with the MOLPS and the relevant VPIUs
will be responsible for reviewing the technical and financial documents submitted by the potential
SPs. The selection of the service providers will be done in accordance with the methods specified in
annex 6.
38.    Contracting of Service Providers. Selected service providers will enter into the following
two contracts with BGK. First, a Consultant Services Contract based on the standard form of
contract for consultants' services for complex, time based assignments issued by the World Bank.
Second, an Agency and Co-financing Agreement which will be entered simultaneously to the
Consultants Service Contract. The Agency and Co-financing Agreement will be an integral part of
the Consultant Service Contract.
- 53 -



39.    The Consultant Services Contract will set forth the obligations of the SPs for (a) establishing
and maintaining adequate accounting and internal control systems to reflect all outlays and
expenditures made under the project in accordance with sound accounting practices, satisfactory to
the World Bank; (b) keeping documentation which will support disbursements of grants and
micro-credits at every stage of the component, and form the basis for generating monthly financial
statements for the SPs and the component; (c) auditing the records of all outlays under the component
each year, by auditors satisfactory to the Bank and in accordance with appropriate auditing principles
consistently applied; (d) providing all necessary information to BGK, the MOLSP and to World
Bank staff to monitor the success in terms of outreach and economic sustainability of the package of
activities financed by the component, including data (i) to compose financial statements (e.g., income
statement, balance sheet, statement of uses and origins of funds) for each SP, and (ii) to monitor the
socio-economic profile and economic activities of beneficiaries.
40.    The Agency and Co-financing Agreement's main purpose is to furnish the SPs with (a) the
obligation of using their existing own resources to co-finance and assume 25 percent of the risk
implicit in each and every micro-loan made during component implementation to eligible borrowers,
and (b) the legal authority to (i) negotiate and sign micro-loan contracts, on behalf of the
Government, with eligible borrowers, and (ii) legally enforce micro-loan contracts on behalf of the
Government; including repossessing collateral and assets from final borrowers.
41.    Consultants to Provide Technical Assistance to Government, BGK and Service
Providers. Following the selection of the SPs, the BGK, MOLSP, the VPIUs and the SPs will agree
on the subjects on which the BGK, the MOLSP, the VPIUs and SPs need technical assistance and
will contract the required consultant services. Consultants eligible for these assignments will be (a)
experts with appropriate background and experience, and (b) internationally recognized (i) consulting
firms and (ii) financial or micro-finance institutions.
42.    Consultants will provide assistance on all aspects related to designing and implementing
self-employment and micro-credit programs. Examples include (a) training SP staff; (b) selecting and
extending methodologies for non-financial self-employment services; (c) setting up monitoring and
information systems (MIS); (d) information campaigns on off-farm self employment; (e) policy
dialogue on labor and micro-finance market issues.
43.    Selection and engagement of consultants for the above activities and studies, which are
expected to amount to about US$280,000 and which will be financed from proceeds from the World
Bank Loan, will be carried out in accordance with the Bank's "Guidelines for the Use of Consultants
by World Bank Borrowers and by the World Bank as Executing Agency".
44.    Technical assistance to Service Providers is justified because adapting to the Polish context
the international experience and best practices for promoting self-employment and delivering
micro-credit requires significant experience, in addition to local experimentation. The proposed
technical assistance to SPs is aimed at adapting to Poland successful micro-finance methodologies
based on: (a) low fixed-cost structures; (b) screening borrowers on the basis of their character and
reputation; (c) conducive incentive schemes for employees making credit decisions; (d) adequate
internal control mechanisms, and (e) innovative collateral and collateral substitutes such as movable
assets and group liability, respectively.
45.    Flow of Funds. The MOF will transfer the resources required for implementing the
component to BGK following signature of the Legal Agreement. The Legal Agreement will authorize
- 54 -



BGK to transfer cash advances to SPs by crediting bank accounts established by SPs for the sole
purpose of implementing the component.
46.    These cash advances will be used for the purposes and under the terms and conditions
established in the Consultant Contracts and the Agency and Co-Financing Agreements between the
Government and SPs; namely: (a) allocating grants to eligible beneficiaries, (b) allocating
micro-loans to eligible sub-borrowers, (c) fees to SPs for (i) managing the Government's share of the
portfolio of micro-loans under the component, and (ii) technical assistance to eligible beneficiaries.
47.    The initial advance to each SP will be for an amount sufficient to cover two months of the
estimated costs of operation for each SP, according to SPs' business plans. However, in no case the
advance to a given SP will be larger than US$100,000 equivalent, or 20% of the total amount of the
contract, which ever amount is larger. SPs will prepare and present to BGK certified Statements of
Expenditure (SOEs) to be used as the basis of replenishing these cash advances. BGK will review the
SOEs and replenishment the SP's account. Supporting documentation for SOEs would be retained by
SPs and BGK and made available for examination by staff of the MOLSP, the World Bank, and by
external auditors.
48.    Since MOF will retain ownership of its shares of micro-loans under the component, the SPs
will provide custodial services for micro-loan contracts and BGK will keep inventory, on behalf of
the MOF of such contracts at all times.
49.    Finally, the SPs will deposit or cause final borrowers to deposit the Government's share of
interest and principal payments on micro-loans under the component in an accounts established by
BGK for the purpose of implementing the component. These accounts will hold all cash related to the
component including, initial allocations from the Government, the repayments of principal, and the
interest accrued by the Government.
50.    Monitoring and Evaluation. The innovative nature of the component requires
extraordinarily (a) close monitoring on the part of the Government, and (b) frequent supervision from
the World Bank. The MOLSP will monitor and supervise SPs operations on the basis of an approved
implementation program detail in an annex of the corresponding Consultant Services Contract.
Service providers will submit monthly reports to BGK, MOLPS and to the relevant VPIU.
51.    BGK, MOLSP, the relevant VPIUs and the World Bank will conduct jointly a Component
Implementation Review on a semi-annual basis. During the first semester of implementation, these
Implementation Reviews would be carried out quarterly. These reviews would include: (a) assessment
of component implementation, (b) evaluation of the work plan for the next six months, (c) meetings
with participating SPs, rural beneficiaries of financial services, and BGK to obtain feedback on (a)
and (b) above and to agree on any required modifications to the originally proposed program. The
relevant VPIUs will inform the RSC about the results of the Implementation Review.
52.    Component implementation would be deemed successful if the following indicators are met
(a) outreach: (i) engagement of at least one SP, (ii) the establishment of at least four SP outlets in the
rural areas of the selected voivodships, (iii) disbursement of 2,700 micro-loans to previously
unemployed and micro rural entrepreneurs, (iv) disbursement of 1,500 grants to eligible beneficiaries,
(v) advisory services delivered to at least 2,000 beneficiaries, and (b) sustainability: (i) SP Subsidy
Dependence Index that has decreased every quarter and with a value of less than 80 percent after two
years of operations for each participating SP.
- 55 -



53.   The Subsidy Dependence Index is a sensitivity measure which indicates the degree of
financial sustainability of financial intermediaries. This index would be the key indicator of the
management information system used to monitor the component. The index will be periodically
calculated for each of the pilot units and for their consolidated operation. The index includes
repayments and profits.
54.   Upon completion of the component, the MOLSP will prepare an evaluation report comparing
the cost-effectiveness of the current regime of social assistance vis-a-vis the alternative approach
financed by the component as an instrument to create and maintain rural self-employment. The report
will be conducted under terms of reference and with participation of recognized experts satisfactory
to the World Bank. The following are some of the variables that will be analyzed in the report (a)
number of previously registered unemployed individuals who gained self-employment and who gave
up social security assistance after becoming component beneficiaries; (b) "survival rates" of newly
created micro-enterprises; (c) loan losses on micro-credits; and (d) administrative costs and service
fees required to manage such a program.
55.   Costs. The following table details the total cost, source of funding, and financing percentages
for the activities that will be carried out under the component.

56.   Disbursement Conditions. The Legal Agreement between the World Bank and the Government
will establish that the following conditions must be met prior to the disbursement of the portion of the
World Bank loan proceeds allocated to finance the activities under the component: (a) selection of at least
one voivodship in which the program will be implemented; (b) Legal Agreement with BGK signed; (c)
selection of at least one SP; (b) consultant services contracts and agency agreements with selected SP(s)
signed; and (d) financial management and internal control systems acceptable to the World Bank in place at
the SPs and BGK. Financing of up to US$100,000 will be available to the DSRP for eligible expenditure
for consultant services incurred after the World Bank Loan has become effective.
Project Component 2 - US$83.13 million
- 57 -



Sub-Component B-1: Labor Redeployment (US$44.84 million).
57.   Sub-component Objectives. Poland has considerable experience in using multiple measures to
assist the hidden and registered unemployed to re-enter the labor market. These programs, referred to as
active labor programs (ALPs), are operated under the 1994 Law on Employment and Counteracting
Unemployment by the Ministry of Labor and Social Policy (MOLSP) through a network of local labor
offices. The ALPs' menu includes employment services, public works, intervention works (wage subsidies)
and support to self-employment. In addition, there are passive income support programs, including
unemployment compensation available for a finite duration to unemployed workers with sufficient recent
work experience, and a means-tested general social assistance program available after exhaustion of the
unemployment benefit. However, many rural citizens do not qualify for unemployment benefits, because
they own land, and many others do not wish to apply for social assistance. The result is a cycle of hidden
unemployment and poverty.
58.   Currently ALPs are predominantly financed by the Labor Fund, but are implemented by a range of
contracted public, private, and NGO service providers. Additional programs and projects operated by
NGOs and specialized agencies are targeted to specific regions or villages or specific groups of
unemployed and, therefore, are relatively small. The labor redeployment sub-component will add value to
on-going efforts, and build on experiences gained by other related initiatives, including those financed by
the Government, and other agencies.
59.   The number of people retrained as part of the MOLPS's programs grew from about 82,000 in 1995
to about 134,000 in 1997. The figure for 1997 constitutes 7.3 percent of the unemployed. The total
government expenditure for retraining in 1996 equaled about 1.3 percent of the expenditures for all
government-sponsored ALPs, at an average unit cost of US$300 per unemployed. In total, less than 10
percent of the unemployed received retraining. Some 50 - 60 percent of the people retrained were able to
find a job, and net impact evaluations indicate these programs make a significant difference. Employment
services are also being provided throughout Poland, by private and public agencies, at a very low unit cost
of approximately US$30 per unemployed, and research consistently shows that these programs have a
positive net impact. Similarly, public works program constituted about 3.2 percent of ALP spending in
1996, at an average cost of US$800 per unemployed. This project will seek to strengthen and expand the
services of existing effective private and public agencies.
60.   This sub-component is focusing on the implementation of selected ALPs specifically targeted and
designed to assist the rural economically disadvantaged population and the unemployed. The
sub-component will build on the experience of the MOLSP, which has implemented a US$80 million
Employment Promotion and Services Bank-financed project which was directed, among other things, to
strengthening the evaluation and delivery of ALPs including retraining, small business assistance, and
employment services. The experiences under that project appear to indicate that there are special needs in
rural low income and high unemployment areas that can be addressed by targeted assistance, as can be
noted in the recent MOLSP evaluation of ALPs. The ALPs under this sub-component will also be
coordinated with other ALP services available in the local labor offices funded by government and
complemented through ALPs provided by NGOs and special agencies.
61.   Based on the experience described above, the objective of the sub-component is to assist
economically disadvantaged unemployed in rural communities to expand and use their human capital in
response to economic and labor market opportunities.
- 58 -



62.    Tasks. The sub-component will finance two main tasks: (i) economic and labor market surveys,
and (ii) labor redeployment services based on regional programs of the selected regions.
63.    Economic and Labor Market Surveys (US$0.51 million). The project will develop a mechanism
to provide (a) projections on economic trends with special regard to their impact on labor markets, (b) a
basis for forecasting registered unemployment, following changes in major indicators of enterprise
performance, and (c) better information to individuals to facilitate their career decision making, and
institutions to target investments in human capital. Key activities will include: (i) designing, (ii) pilot
testing, and (iii) implementing regular surveys in up to seven voivodships. The surveys will be based on
adaptation of similar techniques which have been used successfully in several developed and transition
economies. The sub-component will finance technical assistance and a limited provision of goods.
64.    The first activity will consist of designing a short term labor market survey by reviewing the
methodology and the results of similar surveys in other countries (e.g. Sweden, Hungary) and making site
visits, developing a general methodology for voivodship surveys including preparing the survey instrument,
the sampling techniques, the technical procedures for data collection, the interviewer selection and training,
the data processing, the data analysis, the information presentation and dissemination. The survey will
include, but will not be limited to, information on the following data from firms: (i) capacity utilization,
cause for under-utilization, plans to increase investment and capacity, orders for goods and services, net
sales, financial and property status, general opinions on the state of the economy in Poland and selected
voivodships, (ii) labor market movements by firm and branch of the economy, supply and demand in the
labor market, (iii) characteristics of changes in employment, status of hiring special groups such as school
leavers, policy regarding making staff redundant. The methodology and survey instrument will then be
presented to the project's team in MOLPS.
65.    The survey will then be pilot tested by drawing a sample from one voivodship, training the
interviewers, undertaking the survey, analyzing the data, creating a report, presenting the draft report to the
project's team in MOLPS, adjusting survey procedures on the basis of results of the pilot test and the
review of the project's team in MOLPS.
66.    Finally, regular semi-annual labor market surveys will be undertaken in the targeted voivodships
and the findings disseminated, by drawing a sample in the participating voivodships, training the
interviewers, completing the surveys, completing the data input and analysis, developing semi-annual
reports for each voivodship, presenting data to users in a public forum in each voivodship.
67.    Labor Redeployment Services (US$47.15 million). The project will finance a limited amount of
technical assistance and goods for the project team in the MOLPS. The Redeployment Fund will finance a
broad range of demand-driven "labor services", following criteria agreed with the Bank and in compliance
with programs decided by the regional self-governments. The "menu" of labor services will include:
specialized employment services, on-job and institutional training, support to temporary community
employment, small business assistance services, small business incubators and local economic development
planning grants, public awareness campaign. Services will be made available to economically
disadvantaged and unemployed citizens in rural communities, through local service providers which will be
selected on a competitive basis. Each community and region will select from the menu of services
depending on their unique needs.
68.    The project will first develop a framework to enable the Marszalek Offices to provide resources to
local labor service providers, so they can organize in-depth labor services for citizens in rural communities.
The project will finance publicity materials to inform self-government officials, citizens and potential
- 59 -



service providers regarding: (i) the availability of funds for labor services, (ii) the contracting procedures,
and (iii) the criteria to obtain and use these funds.
69.   In addition, the project will develop a system to monitor the use of funds, and evaluate the impact
of local labor redeployment services on wages and employment.
70.   Costs. The costs of implementing the sub-component are shown in table 2. They include the
recurrent costs that will be covered by the MOLPS for the personnel in the project team and in the
voivodship labor offices.
