Annex 4
POLAND: POLAND RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Economic Analysis
1.    Overview: Reducing unemployment in Poland remains a priority. The Rural Development Project
is expected to generate economic growth and employment in Poland's lagging rural areas. Together with
this main thrust, RDP also includes components to decentralize decision-making and stimulate the
functioning of community based organizations.
2.     Components for the RDP were chosen to address the priority needs based on studies done by the
World Bank ("Competitiveness and Employment. A Framework for Rural Development in Poland", Garry
Christensen and Richard Lacroix, World Bank discussion paper No. 383, 1997). Analyzing the state and
perspectives of Polish agriculture, the authors concluded that significant progress in restructuring
agriculture could be made only if positive changes occur in the rural economy. The economic environment
should be conducive for private sector investment and should lead to diversified economic growth in rural
areas, thus providing alternative jobs for farmers leaving agriculture. The authors have identified several
obstacles to private entrepreneurial activities in rural areas. The most important are: poor rural
infrastructure, low quality of labor force, insufficient support for small and medium enterprise, and low
level of rural financial intermediation. Similar conclusions can be found in papers published by Polish
academics.
3.     The Government of Poland used funds derived from the Agricultural Sector Adjustment Loan
(ASAL) to mount a large program of rural infrastructure development over 1995-1997. A rough estimate
from the ASAL experience indicates that, apart from other benefits, jobs would be created at a cost of
around US$5,000 (PLN 20,000) per job. However, jobs created cannot be only due to the investments
made under the project. ASAL experience also shows that a comprehensive approach towards economic
development has considerably greater impact on expanding the labor market than actions concentrated only
on removal of one obstacle (namely infrastructure). Gminas that prepared and followed strategies for
economic development which treated infrastructure only as one of many elements in the whole vision of
development, achieved much better results in job creation than gminas which built infrastructure without
pursuing a broader vision.
4.     Consequently, the RDP components are targeted to (i) stimulate private sector development, (ii)
raise quality of the labor force, and (iii) upgrade rural infrastructure. Selection of these components was
internally discussed in the Bank and made available to EU researchers working on accession.
5.    Methodology. The nature of the proposed project does not lend itself to a comprehensive ex-ante
cost-benefit analysis and rate of return calculation. The analytical and evaluative process for the proposed
project has been undertaken in following logical and sequential steps:
Step 1: In an effort to provide greater utility and ownership to the local community, RDP has been designed
to empower rural communities to make decisions from a wide menu of options that would meet their
priority needs for development from pooled financial resources. Demand-driven investment selection is the
first step to bottom-up decision making that has been mandated, with representation of local beneficiaries,
in all administrative bodies of the project. Proxy for utility contributed to local communities is measured by
the level of beneficiary and community ownership and financial participation, assuming that all the
beneficiaries are represented.
- 84 -



Step 2: Next evaluative process for "quality" of investment options are based on previous experiences of
similar operations or studies. It also seeks to establish selection criteria based on coherence to sector
policies, technical specification, environmental norms, financial sustainability, cost-recovery, at least
cost-effectiveness thresholds and cost-benefit where possible and the extent of benefits. Examples, where
possible, and rationale for selection are provided to help the communities during implementation. The
following paragraphs discuss each investment option with this back-drop.
6.     Micro-Credit. The essence of the economic and financial analysis of this component is a
comparison between (a) the average cost of providing social assistance to one unemployed person under the
current policy regime, taking into account the expected duration of an unemployment spell, and (b) the
estimated average cost of the alternative intervention implicit in the design of the sub-component; namely a
"package" of grants, technical assistance, and micro-loans to create new and promote existing
micro-enterprises in rural areas. The data suggest that, under rather conservative assumptions, the package
of investments and activities financed under the component may be a lower cost alternative to the
instruments currently used by the government to deal with long term rural unemployment.
7.     The main findings are (a) a survival rate of micro-enterprises created under the component above
59 percent after two years would make the proposed package more cost-effective than the current regime,
and (b) the main difficulty faced by a micro-credit outlet in achieving sustainability is the low incidence of
entrepreneurship in the country's rural areas, which implies that achieving sustainability in rural Poland
may require more time than in other environments. Details on these findings follow.
8.     The principal sources of information used in obtaining the findings above were: (i) Statistical
Yearbook of the Republic of Poland, Central Statistical Office, Warsaw, 1998; (ii) Statistical Bulletin,
Central Statistical Office, Warsaw, 1999; (iii) Labor Force Survey in Poland, Central Statistical Office,
Warsaw, 1998; (iv) Employment, Wages, and Salaries in the National Economy: First Quarter 1999,
Central Statistical Office, Warsaw, 1999; (v) Registered Unemployment in Poland: First Quarter 1999,
Central Statistical Office, Warsaw, 1999; (vi) Report on the Condition of the Small and Medium Size
Enterprise Sector in Poland, Polish Foundation for Small and Medium Enterprise Promotion and
Development, Warsaw, 1999; (vii) Poland Country Profile, Poland Country Report, and Poland Country
Risk Service, Economist Intelligence Unit, 1999; (viii) Source Materials for a Demand Assessment of
Microfinance Services in the Swietoktyskie and Zachodniopomorskie Voivodships, UNIGLOB Group,
Warsaw, 1999; (ix) financial statements of leading NGO micro-credit service providers operating in
Poland; (x) data from business plans for non-agricultural services, production, and trade microenterprise
activities in Poland; and (xi) discussions with representatives of microcredit service providers currently
operating in Poland.
9.     Cost comparison between the current regime and the activities under the component. The average
cost of one unemployed individual under the current regime of social assistance is a function of the
following variables: (a) total amount of unemployment benefits paid out, (b) total amount of social security
insurance (ZUS) for the unemployed covered by the government, (c) administrative costs of managing the
program, (d) the number of individuals who received unemployment benefits, and the (d) weighted average
duration of unemployment spells. Other relevant variables such as the disbursement profile of the social
benefit payments and the discount rates over the period of disbursement are assumed away in this
formulation for the sake of simplicity and lack of sufficient data. This simplifying assumption should not
modify the bottom line comparison between the two alternative regimes, as their costs are calculated in
nominal terms.
10.    On the basis of these variables and using 1997 data, the estimated lower bound of the (nominal)
- 85 -



average cost of the current regime is US$ 3,442.57 equivalent per unemployed individual eligible for
benefits. This lower bound estimate results from the following calculations. The average annual fiscal cost
(Table I) is simply the total annual fiscal cost of the regime (PLN 4.9 billion for 1997) divided by the
average number of individuals eligible to receive benefits during the year in reference (557,000 individuals)
multiplied by the lower bound of the weighted average of unemployment spells in Table 2 (1.41 years for
end 1998). The average cost indicated above is a lower bound, among other reasons, because 78.4 percent
of those unemployed at the end of 1998 had previous episodes of unemployment. The following tables
summarized the data used for this calculation.
Table 1. Average annual fiscal cost of current regime of social assistance to eligible unemployed (as
of end December 1997, thousand PLN)

Average annual cost per unemployed                         8.9
Table 2. Duration of current unemployment episode (percentage of total number of unemployed as of

The weighted average of the current unemployment episode is 1.41 years. In calculating this average, the
midpoints (e.g., 2 months for 1-3 month category) were used for the intermediate intervals. The values used
for the extreme categories were I month and 24 months, respectively.
11.   The analysis continues with the question of "What would be the survival rate of micro-enterprises
created under the sub-component that would equate the cost of the current regime and that of the
proposed set of activities?". Under the assumptions detailed below, the required two year survival rate is
59 percent. That is, under the assumptions below, if more than 59 percent of micro-enterprises created
under the sub-component are still in business after two years, then the proposed package would be more
cost effective to deal with rural unemployment than the current regime.
12.   The following are the variables and assumptions that determine the average cost of the proposed
package over two years of sub-component implementation: (a) eligible unemployed who receive a grant
(US$ 900) and a micro-loan (average of US$ 2,800) become registered entrepreneurs and forgo social
- 86 -



assistance; (b) entrepreneurs whose micro-enterprises perish within two years return to social assistance
costing the system the amount detailed above, regardless of the timing of the closing of the business (US$
3,442); (c) all administrative and technical assistance costs of the sub-component will be "charged" to
chronically unemployed beneficiaries, an average of US$ 512 over two years (as opposed to taking an
average that includes existing micro-enterprises). From the sub-component cost tables, administrative,
financial, and technical assistance costs for the government amount to US$ I million over two years of
sub-component implementation. The expected number of beneficiaries of the above package is 1,958 over
two years. The corresponding average cost is, thus, US$ 512 over the two years.; (d) loan losses are due to
closing of micro-enterprises and the entire principal amount and interest is lost because all micro-loans fall
in arrears immediately and principal is due in a single payment at maturity (24 months); and (e) interests on
micro-loans are accrued annually.
Letting ? be the accumulated death rate of micro-enterprises that would equate the cost of both policy
regimes, then:
Average cost of current regime = Adm. Cost + Grant + ?[loan size] - [(I -?)(loan size) 2 (1 +rate on loans)]
+ ? (cost of current regime).
Under the above simplifying assumptions, plugging the assumed values for each variable, and solving for?,
the lower bound for accumulated death rate over two years that would equate the nominal cost of the
program for the Government is 41 percent (or equivalently, a survival rate of 51 percent.).
13.    Financial Viability of a Rural Micro-credit Service Provider Retail Point. The financial viability of
a microcredit service provider retail point (SPRP) established under the Off-Farm Employment
sub-component of the Rural Development project is dependent upon the cost structure of its operations the
market-related interest rates at which it will receive and lend out funds, the delinquency rates, and the size
of the market it serves in terms of potential clients. More specifically, the degree to which an SPRP will
achieve sustainable operation depends on: (i) the design and execution of the overall microcredit scheme
(the Off-Farm Employment sub-component of the Rural Development project); (ii) the SPRP's employment
of appropriate business practices (e.g., adequate terms and conditions of microloans, screening of potential
borrowers, enforcement of loan contracts, reserves for loan losses); and (iii) the characteristics of the
particular market in which the SPRP will operate (e.g., macroeconomic indicators, prevailing interest rates,
size and makeup of the market, tax regime).
14.    A computerized financial projection model (CFPM) was used to carry out a study of the financial
viability of providing microcredit in rural areas of Poland through newly-established SPRPs operated by
non-governmental organizations (NGOs). The results of the model on the basis of the official data on
employment and entrepreneurship in rural Poland suggest that while SPRPs would significantly reduce
their overall cost of operations relative their lending interest rate, they would not achieve full operational
sustainability by the end of their second year of operations primarily because: (a) the size of the market
(number of existing micro-enterprises who are potential borrowers in the area covered by an SPRP) is too
small; (b) the risks of lending for micro-entrepreneurial start-up activities are too high (i.e., the complement
the residual of the market), and (c) the rates of interests that start-ups (the largest potential according to
official statistics) may be able and willing to pay may be lower than in most micro-finance programs.
15.    The model suggests, under rather punishing assumptions, that SPRPs may achieve sustainability
after about five years of operation, which is longer than the period observed in micro-enterprise programs
that follow best international practices. The additional time predicted by the model may be the result,
however, of strong incentives to under-report self-employment in Poland. Official data on self-employment
- 87 -



in Poland reflect only registered microenterprises and therefore may understate the true incidence of
entrepreneurship given the disincentives to register microenterprise activities. Microentrepreneurs who do
register their operations must: (i) start paying social security tax ("ZUS" tax) equal to 36.5% of their
income (and that of their employees, if any), and (ii) forego their social security entitlements.
16.   The Model. The model generates pro forma balance sheets, income statements, and 40 financial
ratios for a given financial intermediary (Fl) based on assumptions derived from official data for the
macroeconomic environment, size and makeup by client type of the financial market in the area covered by
the Fl, interest rates, financial products, loan portfolio performance, banking regulation, start-up costs, and
operating expenses. The model produces results for six month periods over a duration of five years. The
model was designed to show the relationship between design variables (e.g., financial products, incentive
schemes, initial investments) and the profitability of rural retail financial services. In the case of the Rural
Development project, the model was used specifically to determine the financial viability of a given SPRP
created under the Off-Farm Employment sub-component.
17.    The Assumptions. The purpose of this section is to describe each assumption required by the
model and to justify the particular values used for these assumptions in the base case.
1) Macroeconomic Parameters
Inflation. An initial annual inflation rate of 6%, decreasing to 4% over the next five years is assumed in the
model based on the fact that inflation in Poland has decreased steadily from 33.3% in 1994 to 11.7% in
1998 and is expected to continue to fall gradually.
GDP Growth. An annual real GDP growth rate of 5% for the next five years is assumed based on the fact
that between 1994 and 1998 this rate rose and then fell moderately from 5.3% to 4.8%, and is expected to
remain fairly constant.
Tax on Profits. It is expected that SPRPs will be exempt from paying taxes on their operational surpluses.
Size of the Market. Those individuals and entities eligible for microloans from SPRPs will be: (i)
unemployed persons in rural areas and hidden unemployed (also referred to as "overemployed") persons in
the agriculture sector seeking to start up off-farm microentrepreneurial activities. Henceforth, "unemployed
and hidden unemployed in agriculture" will be referred to simply as "unemployed"; and (ii) existing rural
off-farm microenterprises with five or less employees. Only residents of the two voivodships
(Swietokryskie and Zachodniopomorskie) covered by the Off-Farm Employment sub-component will be
eligible.
Initial Population in the Area Covered by an SPRP. It is assumed that each SPRP will initially cover
approximately six rural and "rural/urban" gminas with average populations of 9,000 each (SPRPs would
be staffed by three loan officers - including a manager - each of whom will cover two gminas). SPRPs are
therefore expected to initially cover an area containing approximately 54,000 individuals. This assumption
is consistent with the experience of microcredit service providers currently operating in rural Poland, whose
loan officers service approximately 2-4 gminas each.
Rate of Growth of the Population. The average annual growth rate of the population in the area covered by
an SPRP is assumed at 0.5%, reflecting the national rate during the years 1980-97. This rate is expected to
remain generally constant during the next five years.
- 88 -



Makeup of the Population by Client Type. Assumptions for the makeup, by client type, of the population in
the area covered by an SPRP are based on official statistical data for the two eligible voivodships. These
data show that: (i) rural unemployed persons constitute about 13% of the rural population; and (ii) there
are approximately three rural microenterprises of five or less employees for every 100 individuals in rural
areas. For the purposes of conservative estimation, the makeup of the rural population is assumed to
remain constant during the next five years, even though the transition to a market economy may lead to an
increase in entrepreneurial activity.
2) Estimation of the Demand for Loans
Potential Demand for Loans. It is assumed that 15% of all unemployed and 45% of existing
microenterprises in the area covered by an SPRP might have an effective demand for microloans (i.e.,
might be both willing and able to borrow). This assumption is based on several factors, including: (i) the
affordability of microloans at market-related interest rates as evidenced by the willingness and ability of
existing Polish microenterprises, some of which operate in rural areas, to obtain and repay (at an
approximate 95% repayment rate) microloans at 29% average annual interest while simultaneously
generating significant positive returns on investment and maintaining the productive base of their
businesses; (ii) the results of surveys conducted by the Agency for Agricultural Property of the State
Treasury, which indicate that 10% of unemployed former state-owned farm workers - regarded as the
worst-off of the unemployed - wish to become self-employed, but lack access to the necessary resources to
do so; (iii) discussions with representatives of micro and small business development organizations in the
Rural Development project's target voivodships, which affirmed the existence of demand for microcredit
for unemployed persons and existing microenterprises in the rural areas and small towns of these
voivodships; (iv) indication from a leading provider of microcredit at market-related interest rates in rural
Poland that the demand for microloans is high; and (v) the recent disbursement of 60 microloans over three
months to rural areas in Poland by another leading microcredit service provider.
Actual Coverage of Potential Borrowers. Actual coverage of potential borrowers refers to the percentage of
those unemployed and existing microenterprises that might demand loans that actually obtain loans from an
SPRP. It is assumed that initially 5% of these unemployed and microenterprises that might demand loans
will actually obtain loans from SPRPs (1% of all unemployed and 2% of all microenterprises in the area
covered by an SPRP). It is further assumed that this 5% rate of actual coverage will increase gradually to
19% over two years due primarily to: (i) increased access to lending services brought about by the physical
presence of the SPRP in the area that it covers (lower transaction costs for borrowers); and (ii) improved
delivery of lending services resulting from the development and application of improved business practices
by the SPRP through (a) technical assistance and training received via the Rural Development project, and
(b) lessons learned over time regarding lending in rural areas.
Regulation of Reserves for Loan-losses. Although SPRPs will not be required by law to maintain reserves
for loan losses, as a matter of sound business practice they will keep such reserves by classifying their
loans in five risk categories (A through E, from lowest to highest risk). Each category calls for a percentage
of the loan portfolio outstanding to be reserved for loan losses. The percentages corresponding to categories
A through E would 0%, 10%, 50%, 75%, and 100% respectively.
Accrued Interest in Arrears. The percentage of accrued interest in arrears for each risk category is
calculated as the product of the sum of the principal outstanding on loans in each risk category and a
percentage of the outstanding loan portfolio, where this percentage increases with the risk category. The
accrued interest in arrears for risk categories A through E would be 0%, 1%, 2%, 5%, and 10%
respectively.
- 89 -



Write-offs from the Loan Portfolio. It is assumed that 8% of the loan portfolio outstanding will be written
off on an annual basis. This write-off rate - higher than actual rates experienced by existing microcredit
service providers operating in rural Poland - considers the added risk of lending for start-up activities.
Write-offs reduce the reserve for loan-losses and gross loan portfolio by equal amounts. Write-offs also
affect profits by the difference between the amount written off and the expense for provisions for
loan-losses that had been required before the loan was stricken from the books.
Interest Penalties for Arrears. It is assumed that SPRPs will increase the interest rate charged on accrued
interest in arrears by 25% of the original interest rate.
Accrued Interest in Arrears that is Collected. It is assumed that 75% of accrued interest in arrears will be
collected.
Elasticity of the Average Loan Portfolio Outstanding Relative to Real GDP Growth and Inflation. The
elasticity of an SPRP's average loan portfolio outstanding considers: (i) elasticity with respect to inflation
and real GDP growth rates; and (ii) the time it takes (lag) for borrowers to adjust their loan balances with
respect to inflation and real GDP growth rates. While estimates for these elasticities are not available, it is
reasonable to assume that within three months of changes in inflation, borrowers would adjust their average
loan balances outstanding to 90% of their desired levels. Within three months of changes in real GDP
growth, microenterprises would adjust their average loan balances outstanding to 90% of their desired
levels, while unemployed would do so to 80% of their desired levels.
Terms and Conditions of Loans. It is assumed that SPRPs will issue two types of loans: short-term and
long-term.
Average Loan Balances and Terms to Maturity. The assumed average sizes of initial short- and long-term
microloans offered by SPRPs are US$ 2,491 (70% of GDP per capita) and US$ 2,847 (80% of GDP per
capita) respectively. The assumed average terms to maturity for short- and long-term loans will be 12 and
24 months respectively. These loan sizes and terms to maturity resemble those offered by the leading
providers of microcredit in Poland's rural areas.
Interest Rates and Commissions. The assumed interest rates on SPRP microloans are 14% for short-term
loans and 16% for long-term loans. These rates are market-related and consistent, on average, with rates
currently charged by microcredit service providers operating in Poland, several of which operate in rural
areas. In fact, these assumed rates appear well below affordable levels when compared with data which
suggest that existing Polish microenterprises, some of which operate in rural areas, are willing and able to
pay average annual interest rates of 29% on microloans while simultaneously generating significant profits
and maintaining the productive base of their businesses. The rates assumed in the model are below these
observed rates because one of the objectives of the Off-Farm Employment sub-component is to induce
currently unemployed to forego the comforts of social assistance and assume the risks of starting up
microentrepreneurial operations.
It is assumed that commissions of 1.5% and 2.5% will be charged on short- and long-term loans
respectively. These rates are consistent with the current practice of existing microcredit service providers
operating in rural areas of Poland.
Quality of the Portfolio. It is assumed that microloans issued by SPRPs will be classified in five risk
- 90 -



categories (A through E, from lowest to highest) according to the repayment capacity and experience of
borrowers. Based on international experience, for microenterprises it is assumed that categories A through
E will contain 70%, 20%, 6%, 3%, and 1%, respectively, of the loan portfolio outstanding. For
unemployed, these categories will contain 55%, 25%, 11%, 6%, and 3%, respectively, of the loan portfolio
outstanding. As mentioned previously, the percentage of the loan portfolio outstanding in each category that
will be reserved for loan losses would be 0%, 10%, 50%, 75%, and 100% for categories A through E
respectively.
3) Interest Rate on Project Funds Received by SPRPs for Lending to Final Beneficiaries. Financial
Investments, and Equity
Interest Rate on Project Funds Received by SPRPs for Lending to Final Beneficiaries. SPRPs will not
borrow project funds for on-lending per se. Rather, they will lend these funds to final beneficiaries on
behalf of the Government. Interest earned on these funds will be returned to the Government. While the
Government will ultimately determine the interest rate on project funds, this rate is assumed at 13%
(12-month T-Bill rate plus 1%) for the purposes of the model.
Equity and Retained Earnings. SPRPs will share the credit risk with the Government by providing, out of
their own resources, approximately 25% of every microloan disbursed. Based on projections for the
average amount, over two years, of project funds received by an SPRP for lending to final beneficiaries
(about US$ 1.9 million), it is estimated that each SPRP would provide approximately US$ 414,000 in
paid-in capital. It is assumed that any operational surplus generated by an SPRP will be invested in
short-term securities.
Financial Investments. It is assumed that any excess cash held by SPRPs will be invested in short-term
securities at the 12-month T-Bill rate (currently 12%) plus 1%.
4) Initial Investment
The initial cash outlay for establishing an SPRP is assumed at US$ 7,690, and includes equipment (US$
4,800), furniture (US$ 790), organizational costs (US$ 1,400), and other investments (US$ 700). The
largest expenditures are computers (US$ 2,000) and staff training (US$ 900). The initial investment is
recognized as an expense over time as the assets acquired are depreciated and the organization cost is
amortized. These figures, as well as those for administrative and personnel expenses below, are based on
actual figures experienced by several leading microcredit service providers currently operating in Poland.
5) Administrative Expenses
The monthly administrative expenses of an SPRP are estimated at US$ 1,075, and include primarily rent
(US$ 250), supplies (US$ 250), telecommunications and postage (US$ 200), and community relations
(US$ 250). Miscellaneous expenses are estimated at 5% of administrative expenses. These expenses adjust
monthly for inflation with an elasticity of 90%.
6) Personnel Expenses and the Productivity of Employees
Salary and Incentives. It is assumed that SPRP employees will earn a base salary plus a performance bonus
based on profits generated by the SPRP.
Performance Bonuses. It is assumed that the employees will receive performance bonuses equal to 2% of
- 91 -



profits generated by the SPRP.
Social Security Benefits. SPRPs will be required by law to pay social security tax ("ZUS" tax) equal to
36.5% of employees' monthly base salary.
Recruitment and Training of New Employees. The non-salary cost of recruiting and training additional
staff is estimated at US$ 150 per new employee.
Salaries and Number of Employees. It is assumed that an SPRP will open with three full-time employees:
one manager and two loan officers. An accountant would be hired part-time or the cost of an accountant
will be prorated to the SPRP's headquarters. The monthly base salary for the manager, loan officers, and
accountant would be US$ 500, US$ 450, and US$ 400 respectively.
Growth of Salaries. Nominal salaries are expected to adjust with respect to inflation every six months with
an elasticity of 85%.
Productivity of Employees. The need to hire additional staff is determined by the productivity of the loan
officers. It is assumed that loan officers could handle an average of 150 microloans each. This figure is
based on the experience of several microcredit service providers currently operating in Poland.
7) Discount Rate for Future Cash Flows
The base case discounted future cash flows are assumed at 15%, 30%, and 60%.
8) Self-sustainability of a Microcredit Service Provider Retail Point
Introduction. The operational self-sustainability of a microcredit service provider retail point (SPRP)
established under the Rural Development project is determined by whether or not its overall cost of
operations is covered by interest income. An SPRP which is not self-sustainable may, in principle, become
self-sustainable by: (i) controlling the overall cost of operations; and (ii) charging adequate lending rates.
Cost of operations as a percentage of the loan portfolio. The components of the overall cost of operations
of an SPRP are: (i) cost of funds (cost of project funds lent by the SPRP to final beneficiaries and cost of
equity); (ii) expense for provision for loan losses; (iii) operating expense (administrative expense, personnel
expense, depreciation and amortization, and miscellaneous expense); and (iv) performance bonuses for
personnel. Under reasonable assumptions made based on official data, each component of the cost of
operations as a percentage of the total loan portfolio outstanding of an SPRP was projected. The largest
components of the overall cost of operations are, in descending order, expense for provision for loan losses,
cost of funds, and operating expense. Of these, expense for provision for loan losses and operating
expenses are the components over which the SPRP may exercise the greatest measure of control.
Controlling the cost of operations. While it is expected that SPRPs will experience a decline in their overall
cost of operations in the short-term, they may not achieve full operational self-sustainability by the end of
their second year of operations due primarily to persistent relatively high levels of loan losses. Controlling
the overall cost of operations would be achieved through the employment of sound business practices.
SPRPs would develop improved methodologies for lending in rural areas through: (i) technical assistance
and training received under the Rural Development project regarding rural microlending best practices; and
(ii) lessons learned over time from lending in the particular rural areas where they operate.
- 92 -



Increasing the lending interest rate. The official data suggest that the assumed 16% annual lending interest
rate charged by SPRPs may not fully cover the cost of operations by the end of their second year of
operations. Further analysis suggests, however, that the cost of operations would be covered if this lending
rate were increased to at least 20.5%, ceteris paribus. It is expected that this higher rate would be feasible
in light of: (i) similar rates charged by the leading provider of microcredit to rural areas of Poland; and (ii)
data from actual business plans on a series of non-agricultural services, production, and trade
microenterprise activities which suggest the affordability of microloans at higher rates still.
Conclusion. Under reasonable assumptions made based on official data, this analysis suggests that SPRPs
established under the Rural Development project will experience a decline in their overall cost of operations
relative their lending interest rates in the short-term. However, SPRPs may not achieve full operational
self-sustainability by the end of two years of operations. To achieve self-sustainability, SPRPs should: (i)
further reduce their overall cost of operations (expense for provision for loan losses in particular) through
adopting improved business practices; and (ii) increase their lending rates if possible (see working papers
for details).
18.    Labor Redeployment. This sub-component includes a small activity to help identify short and
medium term economic trends and labor requirements via local surveys, and a major Labor Redeployment
Fund to support development of needed human capital (labor). The sub-component has both an economic
and social rational. Government support to mitigate the social impacts of economic restructuring and
reform in rural areas is in this sense a cost of building political support for the county's economic reform
program. From an economic perspective retention of surplus labor in state or private enterprises lowers
productivity, increases enterprise losses, impedes enterprise restructuring, delays the potential reallocation
of labor to productive alternative employment, and consumes state budget resources in the form of direct or
indirect subsidies. Workers in loss-making majority state owned enterprises receive direct and indirect
public subsidies in the form of subsidized wages, other subsidized inputs, subsidies to the banking system,
and inter-enterprise arrears. The total cost of these subsidies typically exceeds by a significant margin the
combined cost of unemployment benefits, social assistance, and targeted labor redeployment measures.
19.    The basic economic rationale is that moving workers, via labor redeployment programs, from jobs
where they are not productive and receive public subsidies to jobs where they are productive and do not
receive public subsidies reduces public budget outlays and increases GDP. At the same time, GDP will
increase to the extent that the jobs filled by redeployed workers are new jobs or higher productivity jobs
than participants held previously. The Labor Redeployment Fund finances specific services to: (a) improve
labor mobility and addressfrictional unemployment; (b) enhance and change human capital, via retraining,
to address structural unemployment and improve productivity; and (c) address the lack of demandfor
labor including support to individuals to help them start and maintain micro and small businesses, and to
communities to implement temporary community employment schemes and design local economic
development plans.
20.    While it is very difficult to evaluate the economic impact of the small labor survey component, the
gross and net impact of the labor services financed by the Labor Redeployment Fund can be quantified to
some degree, with the exception of local economic development planning grants for which the impact is
long term and difficult to evaluate within the project time frame. A recent World Bank quasi experimental
design study "Evaluating the Impact ofActive Labor Programs: Results of Cross Country Studies in
Europe and Central Asia ", World Bank Discussion Paper ,June, 1999, found that labor redeployment
services can have a positive net impact on employment, wages, the use duration of income support
payments (i.e. unemployment benefits). The impact varies according to program design and demographics
- 93 -



of participants. This four countries study included Poland and was implemented by the Ministry of Labor
and Social Policy. The information from this has and will continue to be used to design services with the
greatest economic and social impact with different target groups and will be presented to all voivodships
participating in the project prior to use of project funds.
21.    Selection Criteria for the Labor Redeployment sub-component. LRF funds would be available in
rural and urban/rural gminas in each voivodship, as agreed with the Bank. Investments by voivodship
would be based on an algorithm based on the percentage share of rural population for the participating
gminas. There would not be any pre-allocation by powiat or gmina within voivodships, as use of resources
will be by demand and need. All services financed by the component will be continually monitored to
determine their impact on post-program employment and wages, and adjustments will be made during
implementation to improve impact.
22.    The project provides for monitoring of the status of program participants relative to their
post-program employment status, earnings, and receipt of continued public income support payments. It is
anticipated that the gross impact of the services will equal or exceed those found in the previously
mentioned study and that post program employment will be a minimum of 15% after receipt of employment
services, 50% for participants in retraining, 10% of employment in temporary community employment
programs, 5% of those who make initial contact for assistance in starting small businesses will actually
start businesses, and 90% of those entering business incubators will continue to operate a small business at
the end of one year.
23.    Education. Rehabilitation of school infrastructure and development of a school transportation
system in rural gminas will promote the consolidation of the current school network leading, on the one
hand, to increased efficiency in the utilization of resources by increasing student:teacher and
student:classroom ratios. On the other hand, it will bring savings into the system by evolving a school
network that is more energy efficient and is better utilized.
24.   Improvement in the quality of education will reduce repetition and dropout ratios. As a
consequence of this more, students will graduate and it will take less time and resources per graduate, thus
reducing the average cost per graduate. On the other hand, quality improvements will provide graduates
with better and more relevant knowledge and skills, which in turn will increase their potential to be
employed, productive and trainable.
25.   Lessons from Education operations/study. During project preparation, a group of consultants
hired by the World Bank and the seven voivodships (Marszalek Offices) conducted an assessment of
education needs in eligible gminas. As a result of this assessment, over 84 percent of eligible rural and
mixed urban-rural gminas submitted applications for support by the RDP. The interest expressed by
gminas as reflected by the number of application presented in every single intervention area is beyond the
initial expectations. For example, in the area of teacher training gminas expressed the need for training
132,253 participants, including 46,015 participants in information technology training. Gminas applied for
support to purchase a total of 68,650 units of educational materials, out of which 18,023 are books for
rural school libraries. The data show that as a result of education reform and changes in the school
network, the number of student who require transportation to schools will grow by 47 percent, i.e. by
57,681 students in the seven regions. Twenty eight percent of applications concerning school infrastructure
were related to school rehabilitation. This is an indication that the most urgent need in this area, as
expressed by gminas, is for supporting gminas effort to upgrade its current school infrastructure
26.    Selection Criteria for Education Proiects. Distribution of resources by voivodship will be done
- 94 -



based on an allocation algorithm that combines three indices. The first index takes into account the number
of primary and gymnasium level student-age population of rural gminas. The second index will be an
indicator of resource utilization at the school level, which will be estimated using the gmina student:teacher
ratio. The third indicator will measure school overcrowding, which will be estimated by using the average
square meter of school facilities per student-age population. Each of the above indices will have a weight
equal to 33.33% of the total
27.    The evaluation of education projects presented by gminas will be a two stage process. During stage
one, the Regional Steering Committee will screen sub-projects to assess if they are eligible for financing
under the RDP. Under stage two, sub-projects will be ranked based on a set of six criteria. Those
sub-projects with the highest ranking will be selected for financing. Gminas presenting more than one
sub-project proposal in a single area covered by the education component of the RDP should present their
proposals ranked according to their own internal priorities. Priorities will be given to the highest ranked
proposals.
Stage I Eligibility Criteria
1.     Proposed project to benefit primary schools and gymnasia located in rural areas within the
voivodships participating in the RDP
2.     Project proposal addresses needs in the areas of: (a) training of teachers, school directors, school
network administrators; (b) education materials and school equipment; (c) education software; (d)
rehabilitation of school infrastructure; and (e) school transportation
3.     Project proposal is in compliance with the objective of the RDP
4.     Project proposal is submitted by eligible gmina
Stage II General Evaluation Criteria
1.    The gmina has an education improvement plan with: clear definition of short and long term
education objectives; good assessment of school infrastructure and school transportation need based on
demographic and school infrastructure data; good assessment of teacher training needs based on an analysis
of teachers' training needs, professional profile and expertise; and good assessment of needs for
instructional materials based on school inventory
2.     The proposed project addresses one or more of the needs identified in the education improvement
plan
3.     The proposed project was developed in consultation with local stakeholders and has full support of
the gmina council
4.     Comparison of the assessment of needs done by the gmina with standards defined by MONE shows
that there is a deficit that needs to be covered by the project
5.    In presenting its proposals the gmina is able to show that it has analyzed different alternatives and
that the proposed intervention is the most "cost-effective" solution to the problem at hand
6.     Project proposals that contain additional co-financing by gminas (than the standard one established
by the project) receive additional points.

28.    The project provides for monitoring of the status of teachers relative to their post-program
employment status, earnings, performance standards and receipt of continued public income support
payments if they are currently unemployed. It is anticipated that the gross impact of the services will equal
or exceed those found in the pilot programs of the Ministry of Education, and will result in greater cost
savings than without project practice (actual numbers are being assessed). The pilot is being evaluated by
independent evaluators. Based on the latest information, the teachers trained by the program have expressed
their satisfaction with the contents and method of training. The pilot training program has raised the level
of teachers' qualifications thus training them to teach new school courses and remain in the profession by
qualifying them for future accreditation system to be implemented.
29.    Institution building in local and regional administration. The proposed sub-component will
provide a number of benefits. The project will assist in output recovery and sustained economic growth
through policy actions that promote among other things private investments. It will increase the levels of
efficiency and effectiveness of public services by decentralizing decision making, implementation, and
monitoring of programs and investments. Data are not adequate to allow a precise project cost-benefit or
cost-effectiveness analysis. Hence a cost-recovery analysis was undertaken.
30.    Lessons Learned from similar operations in Poland. USAID and the British Know-How Fund
(BKHF) have financed several training programs for self-government, some of which are still on-going.
This component will utilize the experience gained under these programs, and where possible, build on the
results achieved. The main lessons learned by USAID and BKHF are (i) training courses and materials
need to take into account the specific structure and conditions of administrative units operating in remote
rural areas, and (ii) in as much as possible, training should be provided as part of overall institutional
development programs.
31.    In the near future, the most active donor in this realm will be the EU-Phare program, which has
embarked on a series of projects with the aim to strengthen Regional Development capacity in the office of
the Marszalek in a number of voivodships. Self-governments receive training in tourism development and
procedures for accessing pre-accession (SAPARD) and structural funds. The implementation of this
- 96 -



component will therefore aim for a close co-operation with these other donors, especially EU-Phare, to
avoid duplication and achieve mutual strengthening.
32.    Selection Criteria. The Institutional Development Programs (IDP) will be implemented in selected
administrative units. The most important overall criteria for selecting any administrative unit is the degree
of commitment of board members and senior officials, as well as staff, to aim for significant performance
improvements in these units. Other criteria for the selection of pilot units are listed in the operational
manual.
33.    All the other rural administrative units operating in the project areas can choose to send elected
officials and staff to RDP training courses. Since demand for training is expected to exceed by far the
number of training spots available, training requests will be prioritized according to training needs,
willingness to participate in training costs, chances of people trained to apply their new skills and similar
criteria. The IDP investments will focus on the few administrative units to be chosen in two voivodships.
For the overall training it is expected that all seven project voivodships will receive roughly equal shares.
34.    Cost Recovery Analysis. Benefits: This sub-component will provide economic benefits through
increased efficiency of Public Administration in the pilot administrative units and the other beneficiaries,
i.e. units that participate in the training program. Through the Institutional Development Program (IDP),
in the pilot areas, efficiency gains will especially be realized through: (i) an improved organization
structure; (ii) better financial and project management, cutting costs where possible; (iii) out-sourcing
services to the private sector; (iv) the overall introduction of good government, simplification of local
regulations and procedures, 'prudent shopping' practices etc. In the overall Training Program efficiency
gains will be to a larger extent dependent on the extent to which the participating units are willing to apply
the techniques learnt in their own organization. The selected approach however maximizes the likelihood
that the participating bodies will receive relevant and applicable expertise, and will be motivated to apply
the knowledge obtained. The availability of investment funds from other components of the RDP, as well as
from the EU (pre-accession and at a later stage structural funds) will be a strong motivating factor for all
participating units to apply the techniques learnt (see below). Other non-quantifiable benefits, such as
transparency of decision-making, improved public participation in decision making, client-oriented service
delivery etc. will be assessed through the beneficiary and the client survey towards the end of the project.
For this analysis, the IDP is expected to cover two voivodships, four powiats and fourteen gminas, whereas
the general training is expected to reach some 3,000 participants working in about 200 administrative units.
35.    Costs: Investment costs to the amount of US$ 10.1 million, as well as the costs of the Project
Management Unit, have been allocated over the years 1999, 2000 and 2001. Furthermore it is supposed
that, in order to retain the newly trained staff within the administrative units, per unit participating in the
IDP on average US$ 45,000 annually would have to be spent additionally on remuneration, which has been
rounded off at US$ 1,000,000. This represent a net overall wage increase of some 33%, which however is
justified considering the new responsibilities and value added of the trained staff in the administrative units.
USD 200,000 annually (about 10% of investment expenditure) is foreseen for O&M of the equipment
provided.
36.    Data were lacking for empirical cost-benefit analysis. Instead, a cost-recovery calculation analysis
was performed, in which the efficiency gains have been expressed as percentages of the 1999 annual budget
of a voivodship (annual budget US$ 40 million), powiat (annual budget US$ 7 million) and gmina (annual
budget US$ 1.5 million). For gmina and Marszalek's office, including the Sejmik (provicial assembly),
examples were used from the Kujawsko-Pomorskie voivodship. This voivodship will be covered by the
RDP, and is representative for the seven voivodships in the project. For the powiat level, a country wide
- 97 -



average was taken, which, considering the small deviation in the population, is also representative. It was
assumed efficiency gains would amount to 2% of the budget including the participants in the general
training program. Full development was expected in year seven. Over a period of analysis of 15 years, the
Internal Rate of Return (IRR) amounts to about 39%, and the Net Present Value (NPV) at 12% discount
rate is about USD 19 million.
37.    These efficiency gains are an extremely conservative estimates, considering that this project
sub-component will result in a higher value of services delivered by self-governments, and facilitates access
to the other components (e.g. physical infrastructure, rural education and others). In addition, the
component will contribute to making the units eligible for financial support from EU pre-accession funds
(e.g. SAPARD) or, at a later stage, European structural funds. Several EU-Phare programs endeavor to
improve the absorption capacity for EU funds through strengthening public administration, but so far these
efforts have been focused on a limited number of units, and mainly at the voivodship level.
38.    A sensitivity analysis was performed for the number of participating units, which showed that with
one participating voivodship, two participating powiats and seven participating gminas, and reaching out
with general training to 100 units only, the IRR would be about 13% and the NPV US$ 0.5 million.
39.    Infrastructure. Construction of infrastructure is one of the major factors in the economic
stimulation of rural areas especially in regions where insufficient infrastructure is the most limiting factor
for entrepreneurial activity.
40.    Lessons learned from similar operations in Poland. A rough estimate from the ASAL experience
indicates that rural infrastructure investments realized considerable potential for permanent and relatively
inexpensive generation of sustainable jobs directly connected with the construction and operation of the
infrastructure and indirectly resulting from private investment. Stimulation of rural areas through
infrastructure projects should be based on strategic plans of economic development in the municipalities. It
is necessary to supplement the extension of infrastructure with other elements like education, stimulation of
small businesses (supplying them with information, legal assistance, joint promotion, business incubators),
adaptation of local regulations (local physical and land use planning or zoning, currently implemented in
many municipalities) to stimulate small businesses, the involvement of private business in the development
of the area (privatization, joint public and private projects). Infrastructure projects will be mostly located in
areas with relatively high concentration of population.
41.    Selection Criteria. Quality review for investment proposals is being performed by the local office
of ARMA against technical, economic, financial and institutional requirements. Clear rules for qualifying
investment sub-projects include a financial and economic justification based on models prepared by
Agrisystems Inc. and financed by the British Know How Fund. Investments will only be approved if they
generate a nominal EIRR higher than 10 percent (see Table 4). These models for the financial and
economic analysis of small infrastructure investments are incorporated in the operational manual. In
addition, consultants will provide hands-on training for the local offices of ARMA, VPIUs, Departments of
Regional Development and gminas during implementation. As an example, the model for rural roads is
attached in Appendix 2-1. Models for other infrastructure investments are included in the operational
manual.

42.    The RSCs rank the sub-projects following a set of criteria: (i) importance for the broader regional
economic development plan; (ii) potential for direct and indirect job creation; (iii) joint proposals among
gminas which reflect the principle of economy of scale in project design; (iv) contributions of
self-government to investment cost; and (v) economic rate of return.
43.    Project Risks. The principal project risks relate to: (a) the capacity of the local governments to
implement the proposed program; and (b) the willingness and ability of communities to collaborate in
planning, prioritizing, monitoring, implementing and funding development plans. The project has made
sufficient provision to cope with the above risks in the institutional sub-component of the project.
44.    Fiscal Impact. Fiscal costs of unemployment were analyzed to determine the range of benefits
arising from employment generation. The analysis was performed per person moving from unemployed
ranks to employment and on 1997 Central Statistical Office and government data. Future expectations are
therefore legitimate only under the assumption of non significant changes in the conditions which have been
examined.
45.    Budget cost of unemployment as in Table 2 consists of three major elements: (i) unemployment
benefit, (ii) contribution to health schemes, and (iii) administrative costs of managing the unemployment
scheme. Annual cost of unemployment benefit per person for an average unemployment period in 1997 is
about US$ 3,442. This calculation takes into account time limitation in receiving the benefit. Each
unemployed is entitled to free health care. Additionally PLN 100 are the operational and maintenance costs
of the unemployment scheme. This analysis indicates that annual fiscal savings from converting
unemployed to employed per person would be about PLN 2,700 (US$ 825). However, there is one
exception: the administrative costs of managing the unemployment scheme will decline less than
proportionally - the assumption is that reducing the rate of unemployment by half will diminish this cost by
30%.
46.    Employment of one previously unemployed person will also generate additional fiscal benefits to
the budget in a form of income tax and higher revenue from Value Added Tax. Estimation of these benefits
was performed under two assumptions (i) minimum salary; and (ii) average salary. It is difficult to estimate
a priori the level of salary, which will be received by newly employed people under the project. Based on
arguments that the level of professional skills of unemployed persons is usually low and wages in rural
areas are lower than in urban, table 5 covers only the lower half range of Polish wages.

Appendix 2-1 ROAD INVESTMENT EVALUATION MODEL
Explanatory Notes.
1.    The Scope of the Model.
The purpose of the Road Investment Evaluation Model is to provide the central authorities with criteria for
allocating funds amongst competing rural road improvement projects. It is important, therefore, that the
economic evaluations prepared by different gminas for their respective improvement proposals are
consistent, one with another, even at the cost of some minor loss of accuracy in particular cases. For this
reason, it is recommended that as much of the basic working data as is realistically possible be provided to
the gminas by the central authorities to ensure comparable results and avoid deliberate inflation of benefits.
It is also recommended that the model be kept as simple as possible to facilitate its use at the gmina level.
In this model, incorporating a 20 year project evaluation period from 2000 to 2020, the potential costs of
the project are the construction costs of the improved road and subsequent maintenance costs (In many
cases the maintenance costs would be less than those which would be incurred without the project: for this
reason net maintenance costs are shown as a positive or negative item on the benefits side of the evaluation
table).
The corresponding potential benefits are the maintenance cost savings, mentioned above, and the savings in
vehicle operating costs (VOCs) for normal and generated traffic. In some rural road evaluations, the net
value added of induced agricultural production is estimated directly as a project benefit. This approach,
however, tends to be complex, subjective and arbitrary for want of reliable data. For reasons of simplicity
and consistency, therefore, the model takes, conservatively, the operating cost benefits of the additional
traffic generated by the induced agricultural activity as representative of the net value of the increased
production. A further theoretical benefit would be the residual value of the investment at the end of the
evaluation period. Given the effect of discounting over lengthy 20 year project period, however, residual
values would, in fact, be negligible and so have not been provided for in the model: they could be
incorporated if required.
By comparing the estimated costs and benefits of the project in question, the model produces a stream of
net benefits and calculates the corresponding economic internal rate of return (EIRR) and net present value
(NPV). The NPV is calculated on the basis of a discount rate estimated to reflect the current opportunity
cost of capital in Poland. The model is formulated in terms of US dollars but can be adapted for any
currency.
The model provides for the economic evaluation of rural road projects. As the investment yields no
corresponding stream of revenues, as in the case of public utilities where consumers pay directly for the
services provided, and as "operating costs" are limited to standard road maintenance, with the costs of
vehicle operation being borne by users themselves, there is no corresponding cash flow, balance sheet or
profit & loss account. In this circumstance, no meaningful financial rate of return on the road investment
can be estimated so no corresponding financial model has been formulated.
2.     The Structure of the Model.
a)     Road Conditions.
- 102 -



The model provides for the evaluation of rural road improvement projects under six sets of conditions:
upgrading to new bitumen standard from either a gravel or a degraded bitumen standard, in either flat,
undulating or mountainous terrain. Each of these six circumstances would require the evaluator to use an
appropriate variant of the mode]. They could, at the price of some complexity and increased risk of error or
misunderstanding, all be incorporated into a single comprehensive model. It is felt, however, that, in the
circumstances of the PIP, it would be simpler and safer for the evaluator to select the model variant as
appropriate from the following:
I.     Upgrade from gravel to new bitumen in flat terrain.
2.     Upgrade from gravel to new bitumen in undulating terrain.
3.     Upgrade from gravel to new bitumen in mountainous terrain.
4.     Upgrade from degraded to new bitumen in flat terrain.
5.     Upgrade from degraded to new bitumen in undulating terrain.
6.     Upgrade from degraded to new bitumen in mountainous terrain.
These are broad definitions as, obviously, old bitumen roads could be degraded to different degrees or
undulations vary in severity. But it is considered that some standardization is desirable to achieve
comparable conclusions. If practicable, it would be better for the central authorities to decree the standard
of terrain for a particular road or area to ensure consistency between evaluations from different regions.
Additional road conditions could be included in the model if necessary.
Each evaluation would be carried out for a road of specified length. No provision is made in the current
version of the model for changes in length as a result of the upgrading. Such a refinement could be
incorporated if required, again at the cost of increased complexity.
The name, length, type and terrain of each road evaluated would be entered at the head of the model input
& evaluation table as shown in the annex.
b)     Vehicle Operating Costs.
The model assumes that all vehicles using the road could be classified into one of four categories: cars,
pick-ups, medium trucks or heavy trucks. Other categories, such as buses, could be added as necessary. It
is recommended that the VOCs for each vehicle category on each type and condition of road together with
the corresponding potential VOC savings be estimated by the central authorities and provided to the gminas
as standard model input. The estimated standard VOCs would be entered into the model with the
corresponding savings calculated automatically as shown in the attached input table.
As traffic trends and prospects are likely to vary widely in the case of individual roads, it would not be
practicable to provide any standard central input for traffic forecasts. These would have to be carried out
and put into the model by the gminas themselves, in terms of vehicles per day (vpd), on the basis of local
knowledge and observations. Some general guidelines for traffic forecasting could, however, be supplied by
the central authorities. Traffic should be forecast in terms of the four vehicle categories distinguishing
between normal traffic (that which would have materialised even without the upgrading) and generated
traffic (that which would be encouraged to use the road as a result of the improvements or the induced
economic activity of the area served). Account should be taken of the estimated construction period of the
road, allowing for a gradual build-up of generated traffic over this period. If the improvements had the
effect of diverting traffic from other routes, it would be possible to introduce a further category for diverted
traffic but it is not considered that the probability or data availability would be sufficient in the present case
to justify such a refinement: it is recommended that such traffic be classed as generated traffic. Traffic
- 103 -



forecasts should be entered into the model as shown in the input table; the model would automatically
calculate the totals for normal, generated and total traffic.
The corresponding annual VOCs, reflecting the type of the original road, its length and terrain traversed,
are calculated automatically by the model from the above data inputs. The estimates for each vehicle type
and in total, distinguishing between normal and generated traffic, would be as shown in the input table.
The respective total columns for the generated and normal traffic are input automatically into the final
evaluation table as shown. The total traffic column is for the evaluator's information only.
c)     Road Maintenance Costs.
As with VOCs, it would be possible for the central authorities to estimate unit maintenance costs for the
different types and terrains of rural road for distribution to the gminas as standard model inputs. However,
in view of probable wide local variations in labour costs, availability of materials, etc. it is not considered
that road maintenance costs are as amenable as VOCs to standardized treatment. The model assumes,
therefore, that the maintenance cost estimates would be input by the gminas themselves as shown in the
attached input table. Again, central guidelines could be provided. If thought desirable in the interests of
consistency, centrally estimated recurrent and periodic unit costs and corresponding phasing for the new
bitumen roads, where local variations would be less, could be incorporated into the model. The estimation
of the "without project" maintenance costs should remain a matter for the gminas.
The model calculates the stream of total maintenance savings automatically and transfers it directly to the
final evaluation table.
d)     Road Construction Costs.
The model assumes that the estimated road construction costs will be input directly by the gmina. It
provides for a construction period of up to two years, as this is considered to be adequate for rural roads
under the PIP, but a longer potential construction period could be incorporated. As no further processing of
the construction cost estimates is required before the final evaluation, the figures are input directly into the
final evaluation table as shown.
Again, standardized unit construction costs for the different terrains could be provided, but these may not
be sufficiently responsive to local variations. Central support should, therefore, probably be limited to
general guidelines.
For reasons explained in Section 1 above, the residual value of the investment at the end of the evaluation
period is not taken into account in the model.
e)     Economic Evaluation.
The economic return on the road investment in question is calculated automatically without further input
into the final evaluation table, on the basis of the data previously entered. The return is calculated in terms
of the economic internal rate of return (EIRR) and the net present value (NPV) with a discount rate
estimated to reflect the current opportunity cost of capital in Poland. As noted above a separate variant of
the model is used for each of the six possible combinations of road type and terrain.
Sensitivity testing of the returns can be easily applied by changing the appropriate variables in the
respective tables, e.g. the vpd estimates in the traffic forecast table or the construction cost in the final
- 104-



evaluation table.
The EIRRs and NPVs of the respective evaluations would provide the central authorities with initial
criteria for the allocation of funds amongst the competing road projects. Final decisions may take into
account extraneous factors outside the scope of this simple economic evaluation model.
3.     Working Example of the Model.
a)     Road Conditions.
The functioning of the model is demonstrated by applying it to a hypothetical road project, Abc - Xyz, to
produce the attached input & evaluation table. It is assumed that the existing road is a 12 km gravel road
traversing flat terrain. These characteristics are entered into the cells indicated at the head of Sheet I of the
model. Care must be taken to enter the road length into the correct cell as the final evaluation calculation
draws on this figure as a data input. The "Type" and "Terrain" entries are for information, indicating which
of the six possible model variants is being used, but do not otherwise affect the functioning of the model.
b)     Vehicle Operating Costs.
The standard VOCs, which would be centrally provided, are based on rough orders of magnitude in this
example and are shown on Sheet 1. For the hypothetical Abc - Xyz case, the model variant relating to
gravel roads in flat terrain would be used, with calculations derived from VOCs, with and without the
project, for each of the four vehicle types on flat, gravel roads. It is assumed, in the standardized estimates,
that, for all types of road, existing and new, they would be 10%, 20%, 30% and 40% higher respectively
for undulating terrain, with the same percentage increases applied to the resulting figures for mountainous
terrain. (These percentage variations have not been built-in to the model but would be calculated manually
by the central authorities: automatic calculation could, if required, be incorporated). Corresponding
savings for the two types of existing road are calculated automatically in the standardized data, the gravel
alternative being the relevant one in this example.
Regarding the traffic forecasts, vpds for each of the four vehicle types has been arbitrarily estimated for
2001 in this example as it is assumed that there would be no VOC savings for 2000. For purposes of
simplicity, it is assumed that subsequent annual growth would be 3.5% for all normal traffic and 6.0% for
all generated traffic throughout the evaluation period. In reality the forecast pattern of traffic growth is
likely to be more complex than this. It is assumed that the potential generated traffic would be phased in
gradually over the first three years of the project period, proportionately to the length of new road
completed. The totals for normal, generated and all traffic are calculated automatically. It is assumed, in
accordance with standard theory, that the potential VOC benefits to generated traffic would be 50% of
those to corresponding normal traffic.
The corresponding unit VOC savings, grossed-up to an annual basis for the entire road length, are
calculated automatically by the model at the end of Sheet 1. The benefits are assumed to commence in
2001 since, as there would, theoretically, be some VOC benefits on the partially completed road in 2000,
these are likely to be offset by delays caused by construction works.
c)     Road Maintenance Costs.
Road maintenance costs are estimated on Sheet 2 of the input table. It is assumed, again somewhat
arbitrarily, that maintenance costs in 2000 on the existing road without the project would increase at a
- 105 -



constant annual rate of 2.5% throughout the evaluation period. In reality, costs would probably begin to
rise exponentially to a point where some rehabilitation works could no longer be avoided, but a simplified
approach, similar to the above, should be adequate for the PIP evaluations. More realistically, the
recurrent maintenance costs of the new road are estimated to remain constant throughout with periodic
maintenance every fifth year. The resulting savings, calculated automatically, allow for a partial
realization of the benefits during the first two years of road construction.
d)    Road Construction Costs.
It is assumed that the 12 km road will be constructed over a. two year period with two thirds of the work
being completed in the first year. (To illustrate the negligible influence of residual value in the adopted
evaluation approach, it should be noted that, even if the new road investment were estimated to have a
working life of 30 years, the effect of discounting at the project rate of 12% over the 20 year evaluation
period would reduce its residual value to less than 4% of the original capital outlay, raising the estimated
EIRR insignificantly from 20.0% to 20.2%).
e)    Economic Evaluation.
The model automatically estimates the EIRR and NPV of the hypothetical Abc - Xyz road project on Sheet
3. The NPV is estimated using a 12% discount rate assumed to be equivalent to the current opportunity
cost of capital in Poland. It can be seen that, given a 12% reference rate, this project would be clearly
viable with an estimated 20.0% EIRR.
To demonstrate how the model can be used for sensitivity testing, construction costs 20%
higher can be substituted in column I of the evaluation table: this automatically
recalculates the EIRR at 16.0%. Similarly, raising the forecast growth rate for normal
traffic from 3.5% to 5.0% on Sheet I would increase the EIRR to 21.4%.
