Annex 6: Procurement and Disbursement Arrangements
POLAND: POLAND RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
Procurement
Procurement of goods and works will be done in accordance with World Bank Guidelines:
Procurement under the IBRD Loans and IDA Credits (issued in January 1995, revised
January and August 1996, September 1997, and January 1999). Consulting Services,
technical assistance and training will be procured in accordance with the Guidelines -
Selection and Employment of consultants by World Bank Borrowers, January 1997,
revised September 1997 and January 1999. The Bank's Standard Bidding Documents,
Request for Proposals and Forms of Consultants' Contract will be used. Goods and
technical assistance to be financed by the co-financiers will be procured in accordance with
the public procurement regulations or the regulations and practices of each co-financier. A
general procurement notice will be published in the Development Business of the UN in
April 2000.
Procurement responsibilities
The program components will be implemented at different levels: central, voivodship
(region), powiat (county) and gmina (municipality) levels, as shown below. The agencies
listed below will be responsible for procurement under each component.
A.    Micro-credit
-National Economy Bank (BGK) in close collaboration with MOLSP and the relevant voivodship
self-governments
B.    Human Capital Development
-VPIUs with the help of the Project Coordination Unit (PCU) in the Ministry of Agriculture and Rural
Development
-Voivodship Labor Offices and Ministry of Labor and Social Policy for sub-component B- I (Labor
Redeployment)
-Ministry of National Education for the internet based resource center; VPIUs; and gminas with help of the
VPIUs for school rehabilitation and transportation under sub-component B-2 (Education)
-Ministry of Interior and Public Administration for sub-component B-3 (Institution Building)
C.    Infrastructure
-gminas and powiats with the help of VPIUs
-PCU for TA and training
D.    Program Management
-PCU in the FAPA
Most of the proposed implementation agencies that will help the beneficiaries (gminas and powiats)
- 108 -



have experience in public procurement and procurement either under EU-Phare or bilateral funding
such as British Know-How Fund, USAID, etc. They have staff experienced in different aspects of
the procurement cycle, including technical and legal aspects. All the agencies operate in a public and
private procurement environment which is transparent and competitive. The most important
weakness of all the agencies is that they have little experience in international procurement, since the
procurement under the projects assisted by EU-Phare, USAID and BKHF, was normally small in
value and was conducted according to public procurement law. Because of this, the implementation
agencies have been placed in the average risk category.
Action Plan for Capacity Strengthening
In light of the risk-analysis above, recommendations have been made to strengthen the capacity of
the implementation agencies (see Section 2 of this Annex).
Procurement methods (Table A)
The project includes procurement of goods, works, and services. Procurement needs of Components A, B
and D have been identified and included in the detailed procurement plan which is part of the Project
Implementation Plan. A detailed procurement plan for these needs is prepared and included in the
Operational Manual. However, Component C - Infrastructure - which will aim at fulfilling the needs of
gminas and powiats will be demand-driven, except for some sub-projects which have been identified and
specified in the procurement plan. During project implementation, the procurement plan will be updated
every six months.
Thresholds for Procurement Methods
The following thresholds are proposed (the aggregates are given in the footnotes to the procurement table):
(i)   ICB for works = US$3 million or more per contract
ICB for goods = US$200,000 or more per contract
(ii)    NCB for works - Less than US$3 million per contract
NCB can be conducted in accordance with the Unlimited Tendering procedure, subject to the condition that
a point-system of evaluation will not be used; domestic preference will not be applied, international bidders
will not be excluded from bidding, and no restriction will be imposed on the use of foreign labor (except for
unskilled labor) and materials; and the NCB bidding documents will be agreed with the Bank before use.
(iii)   Minor Works = Less than US$200,000 per contract. A list of minor works contracts will be
published for expression of interest in participation in these tenders. All contractors responding to the
advertisement at any time will be invited to bid.
(iv)   International Shopping = Less than US$200,000 per contract; and
(v)    National Shopping = Less than US$50,000 per contract (can be conducted according to the
procedure called Request for Quotations under the Polish Public Procurement Law, subject to a minimum
of three quotations from domestic suppliers);
(vi)   Traditional purchasing practices shall apply to the projects to be funded with the
proceeds of the micro credit-line. Such purchases are not to exceed US$ 1,000 each. The total amount
- 109 -



allocated to the credit-line is US$15.26 million. However, the necessary controls will be put in place to
ensure that the credit amounts are used for the purpose intended.
Procurement of Consultants
Consultants Services will be procured through the Quality and Cost Based Selection (QCBS) procedures.
Such contracts will be advertised in the Development Business and in a national newspaper for expression
of interest, from which a shortlist will be drawn. For contracts estimated to cost less than US$200,000, the
short list may consist entirely of national firms, at least three. Foreign firms, if interested, will be allowed to
participate. Contracts for auditing and financial management will be procured through the Least Cost
Selection method (LCS). Consultants contracts for training and study tours will be procured following a
detailed training program that will be submitted to the Bank for its approval every six months during
project implementation. Contracts for assignments where quality is of prime importance, such as legal
advice, etc., will be awarded through Quality Based Selection (QBS) in which both technical and financial
proposals will be invited in two separate envelopes. Only the technical proposals will be opened first and
evaluated. The financial proposal of the firm that will score the highest in its technical proposal will be
opened and the contract price negotiated. Individuals experts will be selected in accordance with Part V of
the Consultants Guidelines. Candidatures will be advertised, and selection will be made on the basis of
comparison of qualification experience. The aggregates are included in the footnotes to Table A.
Procurement under Labor Redeployment Fund
Procurement under the Labor Redeployment Fund will be exclusively for services based on criteria and
procedures acceptable to the Bank, including, inter alia (i) the type of services to be provided, (ii) the
agencies eligible to provide said services, and (iii) the eligible costs that will be reimbursed in the service
delivery contracts as described in Annex 2 of the PAD, and further defined and discussed in the operational
manual. Individual contracts for services such as employment counseling, retraining and small business
assistance are generally not expected to exceed US$70,000. The Bank's no-objection is required for
contracts exceeding US$70,000. Service contracts for small business incubators are not expected to exceed
US$250,000, and will be subject to NCB (see subpara (ii) under the thresholds for procurement methods).
Procurement under the Labor Redeployment Fund will be carried out by the Voivodship Labor Offices and
generally be carried out on a service delivery contract basis (except for the incubators as noted above).
Voivodship Labor Offices will have to seek the no-objection of the project team located in the Ministry of
Labor and Social Policy for projects over US$35,000.
Prior review thresholds (Table B)
Prior Review Thresholds
(i)    All ICB contracts for goods and works
(ii)    First NCB from each implementation agency
(iii)   First IS and NS contract from each agency
(iv)   All consultant contracts US$200,000 or more each (Full Review)
(v)    All contracts with consulting firms estimated to US$100,000 or more each (Partial Review)
(vi)   All consultant contracts with individuals estimated to cost US$25,000 or more each
Post Review Ratio. One in ten contracts.
- 110-



Disbursement
Allocation of loan proceeds (Table C)
The project is expected to be disbursed over a period of four years. The anticipated
completion date is 01/31/04 and the closing date is 07/31/04. Disbursements will follow
normal Bank procedures and relevant Polish regulations and will be made against eligible
expenditures. Annex 5 shows the estimated disbursements during the life of the project and
table C shows the allocation of loan proceeds.
Use of statements of expenditures (SOEs):
Statements of Expenditures (SOE) will be used for: (a) goods contracts estimated to cost
less than US$200,000 each; (b) works contracts less than US$3,000,000 each; (c)
consultants contracts with firms less than US$100,000; (d) for services of individuals
costing US$25,000 equivalent or less each; and (e) all contracts under the credit-line. Full
documentation in support of SOE will be retained by each implementation agency for at
least two years after the closing date of the credit. This information will be made available
for review by Bank missions during project supervision and by auditors, the minimum
application size for payments directly from the grant account or for issuance of Special
Commitments is 20 percent of the special account authorization.
Special account:
To facilitate disbursements, the MOF will open a Special Account before loan
effectiveness and maintain it until project completion. The PCU will be responsible for the
overall management of the Special Account, and for submitting replenishment applications
and fully reconciled statements of expenditure to the Bank. The Special account will be
drawn upon to meet payments to contractors, suppliers and consultants under the project.
The special account will be established in the National Bank of Poland on terms and
conditions satisfactory to the Bank
The initial allocation to the Special Account will be limited to EUR5 million. The initial
allocation may be increased up to the authorized allocation of EURI0 million once an
aggregate disbursement of US$20 million is reached by submitting the relevant withdrawal
applications. Replenishment applications should be submitted by the PCU on a monthly
basis and must include reconciled bank statements as well as other appropriate supporting
documents. Project accounts will be operated for discrete activities and funded from the
Special Account based on disbursement applications signed by the authorized officials.

Section 2: Capacity of the Implementing Agency in Procurement and Technical Assistance
requirement review
- 115-



Capacity of the implementation agency to conduct procurement has been assessed. As a result,
the following action plan will be implemented:
A procurement book containing the guidelines, templates of tender notices, standard
bidding documents, evaluation formats, sample invitation to quote, request for proposals,
standard consultant contracts (for large and small assignments) should be prepared (both in
English and to the maximum extent possible in Polish), and made available immediately to all
implementation agencies so that their procurement staff will start familiarizing themselves with
the Bank procurement policies, procedures and documents. These documents will also be
provided on a diskette;
Before loan effectiveness but any time after negotiations a project launch workshop will
be held. In this workshop, sufficient time will be devoted to procurement to discuss the
procurement methods, thresholds, review levels, standard bidding documents, invitation to
quote, request for proposals, etc., to be used under the project. Prior to the above mentioned
workshop, each implementation agency will identify a procurement expert with educational
background and experience satisfactory to the Bank. This person will participate in the
workshop. Depending on the volume and complexity of procurement to be handled by an
implementation agency, this staff will attend the procurement training in ILO Turin or some
other suitable training program. This training shall be completed, preferably before the loan
effectiveness.

Additional
Annex No.: 11
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT
I .    The Social Assessment (SA) consisted of two main activities: (a) a collection of case studies,
expert consultations, and position papers focusing on the three topical areas of local governance, education,
and employment; and (b) the start of a system of monitoring and evaluation of primary schools in two
voivodship. The SA report focused on the three main themes of local governance, education, and
employment. Fieldwork in the form of in-depth interviews and participatory observation was carried out in
three rural towns in the Southeastern voivodship of Podkarpacki by a network of researchers highly
respected in the fields of anthropology and sociology; students; and community field workers and NGO
representatives with expertise in rural development and village life. The second activity involved a
collaboration with the Curator's Office in the Podkarpacki voivodship to build upon, and standardize,
existing data collection efforts taking place at individual schools with the aim to assess how they are faring
before and after consolidation.
A. study on local governance, education, and employment
Research Questions
1.    The research questions for the study revolve around three sets of issues: (a) in the area of local
governance, what are the priorities of the gmina and its capacity for long-term investment planning? To
what extent do residents participate both formally and informally in local decision-making?; (b) in the area
of education, what is the capacity for officials to implement the new school network and carry out other
areas of the educational reform? Do parents, teachers, and school administrators support the decision to
close primary schools in some rural communities and bus children to schools outside the community?; and
(c) in the area of employment, are farmers and unemployed persons interested in receiving job training and
job search assistance? What are the social, cultural and structural constraints to moving into a non-farm
occupation, starting a business, or participating in a retraining course?
2.    In addition to the three topical areas, the SA also attempted to gain an understanding of the social
forces shaping the life of the communities under investigation and of the relationships governing access to
employment, credit, and good schooling.
Methodology
I.    Fieldwork for the case studies was carried out in February 1999. A group of 15 researchers -
sociology students at Warsaw University under the supervision of 3 social scientists - spent a week in three
gmina in the Podkarpacki voivodship conducting interviews, making observations, and collecting
documents. A total of 60 individual interviews were conducted with elected officials, teachers, principals,
labor office employees, and private farmers and their families. The social scientists selected towns and
villages in the gminas of Chmielnik Rzesnowski, Debica, and Kamien for the study sites, places where they
had long established local ties and a feeling of trust among the residents.
2.     The survey data have been compared with, and supplemented by, earlier field surveys in the region,
including surveys of agrarian unemployment conducted in 10 villages in December 1996; official data from
- 127-



the Central Statistical Office and National Labor Office; and program documents prepared by government
experts, academic professors, and NGO leaders active in rural areas.
Findings
Local governance
].     During the Solidarity Movement, from 1989-1993, collective protest was one of the main ways
citizens made their voices heard. At its height in 1992, 752,472 people participated in strikes and
2,360,392 work days were lost. Farmers and agricultural workers were the first group to organize
country-wide protests in opposition to government policies. Prompted by its turbulent history and formal
requirements into the European Union, a host of legislation has been enacted in recent years requiring
public consultation in local investment decision-making. USAID has funded a number of
democracy-strengthening initiatives, bolstering the citizen advocacy activities of NGOs and volunteer
organizations, whose number (estimated at 50,000) far exceeds that operating in any other Eastern
European country.
2.     Formal avenues for participation are thus strong in Poland, especially in the urban areas where the
seats of government and the majority of NGOs are located. Some researchers express concern that
democratic processes may function less well in the rural areas. National opinion polls show, however, that
rural residents give higher approval ratings of their local leaders than urban residents. They feel more
recognized and listened to by local leaders and they believe local officials are genuinely interested in the
community's interests. This is likely the result of informal participatory mechanisms characteristic of
village life. Rural residents have easier access to their representatives than urban residents, and may even
know them personally and engage with them in their unofficial capacities.
3.     In principle, village meetings are held twice a year in rural gminas. In the more active gminas, like
Chmielnik, these meetings are very popular and attended by the majority of families. But the meetings
alone are not sufficient in making residents feel they can influence local affairs. In the less active gminas of
Kamien and Debica, there is a general feeling that decisions are taken somewhere "higher up" beyond the
reach of the common resident.
4.     The scope of public participation taking place in a gmina depends almost entirely on the wojt or
mayor's leadership style. The wojt in Chmielnik has been in office for 3 years and is highly respected in
the community. He has been very active in marshaling resources and support for his projects both within
and outside the gmina. Citizens are involved at the very early stages of decision-making and can see the
results of their efforts. In other communities, like Kamien, citizen input is solicited selectively and only
when required. In the community of Jozefow, the wojt and his council exercise tight control, and the
success of citizens' initiatives is tied to their ability to work well with and anticipate the concerns of local
authorities ("All we do, we do with close agreement with the wojt. ") The parish priest also plays a key
role in harnessing public support for investments and in neutralizing any opposing forces. Very often,
citizen initiatives are controlled by a few men, and women's role is confined to assisting the government
rather than challenging it in any way. Fear of outsider status and official retribution limit women's
participation, as well as their perception of what can be accomplished.
5.     Many residents of remote villages are convinced that the wojt is prejudicial toward initiatives
occurring in the more centrally located towns, and tends to select the sites for new infrastructure
investments in the town where the local authority office is located. In poor communities that rely almost
exclusively on central funding for infrastructure investments, the wojt is much more likely to place a
- 128-



greater priority on projects initiated by the central authority, even if they are not community priorities and
opposed by local citizens.
6.    In rural areas interest in local affairs is generally low. Most people focus on earning a living and
supporting their families and so, do not become involved in larger community concerns. Local issues that
provoke interest are those that affect the resident's immediate perception of his or her quality of life.
Infrastructure improvements, such as telephone, water, gas lines, roads, and sewers, are top priorities.
These are also the priorities of local officials.
7.     All gminas are required to have in place by the year 2000 a land use plan and a development
strategy to guide investment. Infrastructure expansion plans are usually the most important part of the
development strategy. Infrastructure investments tend to be financed from the gmina budget, community
contributions, and grants from international donors. In Chmielnik, community collections for the telephone
network, gas lines, and construction of the waste water treatment plant already overstretch the budgets of
most families, who soon will be paying an additional fee to connect their homes to the central sewerage
network.
8.     The capacity for long-term investment planning varies in every gmina. In Chmielnik, the
authorities developed a strategy consistent with past policies and with a broader regional development
strategy. By contrast, in Kamien, the strategy is less well-defined, lacks support from the various political
factions, and relies almost exclusively on external capital. The amount of citizen input into development
strategies also varies. According to Urban Institute studies (1996) on two cities located in the Southeastern
voivodships, Slaskie and Malopolski, the capital improvement, planning and budgeting programs of
Gliwice were developed and implemented without organized public review. In stark contrast, No"y Sacz
prepared its land use plans in consultation with the public, and incorporated 930 of the 1,230 citizens'
comments.
Education
I .   Many local officials feel that the Ministry of Education implemented the educational reforms
without giving sufficient attention as to how gmina officials and teachers will handle their new
responsibilities. In some communities, the reform requires local authorities to "undo" investments made
under the previous educational system, and this is a cause of frustration to the residents who contributed
their time and resources toward the earlier investment. For instance, in Kamien, authorities decided to
close down a newly built primary school and transport students to a larger school located in a nearby town.
However, this school cannot accommodate all the new students and authorities are exploring alternative
buildings.
2.     Local governments are still in the process of developing their plans for the new school network, and
do not have a clear idea how to organize the schools and finance school rehabilitation and student transport.
Issues tend to be more numerous and complex in the larger gminas such as Debica, where the formation of
the school network will involve a fundamental reorganization of the existing school system. It is also more
costly to implement the school network in the larger gminas. Debica authorities estimate that implementing
the standard model advocated by the Ministry of Education would cost 5 times the annual educational
expenditure. Already, the educational expenditure is the largest item in rural gmina budgets and is
supplemented by a very large subsidy by the central government, an amount often exceeding the entire
gmina budget. Local capacity for delivering education thus hinges on subsidies from the center, a burden
gmina officials fear will shift increasingly onto the shoulders of parents.
-129 -



3.    In the majority of instances, the law on educational reform was passed without the prior
consultation of affected communities. In the absence of official communique, the general public, including
teachers and parents, know very little about the reforms and rely on media reports for information.
Curators are now making efforts to consult with parents and teachers. In the Malopolski voivodship, a
school cannot be closed without the approval of parents; however, parental support is not a binding
condition.
4.     Teachers feel ambivalence toward the reforms. While some consider them to be necessary, many
are skeptical as to their future success given the lack of sufficient planning and available financing. Many
teachers worry about losing their jobs and consequently seek retraining to lower their chances of dismissal.
5.     Parents are reluctant to send their children to a school located outside the community. They fear
the commute will expose their children to added stress and to danger, and they do not want to absorb any
additional direct and indirect costs associated with student transport, such as higher school and transport
fees, meal expenses, and lower availability of children for household work.
6.     There is growing opposition in some villages to the closing of primary schools. Residents perceive
the loss of a school as an attack on the importance and status of the local community. Villagers are also
pressing for gymnasia (lower secondary schools) to be opened in small communities. In one isolated
community in Kamien, not only do residents oppose the closing of the primary school, they insist it be
expanded to include a sports gym and dance hall. They are also demanding that a new gymnasium be built
in the community.
Employment
I .    The level of official unemployment in the three gminas is on par with the national average of 10.6
percent; however, a significant share of the population is either underemployed or unemployed and not
counted in official statistics. The highest share of the unemployed are young people with vocational
education. Many of them cannot find a job upon graduation from vocational school and are fully supported
by their parents.
2.     As farms tend to be small in the Southeastern voivodships, the majority of farmers rely on outside
employment to earn a living. In recent years, many farmers have lost their jobs and they cannot afford to
live solely on their farm income. One solution might be to combine farms that are too small for commercial
operation, but many farmers view combining farms as socialistic and they are afraid they will lose control
over their land. There are also legal disincentives against forming cooperatives. Individual farms are not
taxed on sales proceeds, but cooperatives are heavily taxed.
3.     The residents of the three gmina believe that local branches of the county labor offices are
ineffective in finding jobs for the unemployed. Posted vacancies tend to be out-dated, and often require
skills not taught in the village schools or apprenticeships. Many of the vacancies are located in the larger
towns of the county (powiat), and the poorer residents cannot afford to commute or move to another town
where apartment rents are higher. Most recruitment and hiring is done on the basis of personal contacts,
and employers bypass the employment labor offices altogether.
4.     The most serious entrepreneurial constraints include the inability of entrepreneurs to gain formal
access to credit, since real estate is infrequently accepted for SME loans, and the fact that women have
fewer financial resources than men. Other entrepreneurial constraints include: high cost of agricultural
- 130-



inputs; lack of infrastructure, markets, storage, refrigeration, packaging; and low producer prices. Lack of
child care is a severe constraint to women's entrepreneurial and employment opportunities.
5.     Since the transition, women are the first to be let go from their jobs and men are the preferred
employees. Women make up a smaller proportion of the participants in newly established business
associations, and women-owned business comprise only 15 to 30 percent of the loan portfolios of most
banks. No more than one-fourth of newly created firms are owned or managed by a woman.
Impact of the Social Assessment on Project Design and Implementation
I .    The Social Assessment highlights the need for formal and regular public review processes.
Mechanisms have been put into place to ensure that public concerns are articulated and incorporated into
the design of the project. One mechanism is the demonstration of public input into sub-project
identification and selection. This is a basic requirement in the Investment Appraisal Criteria for
investments in infrastructure and in the school network. Gminas will be required to submit evidence
demonstrating public awareness of, and involvement in, the process of identifying local investment needs
and priorities. In addition to providing evidence of community support, gminas desiring investments to
support the consolidation of the primary school network will be required to conduct a Social Assessment if:
(i) schools in the network serve communities with populations that are ethnically, religiously, or culturally
diverse from one another; or (ii.) the transport distance from home to school exceeds 10 kilometers. The
specifics of the SA are indicated in the TOR in the Operations Manual. A second mechanism is the
addition of citizen participation approval methods as a topical area for the training of civil servants in the
training and infrastructure components of the project. Client surveys and beneficiary assessments serve as
the third mechanism for incorporating public views into the project. A positive evaluation of the training
program is a trigger for moving the training component of the project to the second phase.
2.     A follow-on Social Assessment of the Primary School Network is clearly needed and will be
conducted in early August. Since the SA covered a broad range of issues, it was not possible to investigate
any one component of the project in depth. At the design stage of the SA, the educational reforms were still
being debated within the Ministry of Education and their implementation was uncertain. The SA on
Education will identify the main issues arising from the reorganization of the school network as viewed by
local officials, parents, teachers, and students. Specifically, the SA will focus on concerns raised by the
closing of primary schools, the selection of sites for the newly created lower secondary schools, and the
transport of children from remote areas. The findings and recommendations will be circulated to local
educational authorities.
3.     Examples of good practice in all areas of rural development should be articulated to the less active
and poorer wmina. Local officials and residents appreciate, and can learn from, good examples of best
practice particularly when the activities are low cost and already proven worthwhile in like communities.
Efforts to disseminate local innovations are already a part of the project design of the private sector
development component. An important activity in this component is the public relations campaign,
consisting of mobile rural workshops, guest lectures at rural high schools, and a bi-weekly television
program, to espouse the virtues of entrepreneurship and increase awareness of existing support services
available to current and perspective rural entrepreneurs. A similar activity is being considered for the
education component. This activity would be in the form of a road show of workshops displaying the best
practices in education initiated by local teachers and administrators. These workshops would serve as
model examples for the other gminas to follow.
4.     Women's ability to find suitable employment and start their own businesses deserves greater
- 131 -



attention. As a result of the SA, gender issues have become more fully integrated into supervision,
monitoring, and evaluation, first through gender-based monitoring of: persons and businesses gaining
access to credit, business start-ups, types of businesses, successes and failures, employment,
unemployment, and redeployment; and second, through the development and adoption of equal opportunity
criteria in the selection of SME credit applications. Gender issues have been also made more visible in
project design. Criteria for the Labor Redeployment Fund will now contain specific references to women,
and questions will be added to the labor surveys addressing women's experiences in finding and retaining
employment. In addition, effort will be made to ensure that 50 percent of the successful rural entrepreneurs
performing on the bi-weekly television are women.
5.     Affordability analysis on residents' ability to pay for tariff adjustments arising from infrastructure
investments should be given strong consideration in light of the type of financial arrangements made to the
gminas. If the GOP on-lends to the gmina, residents will be expected to assume a growing share of the
costs associated with infrastructure improvements. Hence, during project screening, it is important that
gminas not only demonstrate their financial capacity to support the proposed investment, but also justify
the approach taken in light of available alternatives. They should be able to demonstrate that they
conducted an exhaustive search for a solution that was least cost and affordable. They should also provide
a projection of the anticipated tariff adjustment and state the level of contribution that would be required
from citizens with low, average and high incomes. Criteria to this effect will be incorporated into the
Operations Manual. An affordability analysis-has also been added as a component to the evaluation study
to be completed at the end of Phase I. By the same token, experience from other World Bank infrastructure
projects in the ECA region (e.g. Latvia Daugavpils Water Project) has shown that municipal officials and
enterprise directors are often reluctant to raise tariffs and cut costs by downsizing, severely hampering
project implementation, and in some cases, stalling it altogether. Wherever possible, survey data on
household income and expenditures, often more accurate than official wage statistics, should be used in the
calculation of tariff adjustments.
B. development of monitoring and evaluation systems of primary schools
I .    The Bank initiated a pilot data collection exercise with the Curator's Office in Rzeszow to monitor
and evaluate the progress of schools before and after consolidation. In 10 gminas located throughout the
Podkarpacki voivodship, school inspectors assessed two schools slated to be merged together: a small
school with only a few students and with multi-grade teaching, and a large school with no multi-grade
teaching. The assessment entails classroom observations, an evaluation of teachers, an evaluation of 8th
grade students based on a test designed and implemented in four voivodships, an inventory of supplies and
equipment, an evaluation of physical infrastructure, and the collection of data based on school records
allowing for the calculation of attendance rates, repetition rates, drop-out rates, completion rates, and
progression from primary into secondary. The first round took place in March 1999 and a second round is
planned next Spring several months after the schools have been consolidated.
2.     The pilot test showed tremendous eagerness on the part of the Curator's Office to participate in the
activity and contribute to the lessons learned. Achieving local ownership of the pilot activity is in itself an
important outcome, as it may result in higher quality data collection efforts in the future in this region, and
also in others. The data collected in the first round are of mixed quality. Teachers' evaluations and the
evaluation of classroom processes are based on the inspectors' subjective viewpoints, making cross-school
comparisons difficult; reports of student attrition are very low and likely the result of inaccurate attendance
records; however, the rest of the data are good and can be used in the monitoring and evaluation exercise.
- 132 -



3.     Results of the pilot test will be shared with local and Ministry education officials, and any lessons
learned will be incorporated into the project's implementation arrangements. The first round has
demonstrated the need for school staff to be trained in education management techniques, including
record-keeping and data collection and management. In the long run, it is hoped that the pilot will lead
toward more systematic development of education indicators and improved data collection capacity in local
schools.

SOCIAL DEVELOPMENT DATA SHEET FOR PROJECTS
in the IBRD/IDA Lending Program
Country: Poland
Project ID No:PL-PE-58202
Project Name: Rural Development Project
Total Project Cost: US$301.04 million
Appraisal Date: July 15, 1999
IBRD/IDA Cost:US$120 million
Board Date: February 24, 2000
Task Team Leader: Piotr M. Wilczynski
Managing Unit: ECSSD
Est. date for receipt of SA by Bank: April 30, 1999
SD Team Member: Kimberly Cartwright
SA Categories are rated as follows:
Category A _ Controversial, sensitive, high social risk, or substantial adverse social impacts.
Category B _ Ownership, equity, targeting, or project effectiveness can be improved through SA.
Category C _ No significant SA benefits.
SA Category: B
Date Category Assigned: November 10, 1998
Date Sheet Prepared: April 30, 1999
Date Updated: January 20, 2000
Major Project Components:
The Rural Development Project has four main components: (i) micro-credit, (ii) human capital
development, (iii) infrastructure, and (iv) program management. Financing of these investments will
directly and indirectly support the creation of new non-farm economic activities in the countryside. The
investments include both physical works and intangibles such as skills, knowledge, and the development of
aptitude and industrial operational discipline. The project is designed so that individual elements from
each of the three components can be implemented at the local level, singly or in combination with others.
For example, a gmina (local commune) may identify the need for training for the unemployed and a credit
to help entrepreneurs start their own businesses.
Objectives of each of the 4 components, and their sub-components are, as follows:
(i) Micro-credit
- promote micro-enterprises and create and maintain self-employment in rural areas.
(ii) Human Capital Development
- 141 -



Redeployment of the Rural Unemployed
- help integrate the unemployed back into the economy
- improve the occupational and geographical mobility of the rural work force by providing a mix of
services that will address frictional and structural unemployment as well as promote self-employment and
job creation at the local level.
Education
- improve the efficiency of the education system and quality of graduates of school education by enhancing
the quality of teaching taking place in rural primary schools and gymnasiums and improving the utilization
of school resources.
Trainina for Public Servants in the Local/Regional Administration
- increase the level of efficiency and effectiveness in selected units of regional and local administration
through training and pilot institutional development programs.
(iii) Rural Infrastructure
- improve the technical infrastructure in rural areas in order to stimulate private sector investments,
facilitate the creation of non-farm jobs, improve environmental and health conditions, narrow the gap
between rural and urban areas in economic development.
(iv) Program Management and Technical Assistance
- assist rural communities with project selection, preparation and evaluation of sub-projects for financing
under the program, as well as overall program coordination and management.
Social Development Objectives of the project: (e.g. poverty alleviation and equity, social inclusion,
social cohesion, community development, strengthening of civil society)
The social development objectives of the RDP are to reduce structural poverty, support greater inclusion
of the unemployed into the economic mainstream, reduce regional inequalities in income and access to
services.
Steps for Social Analysis:
If social analysis is based on prior work, identify sources or describe social assessment actions and
schedule for the project?
The Social Assessment is a collection of case studies, expert consultations, and position papers produced
by a network of over 100 researchers, community field workers, NGO representatives, and students living
and working in rural Poland. The majority of the contributors have long standing relationships with the
citizens residing in small villages and towns and have been active in supporting the development of the
countryside. For the Social Assessment, some of them returned to the villages they know best, bringing
along sociology and anthropology students from the University of Warsaw to conduct case studies of
schools, employment offices, and self-government offices in the gminas of Kamien, Chmielnik, and Debica
(Podkarpackie region). Others drew upon their own research and experiences throughout Poland to give
-142 -



their perspectives on the proposed educational reform and on the role of the private sector and the public
regional employment offices to offer assistance to the rural unemployed. The authors (Batory Foundation,
EKO-OKO Foundation and Policy Action Group) also collected a diverse set of materials, such as
advertising leaflets, local newspapers, schools bulletins and official documents for the basis of the
information presented in the report.
Key Social Development Issues arising from the Social Analysis: (summarize from Annex 1)
There is concern that parents will not support the new educational reforms to merge and consolidate
primary schools out of fears that school buses are not safe, the cost of schooling will rise, their children
will travel long distances, and the new school will be inferior to the current school. Teachers are afraid
they will lose their jobs. Many villagers also oppose the closing down of small primary schools as schools
have traditionally been viewed to be symbols of community cohesion. Social tensions among neighboring
gminas may impede the efficient placement of the newly-created gymnasia. These issues will be explored
further in a follow-on SA on education to be conducted in August 1999. The education SA will assist the
Bank in providing policy advice to the local departments of education as to how to mitigate against any
negative social impacts arising from the educational reforms. The Bank is also requiring select gminas to
conduct their own social assessments (communities with long travel distances and those experiencing social
tensions).
There is the possibility that the beneficiaries of the employment and training programs will not respond to
incentives offered by service providers (even though these incentives are based on current demand).
Service providers may false perceptions of demand; the unemployed may not have enough trust in these
programs, or in the institutions administering the programs and/or they may doubt their ability to find
suitable employment after retraining. Client surveys and gender-based indicators will serve as monitoring
devices to evaluate the success of the training programs.
Although tariffs will undoubtedly rise as a result of infrastructure improvements, the stakeholders have not
expressed concern about affordability. However, such concerns are likely to come to the forefront in the
near future, and will be addressed in the feasibility studies and through public consultations required by the
project (in the investment appraisal criteria) and by the European Commission (OJ No L 73/7, 14.3.97).
Participation Plan
Has the participation plan been prepared and annexed to the project document?
Yes[ ]       No[X ]        TBD [ ]
(If yes, complete participation matrix in Annex 2)
What steps are planned to ensure participation in preparation, implementation, monitoring & evaluation?
First, individual gminas and associations of local communities have been consulted on past efforts to
finance infrastructure and on priority investment needs. These consultations were critical in pushing gmina
officials to develop their land use plans and prepare proposals for the pipeline, and to mobilize local
support for the project. A needs assessment is also planned for the education component.
Second, the Polish Business and Innovations Centres Association, an umbrella network of business
incubators, established under a Bank-supported urban employment project, has been consulted on ways to
apply the concept of business incubators to rural areas. Positive impressions gained from visits to several
urban incubators led to the conclusion that rural incubators should be supported in this project.
- 143-



Third, the Social Assessment utilized a highly participatory approach with contributions from local
residents and employees of school and employment institutions, officials at the Ministries of Education and
Labor, community based workers, NGO personnel, university professors, and school curators in one
voivodship (region).
Fourth, there has been strong NGO/CSO involvement in project preparation (see Appendix 4).
NGOs/CSOs will play key roles during project implementation and resources are earmarked to ensure their
participation.
Fifth, surveys, beneficiary assessments, and public awareness campaigns and consultations are planned as
regular activities under the various components and sub-components. These consultations will provide
much of the informational bases of the sub-components, as well as serve as mechanisms for stakeholders
and beneficiaries to voice their opinions, and in some instances, actively participate in project activities.

Involvement of NGOs or Other Civil Society Organizations (NGOs/CSOs)
NGOs/CSOs include professional organizations, academic institutes, the media, trade/peasant unions,
business organizations, cooperatives, NGOs, community based organizations, and other organized forms
of civil society.: (summarize text from Annex 4)
There has been tremendous grassroots involvement from a variety of NGOs/CSOs during project
preparation. NGOs/CSOs, together with ministries and other agencies, developed the eligibility criteria for
the selection of gminas into the project, as well as assisted other areas of project preparation. NGOs will
play important roles in project implementation _ chiefly, as service providers of educational and
employment services and training, and as partners in the creation and operation of business service centers,
and in monitoring the progress of the programs.

SD Data Sheet: ANNEX - 1
Social Analysis
Table 1.1: Key Social Development Issues
I)     (i) Which social groups will be impacted by the investment project?
(ii) What social and cultural factors are likely to effect / be affected by the project?
(i.) Parents of children in primary schools will be affected by the Government's educational reform and
educational investments; entrepreneurs, farmers, unemployed persons will be affected by the training programs
and loan guarantees and micro-credits supported by the project.
(ii.) No anticipated social and cultural factors will effect investments in infrastructure. Social tensions between
communities in some gminas may impede the consolidation of primary schools and efficient placement of
gymnasia. Many villages oppose school closings as the school is a symbol of cultural heritage and plays an
important social function as well. There is concern that farmers, especially former State farm employees, are
passive and dependent upon social benefits and thus, will not exhibit strong demand for retraining.
2)      (i) Who are the major stakeholders?
(ii) What were the views expressed by major stakeholders?
(iii) In what way have these views been incorporated into project design?
(i.) The main stakeholders are, first and foremost, rural residents. Other key stakeholders are: parents of children
attending primary schools, teachers and local school administrators, unemployed persons, employees of the active
labor programs, employees of public utilities, farmers, government agencies (including Ministries of Finance,
Agriculture, Labor, Education, and Environment), self-governments and financial organizations, and EU
institutions and other donors addressing rural development issues in Poland.
(ii. & iii.) Parental concern over school transportation will be explored further in regular community consultations
and individual interviews. Beneficiary assessments evaluating the utility of training programs for the unemployed
and for public servants will be conducted. Although tariffs will undoubtedly rise as a result of infrastructure
improvements, the stakeholders have not expressed concern about affordability. However, such concerns are
likely to come to the forefront in the near future, and will be addressed in the feasibility studies and through public
consultations required by the project and by the European Commission.
3)      Describe the institutional and implementation arrangements to ensure inclusion of the poor or other
excluded/vulnerable groups in project benefits
A large share of the rural poor are small farmers and former State farm employees. The employment programs
financed by the Labor Redeployment Fund are targeted directly at these two groups.
The Gemini report (1994) pointed out that female entrepreneurs face many more coristraints than male
entrepreneurs in setting up their own businesses. No more than 20 to 25% of newly-created firms are owned and
managed by a woman. Women are often excluded from employment opportunities. The same report found that
women have much lower chances than men of finding jobs . To ensure that women are adequately represented in
the employment and training programs supported by the project, the project includes gender-based monitoring of
persons and businesses gaining access to credit, business start-ups, types of businesses, successes and failures,
employment, unemployment, and redeployment.
- 146-



4)      Describe the institutional and implementation arrangements to ensure mitigation of adverse impacts on
project affected persons?
Any potentialiy adverse impacts will be flagged through regular beneficiary assessments and surveys to be carried
out during implementation and through special social assessment studies. The social assessments, that will be
conducted in communities where children will be transported for distances longer than 10 km or those with high
social tensions, require officials to design an action plan for taking public input into account of existing policies.
The client surveys and beneficiary assessments are used as triggers moving the training component ahead to the
second phase. Any negative findings must be taken into account before the component moves ahead to the next
phase.
5) What mechanisms have been established and resources earmarked explicitly for monitoring &
evaluation of social impacts of the investment project?
Social impact indicators for the project components have been developed and will be used for monitoring and
evaluation purposes. The first data set which will give the baseline figures has already been purchased from the
Central Statistical Office (GUS). As it will be necessary to have school-level information for the education
component currently unavailable from GUS, the project is considering supporting data collection efforts in this
area. The Social Scientist has collaborated with school curators from one Southern voivodship and received
qualitative and quantitative data from 20 schools. This information will allow us to compare schools on a variety
of dimensions before and after consolidation.

SD Data Sheet: ANNEX - 3
COMPLIANCE WITH MANDATORY SOCIAL POLICIES
I.    OD 4.20 Indigenous Peoples. The terms "indigenous peoples," "indigenous ethnic
minorities," "tribal groups," and "scheduled tribes" describe social groups with a social and
cultural identity distinct from the dominant society that makes them vulnerable to being
disadvantaged in the development process. For the purposes of this directive, "indigenous
peoples" is the term used to refer to these groups.
The Bank's broad objective towards indigenous people is to ensure that the development
process fosters full respect for their digniity, human rights, and cultural uniqueness. More
specifically, the objective is to ensure that indigenous peoples do not suffer adverse effects
during the development process, particularly from Bank-financed projects, and that they
receive culturally compatible social and economic benefits. For an investment project that
affects indigenous peoples, the borrower should prepare an indizenous peoples development
plan (IPDP) that is consistent with the Bank's policy.
1.1   What are the anticipated impacts of the investment project on indigenous peoples? [describe nature of
impact and number of project affected persons (PAPs)]
N/A
1.2   Does the IPDP (a) ensure that indigenous people benefit from development projects, and (b) avoid or
mitigate potentially adverse effects on indigenous people caused by Bank-assisted activities?
N/A
1.3   What steps have been taken to ensure that the IPDP strategy is based on the informed participation of the
indigenous people?
N/A

ENVIRONMENTAL DATA SHEET FOR PROJECTS
in the IBRD/IDA Lending Program
Country: Poland
Project ID No:PL-PE-58202
Project Name: Rural Development Project
Total Project Cost: US$301.04 million (US$120 million IBRD loan)
Appraisal Date: July, 1999
Board Date: April 11, 2000
Task Team Leader: Piotr Wilczynski
Managing Unit: ECSSD
Est. date for receipt of EA by Bank: N/A
EA Category (A/B/C): Fl
Date Assigned: May, 1999
Date Sheet Updated: July 22, 1999
Major Project Components: The primary objective of the project is to contribute to the financing of
investments in education and training, rural infrastructure, and private sector development that will directly
and indirectly support creation of new non-farm economic activities in the countryside. Investments will
include both physical works as well as human capital development in the forms of skills, knowledge and
aptitude development and industrial operational discipline. There will be four major components briefly
described as follows:
Component A-Micro-credit: typical elements include (i) technical assistance support to small
businesses, (ii) loans to small entrepreneurs
Component B-Human Capital Development: (i) design and implementation of active labor
programs (ALPs) including training, specialized employment services and public works, (ii) programs of
adult literacy improvement, improving teaching in primary schools in rural areas, improve quality and
access to gymnasia in rural areas, and (iii) training for public servants in local/regional administrations
Component C-Rural Infrastructure: small infrastructure investments in water supply and sewage
collection/treatment facilities, solid waste disposal site development, rural road improvements
Component D-Program Management: Assist in the establishment of capabilities in rural
communities for project preparation (selection, preparation, evaluation) as well as technical assistance for
overall program coordination and management.
Major Environmental Issues: Environmental issues are primarily associated with Component C
(Infrastructure). Under Component C, the issues are likely to be experienced primarily in the construction
phase (land clearing, movement of men, equipment and materials to and from project site, etc.). However,
if the projects are improperly designed, environmental issues would be manifested during operations (e.g.
wastewater sludge disposal, solid waste leachates contaminating groundwater supplies, etc.).
Other Environmental Issues : The variety of sub-projects likely to be supported under the project will
require careful attention and evaluation by responsible local/regional (voivodship, gmina etc.) and possibly
national environmental authorities. Environmental legislation and the environmental regulatory framework
in Poland are in general both adequate and effective.
- 154-



Proposed Actions: Specific mechanisms and specific institutions likely to be responsible for specific
sub-project environmental evaluation, permitting, licensing, approval etc. have been identified.
Furthermore, an assessment of the technical capabilities of environmental institutions has been done, and
as required a program of institutional strengthening will be included in the project for them to effectively
discharge their responsibilities for environmental approval.
Additionally, a comparison between World Bank environmental review (EA) requirements and those of
Poland should be made. To the extent that there are differences (e.g. category assignment, public
participation, documentation and implementation requirements for environmental management plans etc.),
local Polish environmental institutions should be provided with adequate and sufficient strengthening to
review and approve projects in a manner.that is consistent with both requirements of Polish law and World
Bank environmental policies and procedures.
Justification/Rationale for Environmental Category: Projects will be identified and prepared at local
levels. Since funds will flow through intermediate institutions, the Fl Category is considered most
appropriate.

Additional
Annex No.: 15
Lessons Learned from Multi-Sector Projects
Background
1. During the preparation of the Poland Rural Development Program, about which more below, it
became clear that success of that program would depend to a large extend upon local implementation
capacity. This is a result of the complexity of many of the components proposed and of the recent
decentralization of many public sector functions and responsibilities from central to local governments. It
was soon realized, though, that reliance on local implementation, with direct involvement of ultimate
stakeholders, might not be a serious constraint, may actually be an advantage. Moreover, it was recognized
that the overall level of development and the nature and character of the local institutions and beneficiaries,
in contemporary Poland, allow for assigning of major responsibilities for implementation to local bodies. It
was at that point that task management wanted to be assured that precedents exist, elsewhere in the Bank,
of rural development projects of a similar nature as the one contemplated for Poland, that have relied to a
large extent on local implementation capacity. If such were the case, management would like to know what
lessons these other Bank projects might have for what is contemplated for the program in Poland. This note
addresses these issues.
The Poland Rural Development Program (RDP)
2.     The Poland Rural Development Program addresses current high unemployment in rural
regions of Poland, and the anticipated further reduction in agricultural employment over the coming years
as a consequence of the restructuring of the farming sector. The program is aimed at facilitating the
creation of new non-farm job opportunities in rural areas through an integrated program of investments in
basic infrastructure, human capital development, and direct assistance for private sector development. The
program will be responsive to grass-roots demands as expressed in regional and local infrastructure
investment plans. Therefore the program will be part of Poland's national public investment program, and
its modalities.
3.    The program's design is driven by economic and social considerations. It will provide a
focus on rural unemployment by creating a more conducive environment for the motor of job-creation, the
SME sector, in the most depressed rural areas, and seeks to integrate lessons learned from other complex,
multi-sectoral projects. In its paper: World Bank experience with Rural Development. 1965-1986, OED
argues that the following issues are crucial for the successful implementation of a complex, multi-sectoral
rural development project: (a) strong government commitment to the project, (b) a favorable
macroeconomic environment, (c) reliance upon locally proven technical innovations, (d) flexibility in
program design so as to allow adaptations during implementation, (e) integration of a comprehensive
monitoring and evaluation process and effective use of its feedback mechanism and, (g) full community
participation. Following is a summary of such multi-sectoral rural development projects whose experiences
are germane to what is proposed for the Poland RDP.
Project Summaries and Conclusion
4.     China: Southwest Poverty Reduction Project. This project successfully demonstrates
- 156 -



the effectiveness of a focused multi-sector rural development project that is locally implemented. In its
QAC report the project's actual and potential impact at the highest policy level is characterized as "best
practice". The rating is in large part due to China's strong commitment to poverty reduction, its impressive
implementation capacities, especially on the county level, strong local participation and the efforts of the
task manager. The project has eight main components, ranging from land and farmer development to the
creation of social services and an employment generation component. Its total cost is almost half a billion
dollars, of which the Bank finances about 50%. A hierarchical structure of implementation agents from
national to county level, with operational focus on the latter, has assured effective creation of the project's
many components, allowing it to reach its developmental objectives successfully.
5.     Cyprus: Pitsilia Integrated Rural Development Project. The main objective of this
project was the development of the mountainous, economically depressed region of Pitsilia by enhancing its
productive resources and improving its social services. Despite its complexity, the project was completed
only one year behind schedule. The lessons learned from this project are: (i) a multi-component rural
development project can be implemented without major delay or deviation from the original objectives, if
there is strong government commitment, competent and devoted management, and a suitable coordination
mechanism and, (ii) flexible coordination arrangements are needed for adjusting to the different phases of
the project and to changing circumstances. The project had three main components: (a) investments in rural
infrastructure, (b) in social infrastructure, notably in schools, health centers and water supplies and (c)
institutional developments. Total project cost was US$ 21 million, of which the Bank financed 85%.
Overall implementation responsibility rested with the Ministry of Agriculture and Natural Resources, while
a total of more than 20 agencies from different ministries were involved with the operational aspects of
implementation. Overwhelming support of the ultimate stakeholders for the successful land consolidation
achieved under the project, attests to the success of the project's participatory efforts.
6.     Korea: Infrastructure Project II. Since 1971, Korea has undertaken a massive program
of rural infrastructure works under the Saemaeul (New Community) movement. These works range from
micro-scale improvements of village drains to sophisticated large-scale utility or irrigation projects. The
Bank has been involved in this sector since 1976 when it approved a loan of US$60 million to finance the
first Rural Infrastructure Project which was satisfactorily completed in March 1980. Under the
Infrastructure Project 11 a US$95 million loan was approved in 1978, to finance 41% of total project costs,
aiming at improving living conditions in rural areas, achieving decentralized economic growth, reducing the
influx of rural people to large cities and building self-reliance in rural areas. The project has achieved or
exceeded almost all of its physical targets. Among the reasons for smooth project implementation was the
development strategy, based on decentralized decision making, self-help from the villagers, and willingness
of the beneficiaries to contribute cash and labor, both for construction, organizing and managing of project
work. The project's success may be partially attributed to the fact that it represented a time slice of
on-going development programs, was implemented by experienced agencies, and was strongly supported by
central government.
7.     Bulgaria: Regional Investment Fund Project (LIL). This LIL aims at testing the social fund
mechanism as a means of improving the standards of living amongst the poor and unemployed during
economic transition. One of the key features of this project, appraised in November 1998, is to delegate
substantial responsibility to the Municipal Councils and NGOs in order to assure maximum local
participation. Under the supervision of the Project Management Unit (PMU), the Municipal Councils and
NGOs prepare micro-projects and select contractors for implementation. Due to the recent banking crisis
in Bulgaria the Municipal Councils are not involved in the flow of funds but authorize the PMU to pay the
contractors. Based on evaluating the Municipal Councils'lNGOs' handling of grants their responsibilities
in dealing with the flow of funds will be gradually extended.
- 157 -



8.    Moldova Social Investment Fund (SIF) Project. This US$ 20.3 million project, of which
the Bank will finance 73% has two main objectives: (i) help empower rural communities by strengthening
their decision making capacity and, (ii) improve the quality of basic social and economic services for rural
populations in Moldova, particular for the poor. The SIF provides a new model of community-based
development, and offers a modest, first opportunity to those who have suffered disproportional from the
economic transition. Government will pass on the funds under the Bank loan, to the Project Management
Unit, which in turn finances micro-projects proposed by community beneficiary groups. Operational
guidelines for project implementation, laid down in a detailed implementation manual, are based, in part, on
experiences gained from a pilot project.
Conclusions
The projects in China, Cyprus and Korea proof that complex integrated rural development projects
designed by the Bank have been implemented successfully. The lessons learned indicate that strong
government commitment, flexible implementation arrangements and beneficiary participation on the local
level are crucial elements for a positive result of project implementation. The projects in Bulgaria and
Moldova which are based on Social Investment Funds show that the Bank supports community based
development by delegating substantial responsibility to local authorities.
- 158-

Evaluation of the    "This is a complex and ambitious project and yet achieves substantial
Project if available  success in implementation." Its actual and potential impact at the highest
policy level can be characterized as "best practice" (QAG Review of
East Asia Rural Poverty Reduction Projects; November 5, 1997)
Project Design and Supervision Strengths:
-Effective targeting to the poorest households even in the poor areas;
-Strong local participation;
Strong task management despite limited resources;
Bringing to bear imaginative approaches for poverty reduction
interventions;
-The commitment, dedication and ability of the Government of China to
implement a complex and sophisticated project, with many lessons for
elsewhere in China and other parts of the developing world.
Areas for Improvement of Design and Implementation:
-Making de facto phasing of the project de jure during mid-term review
by recognizing its complexity and demanding pace for China;
- Greater focus on addressing the broad ranging long term institutional
implications of the project to ensure sustainability
-Greater attention to the myriad specific sectoral and subsectoral policy
and institutional changes thrown up by the project
'Need for simplifying and increasing flexibility in the Bank's
procurement procedures

features           project was the strong government support. Poverty reduction was the
absolute priority of the government. The LGPR, which is in the Chinese
government hierarchy higher than the ministerial level sent out invitations
to the implementing agencies to attend the project launch workshop. The
professional future of the civil servants involved in the implementation of
the project was dependent on the positive results of the project.

The project started of with large Coordination Committee to organize
implementation of the project. (see chart) As this committee turned out
to be too large it was split up into five Working Committees - for soil
conservation, irrigation schemes, Xyliatos-dam, roads and rehabilitation

Process where        components that involved local contribution, project management soon
Stakeholders are     saw the importance of informing and involving farmers quite fully. This
Taken into Account  included the farmers' approval of all irrigation and land consolidation
schemes, establishment of soil conservation division for bench terracing,

contributions towards meeting the costs of most project components.
The most extensive interaction with farmers occurred in the six villages
where land consolidation was implemented after substantial interaction
to take into account objections and reaching solutions. Overall, the
farmers response was positive as reflected by the 80% majority who
voted in favor of consolidation and thus made possible the successful
termination of the farm access roads and irrigation distribution system,
which were affected by the land consolidation.
Current Status of    Loan was approved in August 1977 for the sum of US$10.0 million,

Lessons Learned     In this case, a multi-component project design proved viable in spite of
numerous and complex problems of coordination that might easily have
led to failure. The lessons to be learned are:
(i) a multi-component rural development project can be implemented
without major delay or deviation from the original objectives, if there is
strong government commitment, competent and devoted management,
and a suitable coordination mechanism
(ii) flexible coordination arrangements are needed for adjusting to the
different phases of the project and changing circumstances Various
coordination bodies proved instrumental for the success of the project.
- 164-



Evaluation of the   Despite its complexity, the project was completed only one year behind
Project if available   schedule. Most of the components were completed as planned, and in
several aspects exceeded appraisal estimates.
The key aspects in successful implementation appear to have been
a) a project design which gave first importance to the key limiting
factors, which in this case was water;
b) a competent, highly motivated and persistent project coordinator;
c) strong Government commitment;
d) sound procedures for involving villagers to assure their agreement on
priorities and requiring their commitment throughout implementation;
and
e) warranted flexibility on the part of Bank staff in supporting key
project modifications requested by the project unit.
This was the implementation arrangement at the beginning of the project. As the Coordination Committee
turned out to be too large it was split up into five Working Committees. In the process of implementation
the number of committees was adjusted according to the actual needs.

Project           Korea Rural Infrastructure Project II
(Peter Whitford - Task Manager)
Developmental    The project was part of the Saemuaeul Program which was initiated in
Objective of the    1971 as a nationwide comprehensive self-help program to improve living
Project           conditions in rural areas, to achieve decentralized economic growth, to
reduce the influx of rural people to large cities and to build self-reliance in
rural areas.
Project           1. Minor irrigation (US$81.8 million)
Components        11. River Training (US$37.5 million)
III. Water Supply (US$37.4 million)
IV. Rural Telephones (US$25.1 million)
The Bank financed 41% of total project cost or US$95.0 million. Total
project cost was US$232.0 million (At the closing of the loan the cost
overrun of the irrigation component was estimated at about 25% while the
overall project cost was about 11% above the appraisal estimate.)
Number of         Four Ministries: Agriculture (MOA), Home Affairs (MHA), Health and
Implementing      Social Affairs (MHSA) and Communication (MC)
Agencies
Pattern of        No attempt was made to set up an organization responsible for executing
Project           project works or for coordinating the four executing agencies. The project,
Organization      however, was successfully implemented, mostly because it consisted of a
series of on-going programs, strongly supported by the government and the
project beneficiaries with minimal disruption for each executing agency.
Flow of Funds     Funds to be supplied by the Bank were mainly disbursed to the Ministry of
Finance (MOF) as a reimbursement for completed works. Approved
budget amounts were disbursed by the MOF to the responsible ministries
in four equal installments at the beginning of each calendar year. For the
irrigation component government loans were made to the subproject
owners, the Farmland Improvement Associations (FLIAs). Government
funds for the water supply component were disbursed through the MHSA,
provinces and counties to the villages affected.
Procurement       Procurement was the responsibility of the Office of Supply of the Republic
Methods           of Korea except for very small quantities required at the scattered work
sites. Some field vehicles and motorcycles were bought from local sources
rather than through ICB (more cost efficient).

Democratic        The Saemaeul influence was reflected in the substantial labor contribution
Decision Taking    provided by the villagers in the construction or improvement of the river
Process where     training and water supply sub-projects as well as the increasing
Stakeholders are   responsibility of local institutions and villagers for their operation and
Taken into        maintenance.
Account
Current Project   Loan of US$95 million was approved in March 1978 and became effective
Status            in June 1978. The closing date of this loan was June 1984.
Evaluation of the   (i) The project has achieved or exceeded its physical targets,
Project if        except for minor irrigation. Among the reasons for smooth
available         project implementation was the development strategy, based on
decentralized decision making, self-help from the villagers, and
willingness of the beneficiaries to contribute cash and labor,
both for construction, organizing and managing of project
work.
(ii) The project was not subject to many of the problems
usually associated with multi-component, rural development
projects. The project success may be partially attributed to the
fact that it represented a time slice of on-going development
programs and was implemented by experienced agencies. The
project consisted of a serious of on-going programs, strongly
supported by the government and the project beneficiaries, with
minimal disruption for each executing agency.
iii) A significant achievement of the project was the further
strengthening of the institution building process which has been
initiated under the Saemaeul program.
iv) Another major achievement of the project was a good
monitoring and evaluation system, which was introduced under
the first project and continued to operate under the second one.
A number of evaluation studies to measure the project impact
were carried out by independent research institutes.
